Small-Stock Focus:Broad Selloff Pushes Investors to Food Retailers
Small-Stock Focus: Broad Selloff Pushes Investors to Food Retailers
By Karen Talley
Dow Jones Newswires
939 words
7 May 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
C8
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
NEW YORK -- You know it's going to be an edgy day when investors flock to supermarket stocks like South
Floridians stocking up ahead of a hurricane. The group is rarely a market leader and the rush to snap up
shares reflected investors' uncertainty of where to turn as big-cap bellwethers broke down and small caps
followed, traders said.
Indeed, it was a day for broad selling -- aided by Moody's Investors Service saying investigations of
brokerage firms that lead to indictments could prompt debt downgrades, but that was hardly the chief reason,
since selling occurred across the board.
In a rare show of unity, small caps' declines kept pace with large caps.
The Russell 2000 Index of small-cap stocks fell 9.41, or 1.84%, to 502.91, its biggest percentage decline
since Feb. 21. The Standard & Poor's SmallCap 600 Index shed 4.61, or 1.80%, to 251.78.
The Nasdaq Composite Index shed 34.55 points, or 2.14%, to 1578.48, its lowest close since Oct. 9.
The Nasdaq started the day up, but that didn't last. Instead, as the session continued, traders counted with
one hand, then two, the number of bellwethers that were breaking below key price levels. The group included
International Business Machines and General Electric, they said.
"There were no safe havens," said Jay Indovino, head of trading at SunGard Institutional Brokerage Inc. in
New York. "Small caps were holding up, but even they succumbed. On days like these, all of a sudden your
0.7% money market return doesn't look so bad."
The session actually offered little in the way of fireworks that could rattle the market. There were no major
earnings announcements or economic news.
Those reports come today, when Cisco Systems reports and the Federal Reserve meets. The way things are
going, some strategists say there is a chance the Fed, after adopting a neutral bias at its last meeting, could
decide to readopt a bias that leans toward economic weakness. At the very least, it is very hard to find an
economist who believes the central bank will act to raise rates.
Food retailers and precious metals went back and forth as the two best-performing groups as investors
looked for some areas that might offer safety. Small-cap Wild Oats Markets got the food group fired up,
jumping 3.15, or 29%, to 13.95. Among other small caps, Great Atlantic & Pacific Tea added 69 cents, or
2.8%, to 25.25 and Fleming rose 45 cents, or 2%, to 22.45, both on the New York Stock Exchange.
Small-cap precious metals stocks had mixed results, with Meridian Gold gaining 42 cents, or 2.5%, to 17.02,
and Stillwater Mining dropping six cents to 17.18, both on the NYSE.
Selling predominated, with 79 of 86 industry groups that Dow Jones tracks losing ground.
The biggest decliners were oil drilling and equipment stocks, with Atwood Oceanics shedding 2.52, or 5%, to
47.80, Seacor Smit off 1.68, or 3.5%, to 46.72 and Grey Wolf down 49 cents, or 10%, to 4.50, all on the Big
Board.
Biotech got blasted again, a sector some predicted would be 2002's stars as investors sought alternatives to
technology and telecommunications. But that has been far from the case. This time, small caps Affymetrix
slipped 1.54, or 6%, to 23.41, Caliper Technologies dropped 68 cents, or 7%, to 9.01 and Curagen fell 79
cents, or 10%, to 7.53.

--- Page 6 ---
Page 6 of 243 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
IntraBiotics Pharmaceuticals was the Nasdaq's biggest percentage decliner, but not by much. Shares
plummeted 2.71, or 69%, to 1.19 after the Mountain View, Calif., antibacterial drug developer said Phase III
clinical trial data for its lead product iseganan were "disappointing," with no difference between the product
and placebo in primary or secondary end-points.
Peregrine Systems was close on IntraBiotics' heels, plunging 1.68, or 65%, to 89 cents. The software
developer said potential accounting irregularities had turned up as much as $100 million in
revenue-recognition irregularities and that its chief executive and chief financial officer had resigned. This is
the latest blow to a stock that started sliding last week when the San Diego concern, which retained KPMG to
replace Arthur Andersen as its auditor, said it would delay the release of fiscal fourth-quarter results.
Then there is Pemstar, skidding 4.29, or 60%, 2.84. The Rochester, Minn., engineering, manufacturing and
distribution firm lowered its fiscal fourth-quarter outlook, saying it expects a per-share loss ranging from 22
cents to 32 cents, in contrast with earnings of six cents a share estimated by analysts.
Priceline.com dropped an additional 33 cents, or 7.9%, to 3.85 after tumbling 1.16, or 22%, to 4.09 Friday.
The Norwalk, Conn., name-your-own-price Web site posted lower first-quarter revenue because of weak
air-ticket sales. Merrill Lynch downgraded the stock to "near-term neutral" from "buy."
Even superheroes couldn't do much good. Marvel Enterprises rose just four cents to 7.70 as the New York
publisher of comic books, including Spider-Man, received moderate benefit from the film's massive opening
when it took in a box-office record $114 million in three days.
PMR was the Nasdaq's best performer, surging 72 cents, or 30%, to 3.10. The San Diego manager of
outpatient psychiatric-treatment programs agreed to be acquired by closely held Psychiatric Solutions.

Financial, Oil and Health Stocks Lead the Pack
Small Stock Focus
Financial, Oil and Health Stocks Lead the Pack
By Karen Talley
Dow Jones Newswires
1,175 words
6 May 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
C8
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
NEW YORK -- With first-quarter earnings season about midway through, financial, oil-service and
health-maintenance stocks are the cream of the small-cap crop.
The groups are not only outpacing their large-cap peers, but they are also coming in with more upside
earnings surprises that pretty much guarantee a bump in share price.
Small-capitalization financial-service firms, especially banks, are averaging 12% in earnings growth,
compared with last year's first quarter, while larger financial institutions are averaging just 2% growth, said
Satya Pradhuman, chief small-cap strategist at Merrill Lynch.
That's because smaller banks are big mortgage lenders and profit more from lower interest rates and
stepped-up home loans. Among small-cap banks that the Merrill analyst likes is Texas Regional Bancshares.
In the energy sector, small caps are showing an average of 15% earnings growth while large caps are seeing
a 30% decline. Mr. Pradhuman credits oil-service stocks, many of which are small caps that are still seeing
demand for exploration services. Mr. Pradhuman is keeping an eye on FMC Technologies in this group.
Health-maintenance groups are doing well across the board, but small caps have the edge, Mr. Pradhuman
said, because they are less likely to experience blowups from failed acquisitions. Among smaller-cap
health-service concerns, Mr. Pradhuman likes LifePoint Hospitals.
But there are areas in which small caps lag behind big caps, with one of them technology, specifically
software and Internet services, where a lot of small caps dwell.
Small caps also fall behind in the transportation area, down 30% to big caps' 7% gain. The culprit:
once-mighty airlines that are now small-cap stocks. That happened Friday, when airlines were socked,
pushing Northwest Airlines' stock to small-cap size with its decline of 80 cents, or 4.4%, to $17.57; the stock
now carries a market cap below $1.5 billion, the criteria for inclusion in this column.
Large-cap companies overall are expected to post better profits than small caps in the first quarter. But after
that, small caps may flex their muscles. For the year, S&P SmallCap 600 earnings are expected to rise 20.6%
while S&P 500 profits are seen 14.5% ahead of their 2001 results, according to Thomson Financial/First Call.
In other words, "Don't let the first quarter fool you," said Gary Merwitz, small-cap strategist at Morgan Stanley.
He said small caps that should show the most improvement by the end of the year include Internet concern
HomeStore.com, going to a seven-cent per-share profit in the fourth quarter from a 73-cent loss a year
earlier. He also noted that aerospace-technology developer Orbital Sciences, is expected to post a
fourth-quarter profit of 17 cents a share, up from an 80-cent loss last year; and Elizabeth Arden, is expecting
net income of 13 cents a share, compared with a $1.13 loss in last year's fourth quarter.
---
Friday's Market Activity
It was a mixed day for small caps while large caps were uniformly down.
The S&P Small-Cap 600 Index rose 0.07, or 0.03%, part of a gain for the week of 5.82 points, or 2.3%, to
256.39. The index is knocking on the door of its record high, 257.81 set on April 16.
The Russell 2000 Index of small stocks fell 1.05, or 0.2%, Friday but finished the week up 10.82 points, or
2.2%, at 512.32. The Nasdaq Composite Index of large and small stocks was the big loser, falling 31.79

--- Page 8 ---
Page 8 of 243 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
points, or 1.93%, to 1613.03 Friday, leaving the index with an overall drop of 50.86 points, or 3.1%, for the
week.
Ameripath engaged in a game of musical chairs and emerged as the small-cap beneficiary of the merger
between Hewlett-Packard and Compaq Computer. Compaq's departure from the S&P 500-stock index left a
vacancy that First Tennessee, a member of the S&P Midcap 400 Index, was tapped to fill. That meant some
company had to replace First Tennessee on the S&P 400, and Constellation Brands was named, which
required shifting the company over from the S&P SmallCap 600. That left a vacancy on the S&P 600 to be
filled, and Standard & Poor's index selection committee chose Ameripath, a Riveria Beach, Fla., physicians
group that rose $2.21, or 8.1%, to $29.43 as a result of its new place at the table.
What a bungee ride for Tesoro Petroleum, the New York Stock Exchange's biggest percentage gainer on
Friday after being its biggest decliner the day before. The San Antonio petroleum-products provider rose
1.23, or 17%, to 8.48 after saying it is renegotiating the terms of its $1.125 billion purchase of the Golden
Eagle Refinery in Martinez, Calif., from Valero Energy. Tesoro plummeted 3.11, or 30%, to 7.25 on Thursday
after saying its wider-than-expected first-quarter loss raised questions about sufficient liquidity to complete
the purchase.
Aurora Foods was the NYSE's biggest percentage decliner, dropping 1.53, or 33%, to 3.08. The St. Louis
branded-food producer reported a first-quarter loss that widened from a year earlier, even when a
goodwill-impairment charge was excluded. During the first quarter, the firm took a per-share charge of $1.32
to write down the value of its trade names, in accordance with new accounting rules. Excluding that charge,
Aurora's loss was 19 cents a share, exceeding the 11-cent loss posted a year earlier.
Chip stocks were Friday's major laggards. Among small-cap decliners, Centillium Communications slid 63
cents, or 6.3%, to 9.36; Ess Technology fell 2.08, or 13%, to 14.02; and LTX lost 1.55, or 7.6%, to 18.75.
The Nasdaq's biggest percentage gainers included Generex Biotech, ahead 1.02, or 36%, to 3.88. The
Toronto drug- development company received Food and Drug Administration approval for an investigational
new drug application allowing human clinical trials of buccal morphine to treat pain.
Sapient was the Nasdaq's worst percentage decliner, off 1.80, or 39%, to 2.81. The Cambridge, Mass., online
business consultant posted a wider-than-expected first-quarter loss after having already lowered its
projections in February.
Precious metals brought out the buyers who wanted hard assets like gold behind their stock certificates.
Among small caps, Meridian Gold gained 95 cents, or 6.1%, to 16.50, and Stillwater Mining was ahead 64
cents, or 3.9%, to 17.24.
Nonferrous metals, or minerals excluding iron, were up as well, with Olin rising 67 cents, or 3.7%, to 18.63;
and RTI International Metals ahead 60 cents, or 4.7%, to 13.50.

Hike Rates Now
Hike Rates Now
By Brian Wesbury
884 words
6 May 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
A20
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
In my 20-year career as an economic forecaster, I cannot remember an economic recovery that was so
widely anticipated, and yet met with so much skepticism. Every piece of data, every rumor, every headline is
treated with the reverence usually given to important news. Consequently, the mood of the market is dour
and volatile.
The cynics use dozens of reasons to explain why this recovery will be wimpy and wobbly. But, and this is
important, most cynics have not yet come to grips with exactly what caused the recession in the first place.
As a result, there are no guideposts or bellwethers. This also creates uncertainty, moodiness and volatility.
Eventually, the fears will subside, as they always do. But if the Federal Reserve gets lost in the debate about
the strength of the recovery and decides to hold interest rates at current levels, it will be making a huge
mistake. While my supply-side colleagues may want to shoot me for saying this, the Fed needs to hike rates
and it needs to do so now.
One of the first mistakes analysts make in assessing our current economy is to doubt that a recession ever
occurred. While it is true that reported GDP declined in only one quarter last year, if we exclude imports
(which actually boost GDP when they fall) and government spending (which grew at the fastest rate since
1985 last year), the economy contracted in all four quarters of 2001. That's the only explanation for a record
drop in corporate profits and the loss of 1.8 million jobs.
The key cause of the recession was excessively tight Federal Reserve policy. The Fed raised rates in 1999
and 2000, increasing the federal funds rate to 6.5%, while inflation remained stable at near 2%. This pushed
the real federal funds rate to 4.5%, its highest level in 10 years. Instead of watching sensitive price indicators,
Fed Chairman Alan Greenspan was eyeing what he thought was "exuberant" growth.
Consequently, signs of tight money spread without notice. The yield curve inverted and the 10-year Treasury
bond, at 5.25% in December 2000, was 125 basis points below the federal funds rate. The dollar on foreign
exchange markets was strong, gold and commodity prices fell, and deflationary pressures became evident.
Tight money, and the deflation that it caused, became a major problem for the U.S. economy in 2000 and
2001.
We're in a different mess today. The same indicators that forewarned of recession 16 month ago now indicate
an excessively easy monetary policy. Gold prices, at $310 per ounce, are up 20% from year-ago levels.
The foreign exchange value of the dollar, measured against the currencies of our largest trading partners, has
fallen 4.2% in the past two months. Commodity prices are up and the 10-year Treasury bond yield is 5.1%,
335 basis points above the federal funds rate, the widest spread since 1993.
Not only will this "accommodative" stance in monetary policy create a very strong recovery in economic
growth this year, but 11 interest rate cuts have also ended the threat of deflation. Now, the real fear is that the
Fed will maintain its current policy stance for so long that inflationary pressures will begin to build.
The potential hurdles to economic growth this year have nothing to do with Fed policy. Enron and Arthur
Anderson, Eliot Spitzer and the SEC, steel and lumber tariffs and potential retaliation from our trading
partners, exploding government spending, the Middle East, terrorism and oil prices -- each has the potential
to slow investment and undermine confidence.
Using Fed policy to offset these exogenous forces would be a huge mistake. That is what the U.S. did in the
1970s, when the Fed kept rates too low in an attempt to keep an over-taxed, over-regulated economy
moving. The result was stagflation.
Monetary policy should be focused on price stability and should strive to be neutral at all times. It is just as
wrong to use monetary policy to slow down an economy that some Fed members think is overheating, as it
is to artificially stimulate an economy that is facing headwinds caused by non-monetary factors.

--- Page 10 ---
Page 10 of 243 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
For example, the unemployment rate jumped to 6% in April, in part because of the new 13-week extension in
unemployment benefits included in the most recent stimulus bill. For the Fed to hold off on tightening
because unemployment is too high would repeat the same mistake Mr. Greenspan made by tightening just
because unemployment was so low in 1999.
While it is easy to understand the Fed's reluctance to increase rates, the market is clearly sending signals
that monetary policy is too easy. The longer the Fed holds interest rates at the current level, the worse
problems with the misallocation of resources will become. Now is not the time to go soft on the battle against
inflation, nor is it time to accommodate fiscal policy errors. The Fed should stay true to its number one goal,
price stability, and hike rates as soon as possible.
---
Mr. Wesbury, chief economist at Griffin, Kubik, Stephens, & Thompson in Chicago, was the most accurate
economic forecaster in The Wall Street Journal's 2001 survey.

Commodities Report:Gold Futures Surge on BuyingRelated to Dollar, Stock Drops
Commodities Report: Gold Futures Surge on Buying Related to Dollar, Stock Drops
By Gavin Maguire OsterDowJones Commodity News
542 words
6 May 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
C13
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
NEW YORK -- Gold futures on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange rose to nearly
two-year highs Friday as declines in the U.S. dollar against other currencies coupled with stock-market jitters
attracted speculative buying.
The benchmark June contract rallied $3.90 to finish at $312.50 a troy ounce, after hitting a 22-month high of
$313.80 during the session.
Friday's decline in the dollar against other currencies rendered gold increasingly affordable to foreign
consumers, while concerns over U.S. corporate earnings coupled with disappointing unemployment data saw
investors buy gold as a safe-harbor investment opportunity.
Gold has also been benefiting increasingly in recent weeks from the heightening tensions in the Middle East
and the resulting apprehension that further instability in that region could derail the U.S. economy's nascent
recovery by sparking a surge in oil prices.
On the back of this "war premium," gold has not only attracted individual investors seeking to buy into an
asset seen as a hedge against inflation, but also large speculative funds looking to diversify their managed
portfolios and allocate funds away from the currently declining equity markets.
As a result, an increasing amount of money has entered the gold market so far this year, causing prices to
rise steadily by more than 10% since the turn of the year. At the same time, potential sellers of gold have
retreated to the sidelines to enhance profit-making possibilities.
Leonard Kaplan, president of Prospector Asset Management, said that as long as the dollar continues to slide
and stock markets remain unattractive to investors, gold will benefit. "We're in a bull market here, and we're
definitely going higher," he said. "If the dollar gathers momentum to the downside, gold will keep climbing at
a decent rate."
Mr. Kaplan also argued that, aside from the dollar-influenced investors, momentum-based investors will
become increasingly attracted to gold. "Most Western world or U.S. investors are momentum investors, not
value investors. They don't seem to care if something is comparatively cheap, they just want to know if it's
going higher," he said.
"Back when gold was $260-$270 per ounce, you couldn't give it away. Now, more and more people want to
ride this train, and I think that trend will also continue," he noted.
Over coming days, prices are expected to undergo a slight technical correction following Friday's sharp gains,
but any price dips are equally widely expected to be viewed as buying opportunities.

New Englanders Pull In
The Biggest Bucks in U.S.
New England residents make more money than people in any other region -- and new data show the gap is
widening.
The per-capita income of residents in the six states was $36,870 in 2001, according to the U.S. Bureau of
Economic Analysis. That's 21.8% higher than the U.S. average of $30,271. New England residents earned
21.4% more than the U.S. average a year earlier and 16.8% five years ago. It was the 17th straight year New
England has led all regions.
What puts the region on top: a tighter labor market, a higher percentage of women working -- boosting the
labor force's size -- and more high-wage, high-tech manufacturing jobs, says Jill Thompson, senior economist
with Boston-based FleetBoston Financial Corp.
After New England was the Mideast ($34,791), the Far West ($31,951), Great Lakes ($29,848), Great Plains
($29,106), Rocky Mountains ($28,499), Southwest ($27,280) and the Southeast ($23,350). While trailing,
Southeast per-capita income rose 3.1% over 2000, compared with a 2.7% increase for the nation.
Residents of small, energy-rich states New Mexico, Wyoming and Oklahoma saw per-capita personal income
grow 5.6%, 5.2% and 4.8%, respectively, the fastest rates in 2001. The slowest growth occurred in
Washington, Nevada and Oregon, where residents saw their incomes grow by 1.1%, 1.2% and 1.2%,
respectively. Washington and Oregon were hurt by declines in construction income. In Nevada, population
growth outstripped income growth.
-- Russell Gold
Western Homebodies
The hard-charging housing market is losing some steam in most of the nation, but new-home sales in the
West continue to chug along.
The West was the only region to see the sales of new single-family homes grow in March, rising 3.3% from
their February pace, the Commerce Department reported. In contrast, the Midwest saw new-home sales dive
19.3% from February, while the Northeast and South had respective declines of 4.4% and 0.2%. Nationally,
new-home sales dipped 3.1% in March.
Economists, noting the overall housing market remains strong despite the declines, say the West has
continued to post gains in large part because it is still trying to catch up with its 1990s population boom. And
Robert Rivinius, chief executive of the California Building Industry Association, a Sacramento lobbying group,
says the state's builders still have a long way to go, as a slow regulatory process contributes to an annual
production shortfall of 70,000 to 100,000 units. "Virtually anything that can be brought online is going to sell,"
says Mr. Rivinius.
-- Robert Gavin

The Gulf Coast petrochemical belt -- a concentration of refineries, petrochemical plants and energy
companies that runs from New Orleans to Corpus Christi, Texas -- pumped out more jobs than the nation and
its neighbors in the past year. In the 12 months that ended Feb. 28, the region added jobs at an annual 0.3%
pace, well ahead of the nation's drop of 0.9% and Texas' decline of 0.8%, according to the Federal Reserve
Bank of Dallas.

Commodities Report:Gold Reaches Two-Year HighsOn Dollar Weakness, Mideast
Commodities Report: Gold Reaches Two-Year Highs On Dollar Weakness, Mideast
By Gavin Maguire OsterDowJones Commodity News
448 words
29 April 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
C16
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Gold futures surged to two-year highs Friday as heightening tensions in the Mideast spurred buying, while
further weakness in the U.S. dollar enticed increased demand for physical gold.
The benchmark June contract on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange settled at
$312.10 a troy ounce, up $3.50 on the day and more than $9 above where prices opened the week.
Gold has been benefiting from apprehension that increased Mideast instability could derail the U.S.
economy's nascent recovery by sparking a surge in oil prices.
On the back of this "war premium," gold has attracted not only individual investors seeking to buy into an
asset seen as a hedge against inflation, but also large speculative funds looking to diversify their managed
portfolios and allocate funds away from the currently declining equity markets. Gold prices have risen 10%
since the start of the year.
Good levels of "hedge buybacks" by gold producers were noted in some recent quarters. This buying occurs
as mining companies withdraw their previously arranged forward-price commitments in expectation of being
able to renegotiate better prices later in the year.
Bullion dealers said technically based funds also were heavily involved through the day, banking on further
chart-based gains over the coming days and weeks.
They said the breach of the previous 2002 high around $310 on Friday afternoon brought into view upside
targets from 2000 such as the $314, $320 and $325 levels.
Charles Nedoss, at metals-trading firm PeakTrading.com in Chicago, said he was looking for $314 to be the
next big hurdle and that, beyond there, prices could quickly scurry to the $325 area.
"I like what we've been seeing [Friday and Thursday] -- higher highs and higher lows -- so I think we're in
good shape to test higher," he said. "I don't see too much between $314 and $325, so the rise from $314
could be pretty quick."
In other commodity markets Friday:
CATTLE: Chicago Mercantile Exchange live-cattle futures rose by their limit following gains Thursday. The
June contract Friday rose 1.5 cents to 62.77 cents a pound. What contributed to the surge was short
covering, or buying to negate previous sales; buying by some fund-connected accounts; and buy-stops, or
purchase orders placed in advance that were set off as the market rose.
HOGS: CME futures for hogs slid by their limit on continued concerns about soft cash prices after some weak
early cash bids. The May futures contract fell two cents to 47.72 cents a pound.

The Economy:Recovery of 2002 May Be Stalling---Early-Year Strong ShowingNow Appears to Have Been`Borrowed' From Spring
The Economy: Recovery of 2002 May Be Stalling --- Early-Year Strong Showing Now Appears to Have
Been `Borrowed' From Spring
By Russell Gold
669 words
26 April 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
The honeymoon appears to be over.
After a roaring start to the year, fresh data suggest the U.S. economic recovery is stalling. Sales of existing
homes dropped last month from their blistering pace of early 2002, and the government also reported
yesterday that upward pressure on wages and benefits was at its lowest point in three years. On Wednesday,
a Federal Reserve report showed that while the recovery is geographically broad, it is losing steam. A drop in
new durable-goods orders in March, the first fall this year, reinforces the theme.
It now appears likely that strong economic numbers early this year were boosted by "borrowing" from March
and April. "We are facing a payback for the exceptionally strong growth we saw in January and February,"
says Mark Vitner, senior economist at Wachovia Securities. He says this supports Federal Reserve Chairman
Alan Greenspan's message that the recovery will be slow-moving, and it increases the chance that interest
rates won't go up until at least late summer.
The National Association of Realtors reported yesterday that existing home sales dropped a seasonally
adjusted 8.3% in March to an annualized rate of 5.4 million units. A rush to lock in interest rates and mild
weather combined to make January and February the two strongest months on record, so the March drop-off
was somewhat inevitable, says David Lereah, the association's chief economist. The weak recovery should
keep interest rates and 30-year mortgage rates low, allowing the current pace to be sustained through the
late summer, he says.
Meanwhile, the Labor Department reported that its employment cost index, a measure of employee
compensation costs, rose 0.8% in January through March, following a gain of 1.0% in the previous three
months. This was the slowest quarterly reading since the first quarter of 1999.
Although the ECI data underscore the struggles of workers to get ahead as the overall economy emerges
from recession, they also contain potentially good news for the broad economy. The lack of upward
wage-and-salary pressure provides relief for corporations. In turn, this could make it more likely for executives
to begin authorizing more capital expenditure, which could boost the economy.
But workers, meanwhile, are still struggling. In a separate release, the Labor Department also reported that
first-time claims for unemployment insurance dropped by 31,000 to 421,000 in the week ended April 20.
While the drop appears large, economists believe it is the product of a falloff from an unusually high level in
the prior week as workers filed for extended benefits. Continuing unemployment insurance claims remain
high and the four-week moving average of first-time claims, a gauge that smooths out weekly fluctuations,
continued to inch upward to 452,500 from a revised 450,750.
What's more, finding a job for the unemployed is still a challenge. The Conference Board reported that its
help-wanted advertising index, which tracks newspaper job listings, fell to 46 in March from 47 in February.
The index hasn't budged much since November.
To be sure, while there is growing evidence the economic recovery will remain weak for the next couple of
months, there is little to support a thesis that the recovery will reverse course.
Indeed, the National Association of Business Economics said its quarterly survey of 136 corporate
economists found profit margins improved for the first time in seven quarters. Low cost pressures and
productivity gains helped boost profits and contributed to the largest quarterly jump in the profit index since
the end of the 1982 recession.
All told, the indications are that "we will be seeing more moderate economic growth, but there is nothing that
is so weak in the data that says we will relapse into a double-dip recession," says Maury Harris, chief U.S.
economist at UBS Warburg.

EUROPE
Germany's Kamps
Agrees to New Bid
By Italy's Barilla
Italian pasta maker Barilla sweetened its takeover offer for Kamps by half a euro to 12.50 euros a share,
valuing the German bakery chain at 1.8 billion euros ($1.6 billion), and ending Kamps's attempts to fend off its
suitor. According to the agreement reached by the companies, Kamps will remain intact and will be based
and run in Germany. The deal isn't expected to encounter antitrust objections. Kamps Chief Executive Heiner
Kamps asked shareholders to accept the new offer for all of Kamps and sell their holdings to Barilla.
Mr. Kamps said his decision was largely in deference to the interests of Kamps's institutional investors, which
hold the lion's share of Kamps stock. Barilla's offer is 21 times Kamps's pretax earnings for 2001.
BBVA CEO's Indictment Is Sought
Spain's anticorruption prosecutor has asked Judge Baltasar Garzon to indict, among others, the chief
executive of Banco Bilbao Vizcaya Argentaria, Jose Ignacio Goirigolzarri, and the country's deputy finance
minister, Estanislao Rodriguez Ponga, in connection with a criminal investigation into allegations of money
laundering and bribery by BBVA in Latin America. Judge Garzon this month took over an inquiry into a series
of secret offshore accounts that the former Banco Bilbao Vizcaya -- now merged into BBVA -- maintained
from 1987 to 2000. BBVA spokesmen said the charges are unfounded and false, and said the bank fully
supports Mr. Goirigolzarri. The operations in question were legal and well documented, they said. The
prosecutor asked the judge to indict 27 former and current BBVA directors and executives. Mr. Rodriguez
Ponga, a former tax adviser to BBVA, was cited because of his former links to the bank.
Infineon Results Top Expectations
German chip maker Infineon Technologies posted a fiscal second-quarter net loss of 108 million euros ($96.1
million), compared with profit of 23 million euros a year earlier. Economists polled by Dow Jones Newswires
had forecast a loss of 202 million euros for the quarter ended March 31. But Infineon maintained a cautious
outlook. Sales fell 16% from a year earlier to 1.39 billion euros.

oil field that is estimated to need about $2.5 billion to develop. A TotalFinaElf spokesman said that "nothing
has been signed yet" with Anglo-Siberian Oil PLC, which holds a 59% stake in the license rights to the
Vankor field in Russia. "We are still in negotiations," he said. Anglo-Siberian's management is expected to
seek approval of a deal at an extraordinary general meeting today. The company said it plans to conclude
talks with TotalFinaElf after the meeting.

Brazil Current-Account Gap Narrows
Brazil's deficit in the current-account, the broadest measure of trade, narrowed in March, the central bank
said. Aided by a buoyant trade surplus and less spending abroad by Brazilians, the current-account deficit for
the 12 months ended March 31 narrowed to $19.78 billion, equal to 3.88% of gross domestic product, from
$21.39 billion, or 4.22% of GDP, for the year ended Feb. 28. In March alone, the current-account gap
narrowed to $997 million from $2.61 billion a year earlier, helped by a $594 million trade surplus.
Barrick Gold Unveils Peru Discovery
Canada's Barrick Gold said a $6 million investment in a remote property high in the mountains of Peru
promises to be one of its most significant gold discoveries in the past decade. Barrick, the world's
second-largest gold producer, said the discovery at its Alto Chicama property in north-central Peru could
contain more than 3.5 million ounces of gold. Barrick has been exploring the property since early last year,
after it was the only bidder in a tender by Peruvian state mining company Centromin. The only financial
commitment Barrick had to make was to agree to spend $6 million over a three-year period and prepare a
feasibility study for mine development. It has already spent $3 million. It must pay an additional $2 million and
give Centromin a net smelter royalty of 2.5% over the life of the mine. The discovery is about 105 miles north
of Barrick's Pierina property, a low-cost producer with average annual production of 500,000 ounces of gold
at total cash costs of $90 an ounce.

TrizecHahn said its shareholders approved its plan to transform itself from a Canadian property company into
a real-estate investment trust based in New York. Once the reorganization is complete, the company's name
will change to Trizec Properties and its common stock will be listed on the New York Stock Exchange. In
Canada, it will be called Trizec Canada, and subordinated voting shares will be listed on the Toronto Stock
Exchange. The change must still be approved by the Ontario Superior Court.

States in the West Stage
Employment Comeback
Is the West's economy beginning to return to form?
After getting pounded in the last quarter of 2001, the nine-state region began 2002 significantly outpacing the
nation in job growth, according to a report by the San Francisco Federal Reserve Bank.
Employment in the West grew at an annualized rate of 2.1% in January while the nation saw employment
shrink at a rate of 1.7%. The region gave back some gains in February, with employment falling at an
annualized rate of 0.6% that month compared with 0.1% job growth in the rest of the nation. Still, says Mary
Daly, a senior economist at the San Francisco Fed, the West's economy "is stabilizing, and improving from its
fourth-quarter bottom," when the region was shedding jobs at a rate of more than 2% a year.
In particular, travel and tourism employment turned around sharply between December and February,
growing at an annualized rate of 2.8% after losing jobs at a rate of 10.6% a year in last quarter of 2001.
Hawaii and Nevada, the states most dependent on travel and tourism, posted back-to-back employment
gains. Hawaii's tourism industry added jobs at a annualized rate of 0.9% in February on top of a 9% increase
in January, while tourism jobs in Nevada grew 1.3% in February following 10.7% annualized growth in
January.
Hawaii in February reported the most visitors since before Sept. 11. The number of visitors to the islands --
509,000 -- was down 8.8% from the year before, but still represented marked improvement from September,
when visitors plunged 33.8% from the previous year to 357,000. Meantime, Las Vegas ended five
consecutive months of year-over-year declines in February, matching the 2.76 million people who visited in
February 2001.
-- Robert Gavin
Power Pacts
Hoping to satisfy government regulators' desire to resolve regional transmission bottlenecks, power providers
in the Southeast and Midwest are moving ahead with accords to cooperate on electricity transfers.
The federal Tennessee Valley Authority is expected to say today that it has signed informal pacts with
Midwest Independent Transmission System Operator Inc. of Carmel, Ind., Southern Co. of Atlanta and
Entergy Corp. of New Orleans to coordinate on transfers. Among other measures, the deals provide for
simplified transmission rates for moving power into and out of the regions, as well as a framework for
reserving use of high-voltage lines and managing congestion. Together, the four own or operate almost
150,000 miles of transmission lines.
The deals could help iron out bottlenecks between the Midwest and Southeast, which are especially bad
during hot periods when power generators try to ship voltage across the TVA's busy system. The government
utility, based in Knoxville, Tenn., is a linchpin in efforts to resolve logjams because the heart of its territory --
the 500-mile-long state of Tennessee -- sits at the crossroads of interstate power transfers.
Similar cooperation pacts are emerging in other parts of the country. They fall shy of the Federal Energy
Regulatory Commission's calls for utilities to turn over their entire transmission systems to regional
cooperatives, but regulators agree they are a step in the right direction.
-- Will Pinkston
Andersen Backlash

--- Page 21 ---
Page 21 of 243 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
States are beginning to follow the federal government's lead by ending their relationship with Arthur Andersen
LLP. Most states don't do much outsourcing for audits, but those that do are cutting their ties.

of the firm's work, but not its ability to survive. "You really have a problem if Andersen gets involved in an
audit and can't finish it," says Relmond Van Daniker, executive director of the National Association of State
Auditors, Comptrollers and Treasurers in Lexington, Ky. Mr. Van Daniker says he doesn't know how many
states have contracts with the firm.
Arthur Andersen spokesman Patrick Dorton declines to says how many states use the firm's auditing
services. "We are proceeding with confidence in building the audit firm of the future," he says, a future that he
says he hopes includes state government work.

Stock Recovery May Take Time---History Shows That ReboundsFollowing Speculative BubblesCan Be Slow to Materialize
Abreast of the Market
Stock Recovery May Take Time --- History Shows That Rebounds Following Speculative Bubbles Can
Be Slow to Materialize
By E.S. Browning
1,849 words
15 April 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
HAVE THE FROTH and excess finally been squeezed out of the stock market?
More than two years after stocks began their painful declines, a look back at previous market bubbles
provides a hint as to where shares are headed next.
What history suggests is that, following bubble periods, stocks take time to recover -- more time than has
passed so far. By that standard, the broad Standard & Poor's 500-stock index or the Dow Jones Industrial
Average may be closer to a lasting recovery. But the tech-dominated Nasdaq may still face a rocky road.
"The way I look at markets is that things generally tend to be symmetrical," says Kevin McClintock, chief
investment officer for stocks at money-management firm David L. Babson in Cambridge, Mass. "If you have
had a runup that has taken a couple of years, it doesn't tend to correct itself in six months."
The Nasdaq Composite Index, for instance, looks surprisingly like the U.S. stock market around the 1929
crash, or the Japanese stock market during its runup, according to studies by Ned Davis Research in Venice,
Fla. It also has behaved similarly to mining stocks during the gold bubble of the late 1970s, while the S&P
500 looks a little like it did in the early 1970s, as the "Nifty 50" bubble was popping.
No one, of course, is forecasting a period like the 1930s, or even a near-depression such as the one Japan
has experienced. The look back at history also does nothing to reflect the classic way that most experts
evaluate stocks, which is to compare stock prices to companies' earnings.
Last week, amid growing concerns that corporate earnings won't be strong enough to pull stock values
higher, stocks continued their four-week slump. The Nasdaq composite fell 0.8% for the week, and the S&P
500 fell 1%. The Dow Jones Industrial Average fell 0.8% to 10190.82, despite a rise of 0.15%, or 14.74
points, Friday.
The Nasdaq Composite didn't exist in the 1920s. Electricity and the radio were the networking technology and
the Internet of their day, and the Dow Jones Industrial Average was the speculative index.
From April 1921 through the market peak in September 1929, before the October crash, the Dow Jones
industrials rose 497%. The Nasdaq Composite, by contrast, rose more than 1,300% from October 1990
through March 2000. Because the industrials were generating more dividends than Nasdaq stocks do, that
comparison understates the full gains of the 1920s. Still, the Nasdaq bubble was huge, and it is little wonder
that the Nasdaq still is struggling.
"It is going to take some time to get the Nasdaq valuations back to reasonable levels," notes Tim Hayes, Ned
Davis's global stock strategist.
Following the 1929 crash, the Dow Jones industrials fell 89% through July 1932. The Nasdaq, at its low, was
down 72%.
Does that mean the Nasdaq has further to fall? Not necessarily. The industrial average's troubles were
worsened by inept economic and monetary policy in the 1930s. If today's economic managers prove better
-- and if the terrorist threat doesn't worsen -- the Nasdaq could fare better. Mr. Hayes's best guess: "They
don't have to go down any farther than they are," but the index could have trouble making significant progress
anytime soon.
If the Nasdaq of the 1920s was the Dow Jones industrials, the Nasdaq of the 1980s was Tokyo's Nikkei Stock
Average. Amid a spasm of real-estate and stock-market speculation, the Nikkei rose 468% during its bubble,

--- Page 23 ---
Page 23 of 243 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
which ended at the close of 1989, just before the U.S. bull market got under way. Since then, the Nikkei has
collapsed, losing three-quarters of its value.
If the Nasdaq were to behave like the Nikkei, that would mean that it has a long and grueling period of further
decline ahead of it; the Nikkei continued to fall from the start of 1990 through this year, a period of more than
a decade.
Here, again, however, the outlook may not be that grim. The Japanese government's effort to prop up
companies and banks threw the country into a series of recessions that some economists have characterized
as a near-depression. While few analysts think the Nasdaq decline needs to last as long as the Nik-kei's, the
Japanese experience doesn't help the case for a quick Nasdaq rebound.
In the late 1970s, with inflation soaring and the economy uncertain, investors became fixated on one of the
oldest speculative investments: gold.
As the Hunt family tried to corner the silver market, gold prices surged. "The mania that occurred in the gold
market sent it up to $800 an ounce. It was one of those rare, 1929-style events," notes Mr. Hayes of Ned
Davis.
The rise and fall of gold-mining stocks during that period looks surprisingly like that of the Nasdaq. The
experience with gold offers a measure of hope, but also some concern.
The good news is that the gold-mining stocks bottomed out at a point in their cycle similar to the recent
Nasdaq bottom, which suggests that maybe the Nasdaq index can escape any further decline. The bad news
is that gold-mining stocks never returned to their high of 1980.
Of course, a bar of gold is a lot different than a computer chip. "The Nasdaq composite might not have to
wait as long as the gold-mining stocks did for a new high, but there still is a strong case to be made that it
could be a long time before we go back to new highs" on the Nasdaq, Mr. Hayes says.
Something very similar to the bubble of the 1990s occurred in the early 1970s, with what was known as the
Nifty 50 stocks, as investors shifted more and more of their money to a small group of solid, powerful
companies such as American Express, Avon and Polaroid -- the Ciscos, Oracles and Microsofts of their day.
Those stocks soared, and they became known as "one-decision stocks." All you had to do was buy and hold;
they would be good forever.
Their unraveling in 1972 and 1973 brought down the S&P 500 and led to one of the most frustrating periods
for stocks since the 1930s. Although stock indexes alternately soared and plunged during the rest of the
1970s, they didn't make any lasting progress until 1982.
There is every reason to think the S&P 500 will do better now than in the 1970s. This time, there are worries
about stock valuations, earnings and terrorism. Back then, on top of valuation and earnings issues, there was
terrible inflation, anguish over the Vietnam War and disaffection with the White House, the military and
American society. Things are better today.
Stock strategist Tobias Levkovich of Salomon Smith Barney believes investors still have inflated hopes for
tech stocks. He notes that, while tech companies make up 3% of employment, 4% of the nation's total output
and 7% of industrial production, they represent more than half of stock-trading volume.
As has been the case with other fallen darlings after their bubbles, tech stocks could be facing a series of
lurching ups and downs, as hopes spring up and are dashed. The roller coaster could last until excessive
expectations for tech stocks finally get washed out.
"And that could mean that some of the shakeout of the tech bubble isn't done," Mr. Levkovich says.

As CEOs' reported salaries and bonuses get pinched, many chiefs arefinding hidden ways to increase their compensation
Under the Radar
Executive Pay (A Special Report)
As CEOs' reported salaries and bonuses get pinched, many chiefs are finding hidden ways to
increase their compensation
By Joann S. Lublin
3,110 words
11 April 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
B7
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
IT'S HARD TO FEEL SORRY for Terrence Murray.
Like many business leaders, the head of FleetBoston Financial Corp. saw his bulging wallet shrink last year.
The bank gave him no raise and halved his bonus.
But life becomes much sweeter once the 62-year-old chairman retires at year end. Under an unusually
generous formula FleetBoston directors embraced in August, Mr. Murray (who recently relinquished the chief
executive post) will collect a $5.8 million annual pension. That's more than twice the estimated $2.7 million a
year that a prior formula would have paid. Neither figure appears in any FleetBoston regulatory filing.
In the aftermath of the recession, the bearish stock market and Enron Corp.'s collapse, corporate boards face
unparalleled pressure to pinch their top executives' pay. "It's the end of a golden era," says Pearl Meyer, a
veteran New York compensation adviser.
Sort of. Because while their more-public pay numbers are shrinking, certain corporate captains have
discovered less obvious ways to enrich themselves. This so-called stealth wealth remains obscured because
executives reap rewards after they depart, federal disclosure requirements are weak -- or both, as in the case
of Mr. Murray. Besides postretirement windfalls, such tactics include stock sales to an employer, lucrative
deferred-compensation plans and low-interest loans.
"Chief executives are finding well-concealed ways to restore compensation that the downturn denied them,"
says Sarah Teslik, executive director of the Council of Institutional Investors, a Washington group that
represents 120 pension funds with more than $2 trillion in assets. "Disclosure laws aren't adequate for us to
track [this]."
The result: People steering the largest enterprises are sharing some -- but not all -- of the nation's economic
pain.
Chief executive officers with at least two years' tenure incurred a 2.8% median decrease in salary and
bonuses last year to $1,603,125, concludes a study of the latest proxy statements of 350 major U.S.
companies by New York-based Mercer Human Resource Consulting. The companies' profit plunged a
median of 17.8% in 2001, according to Mercer's analysis. Standard & Poor's 500-stock index, meanwhile, fell
13%, its biggest one-year decrease since 1974.
The drop in CEOs' cash compensation was the first since The Wall Street Journal began tracking their
fortunes in 1989. It also was a stark reversal from the 10% median gain to $1,844,429 in 2000. (The Mercer
survey's 2000 figure reflects a slightly different sample of 350 companies.)
Despite broad cuts in pay and jobs, white-collar staffers fared better than their companies' highest bosses --
for the first time since 1991. Paychecks of nonunion salaried employees rose 4.4%, after advancing 4.2% in
2000. Excluding bonuses, which these workers usually don't collect, eliminates the gap. Base pay of business
leaders climbed 4.7% in 2001, following a 5.3% increase the prior year. Overall U.S. wages and benefits rose
4.2% last year, below the 4.6% growth in 2000.
A number of corporate commanders took home smaller bonuses, a payment often tied to profits. In the
Mercer study, 186 received reduced or zero bonuses, a dramatic increase from 122 in 2000. The chiefs of
Boeing Co., Eli Lilly & Co., Merrill Lynch & Co. and McGraw-Hill Cos. were among those whose bonuses
were slashed in 2001. Bonuses of studied CEOs tumbled 13.5% to a median of $697,657 last year,
compared with a 12% increase to $1 million during 2000.

--- Page 26 ---
Page 26 of 243 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Some bosses took their shrunken compensation in stride. An omitted bonus, a 10% salary cut and largely
worthless stock options simply "make me work harder," says Ned Barnholt, chief executive of Agilent
Technologies Inc., a Palo Alto, Calif., producer of testing and measurement equipment. "I don't focus on the
money aspect as much as I focus on navigating through difficult times."
Total direct compensation for polled CEOs fell 0.9% last year to a median of $2,959,361, again the first
downturn since the Journal starting keeping track. The latest figure compares with an 8.2% rise to $2,902,886
in 2000. (In addition to salaries and bonuses, total direct compensation includes the value of restricted stock
at the time of the grant, gains from exercising options, and other long-term incentive payouts.)
Performance counts here too, though, and not just profits. The chief executives of companies with the best
shareholder returns were more handsomely paid than their counterparts at companies whose investors fared
the worst. Heads of the 10 best-performing businesses experienced an 8.6% increase in total direct
compensation to a median of $2,112,842, Mercer reported. The leaders of the 10 with the poorest returns
saw their remuneration sag 52.3% to a median of $879,167. The median total shareholder return, which
equals stock-price appreciation plus reinvested dividends, was 3.6% last year among the 350 companies
studied.
The protracted market slump restrained CEOs' rewards from options. The median realized gain from option
exercises totaled $2,057,580, up from the prior year's $1,892,938. There were glaring exceptions. Oracle
Corp.'s Lawrence J. Ellison scored a $706.1 million paper profit when he exercised options during the fiscal
year ended May 31. The co-founder and biggest shareholder of the Redwood Shores, Calif., software
concern said he did so because those options were about to expire.
Altogether, 148 of the surveyed chiefs cashed in a median of 102,601 options apiece, compared with the 151
exercising a median of 98,333 options each in 2000. Many hoping for rosier days ahead can take comfort
from a big remaining stash. Mr. Ellison, for instance, still held 47.9 million exercisable options, valued at about
$497.6 million, at the end of fiscal 2001.
Median unrealized gains from options amounted to $4.96 million in 2001, down from 2000's $5.84 million, the
Mercer analysis showed. Overall, unrealized option gains for polled executives sank to $7.6 billion from
$15.48 billion.
L. Dennis Kozlowski, the 55-year-old leader of Tyco International Ltd., didn't exercise any options in the
Bermuda-based conglomerate's fiscal year ended Sept. 30, because his nearly 10.15 million exercisable
options were then worth a scant $6,667. Tyco's stock hasn't been entirely cooperative since then, either. It
climbed 32% from $45.50 on Sept. 28, the last trading day of the fiscal year, to its current 52-week high of
$60.09 on Dec. 5. But it has since dropped about 47% to around $32 recently in New York Stock Exchange
composite trading.
On the other hand, Tyco gave its chairman and chief executive restricted shares valued at $30.4 million and
1.4 million options initially valued at $21.8 million during fiscal 2001. (Mr. Kozlowski could lose his restricted
shares if the business misses tough earnings goals. His new options replaced exercised ones.)
Last year, leaders of 111 businesses tracked by Mercer got such so-called megagrants, up from 85 in 2000.
An options megagrant has a face value of at least eight times an individual's salary and bonus. The face
value is computed by multiplying the number of options by the market price at the time of the grant. A
restricted-stock megagrant has a face value of at least two times salary and bonus. Restricted shares
typically cost the executive little or nothing; limits on selling the stock usually disappear within five years.
In addition to his megagrants, Mr. Kozlowski made more than $70 million selling about 1.2 million shares
back to Tyco during fiscal 2001. The three sales occurred while he continued to state publicly that he rarely if
ever sold his Tyco shares.
And even though he shed most of that stake in October 2000, Tyco didn't disclose the transactions until a
Nov. 13, 2001, filing. The Securities and Exchange Commission allows companies to disclose such insider
transactions just once a year, with the filing deadline set 45 days after the fiscal year ends.
Mr. Kozlowski says he routinely sells shares to Tyco to repay loans taken to cover his tax liability on
restricted-share grants. "Every year, we turn back stock because we have to," he says, calling scrutiny of his
share sales a case of "Enronitis." Stung by criticism that the permitted reporting delay concealed sizable
compensation, he vows that in the future, "I will not give back one share of stock unless it's preannounced."
SEC rules also explain why no one knew until February of this year that former Enron Chairman Kenneth Lay
had sold $70.1 million of stock back to the Houston energy trader between February and October 2001. He
did so to repay borrowings from Enron. During much of that period, Enron's share price kept sinking and Mr.
Lay was exhorting employees to keep buying shares. Enron's December bankruptcy filing wiped out much of
the retirement savings for most workers there.

--- Page 27 ---
Page 27 of 243 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
"CEOs are getting far more skilled at hiding this [pay] stuff," says William Patterson, director of the AFL-CIO's
Office of Investment. "There is far more anger because of the surreptitious disclosure." He is especially
enraged over Mr. Murray's FleetBoston retirement package.
The Boston bank changed a pension formula previously based on roughly 60% of its CEO's last five years'
annual salary and bonus, according to an SEC filing. Instead, the board will base Mr. Murray's pension on the
average of his highest three years of taxable compensation from 1996 to 2000. The revised formula covers
salary, bonus, option-exercise gains, vested restricted shares and the payout of certain deferred
compensation.
"I cannot think of another major company that counts stock-option gains in determining a CEO's pension,"
says Brian Foley, an executive-compensation consultant in White Plains, N.Y., who calculated the annual
$2.7 million due Mr. Murray under the old method. Bank officials computed the new formula's price tag of $5.8
million a year in response to a Wall Street Journal query. "This is an extraordinary pension in terms of sheer
size and how it was calculated," Mr. Foley says.
Mr. Murray declines to comment, according to Fleet spokesman Jim Mahoney. The bank's latest proxy
statement says the richer pension formula partly reflects the fact that "Mr. Murray built the corporation into the
seventh-largest financial holding company and is a long-service and high-performing chief executive."
FleetBoston's market capitalization soared to about $40 billion by the end of 2001 from $200 million when Mr.
Murray took the helm in 1982, says Mr. Mahoney. Directors, he adds, felt "that's an enormous addition to
shareholder value," which took precedence over the bank's earnings slide last year.
Others disagree. FleetBoston shares "have underperformed the average bank for a decade," says Michael
Mayo, a Prudential Securities banking analyst. "What happened to [just] getting a gold watch?" Mr. Mayo is
equally miffed over what he considers the bank's insufficient disclosure of the revamped pension's cost. "The
owners of the company have a right to know what managers are getting paid, especially after they retire," he
says.
The new pension formula, Mr. Mahoney replies, "is laid out pretty clearly in the proxy."
Once Mr. Murray leaves, another regulatory filing states, he also will enjoy lifetime use of a corporate aircraft
for as much as 150 hours a year, a car and driver when requested, an office and assistant, a company-paid
home security system and financial planning. His wife and guests can take free flights even if he stays behind
-- an extremely rare perk.
Boards increasingly let a retiring leader use a corporate jet for life, because fuel usually is the only extra cost.
The permanent perk "is an efficient way of delivering something of value to the executive," says Yale D.
Tauber, a Mercer principal in New York.
Exactly how valuable? FleetBoston's filings don't say. If the Murrays and a friend take 15 coast-to-coast trips
every year on a typical corporate jet, the former chief executive will have to pay taxes on about $180,000 of
imputed income, estimates Mary B. Hevner, a compensation specialist and partner at Washington law firm
Baker & McKenzie.
General Electric Co. spurred jet envy elsewhere by negotiating a postretirement consulting accord with
longtime leader John F. Welch Jr. that gives him lifetime access to company aircraft in exchange for working
up to 30 days a year. He stepped down in September. His personal use of the aircraft was "minimal" in 2001,
a spokesman says.
GE also gives Mr. Welch several other perks whose value isn't readily apparent, on top of a pension that Mr.
Foley estimates at nearly $9 million a year. (GE doesn't disclose the exact figure.)
A special life-insurance policy GE set up in 2000 will pass benefits to Mr. Welch's heirs free of estate and
income taxes. In what is known as a split-dollar policy, businesses pay the vast majority of premiums for an
arrangement typically used to shelter a senior executive's pension. "It's like an interest-free loan," says David
M. Leach, head of compensation consulting for Buck Consultants in New York.
GE also allowed Mr. Welch to contribute as much as 90% of his $3.4 million salary last year to a
deferred-compensation account and guaranteed him a 12% annual return. The company has paid him
interest ranging from 10% to 14% a year on money deferred since 1987. The spokesman declines to divulge
how much any GE staffer defers.
Mr. Welch can withdraw this nest egg over 20 years; the balance will keep earning the same returns. GE
investors have no idea "how much Jack Welch will continue to tap into the coffers of the corporation," says
Judith Fischer, managing director of consultants Executive Compensation Advisory Services in Alexandria,
Va.

--- Page 28 ---
Page 28 of 243 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Mr. Welch was unavailable to comment for this article.
A guaranteed high interest rate for a CEO's deferred compensation "is the scam of the century," says Patrick
McGurn, director of corporate programs for Institutional Shareholder Services, a proxy-advisory firm in
Rockville, Md.
Some critics say the same about the stealth wealth that WorldCom Inc. provides in the form of bargain
interest rates on loans to Bernard J. Ebbers. The Clinton, Miss., telecommunications company, which he
founded and runs, charges him 2.15% annually on a $198.7 million loan, and between 2.14% and 2.16% a
year on a separate loan of about $144.3 million, a March 13 regulatory filing shows.
WorldCom has said those are its own borrowing rates. The $343 million is the most any publicly held
corporation has lent to a single officer in recent memory. The low rates significantly sweeten the deal. Mr.
Foley figures Mr. Ebbers saves about $15.6 million a year, based on the difference between the debt's
average interest rate of 2.15% and a recent benchmark corporate bond rate of 6.69%.
WorldCom says the loans benefit shareholders, who would suffer if Mr. Ebbers were compelled to sell
millions of shares to raise collateral. The SEC is investigating the Ebbers loans as part of a broader inquiry
into the company's finances. Mr. Ebbers declines to comment, according to a WorldCom spokeswoman.
Charging little for massive loans suggests "you have overcompensated executives, and boards that lack
backbone to admit it," says Ms. Teslik of the Council of Institutional Investors. "They are just trying to hide
compensation."
Outrage over stealth wealth is swelling. The council has asked the SEC to require more proxy information
about repricing of worthless options. TIAA-CREF, a leading pension system for U.S. colleges and
universities, wants the commission to mandate greater disclosure of the value of executive pensions and
deferred compensation. The SEC has yet to act.
And last month, Rep. George Miller, a California Democrat, introduced a bill that would require executives to
inform their corporate pension plans' administrators of insider stock sales within 24 hours. Plan officials would
then have to immediately notify employees. The measure also covers shares sold back to a business. The
House of Representatives may consider his proposal today.
In response to such outcry, two big companies have decided to disclose top executives' options awards
quarterly rather than annually, says Frank Glassner, CEO of New York consultants Compensation Design
Group. He declines to identify those clients or three others considering the same move. Their voluntary
disclosures will "put executive pay further out in the open," he says, sending investors a message "that not
everyone is a bad guy."

As Investors Seek Safer Havens,Hard Assets Gain in Popularity
As Investors Seek Safer Havens, Hard Assets Gain in Popularity
By Jane J. Kim and Kathy Chu
Dow Jones Newswires
601 words
10 April 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
D2
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
NEW YORK -- It looks as if investors may have a soft spot for hard assets.
Recent evidence suggests that investors may be shifting money into so-called hard assets, possibly at the
expense of more-liquid securities, such as stocks. Hard assets typically include real estate, precious metals,
and commodities such as oil, gas and timber. Other hard assets include collectibles such as art and gold
coins.
"It's a return to a perceived safer haven," said David Kelly, an economic adviser at Putnam Investments in
Boston. Today's baby boomers are building one of the biggest financial bridges between working life and
retirement and are looking for a place to put their accumulated wealth, he added.
Take real estate, for example, which continued its robust pace as sales of new and existing homes logged
another month of strong sales. The Commerce Department said February sales of new U.S. homes rose
5.3% from January. And even the more-speculative hard-asset classes such as art and gold coins are
experiencing a mini-renaissance.
Psychology and economics appear to be driving the shift. Spooked by jittery financial markets and burned by
dot-com blowups and corporate accounting debacles, investors are returning to tangible assets that could be
poised for stronger returns.
"People have swung to the other extreme of the pendulum," said Ron Florance, senior vice president and
director of asset allocation at Bank of America's Private Bank. "If you own real estate, you can see the
building, you can stand on the dirt," he said.
And as the economy emerges from a recession, investors could be anticipating a return to inflation. That fear
may be prompting people to move their money into assets that typically benefit from rising prices, experts
said.
Investors' portfolio mixes shift as the prices of those assets change. For example, as real-estate values rise
and stock prices fall, real estate will make up a larger percentage of an investor's portfolio. "In the last 18
months, asset allocation has become much more important," Mr. Florance said. "Driving it is the collapse of
large-cap growth stocks."
Real estate is one area that investors are using to diversify their portfolios. Not only does it act as an inflation
hedge, but it also gives investors a chance to profit from price appreciation and tax advantages. And a
low-interest-rate environment is making it cheaper to buy. Indeed, leverage is one reason that real estate can
generate higher returns than stocks, said Barbara Raasch, partner in charge of wealth management at Ernst
& Young's New York office. For one, investors can borrow more money to buy real estate (in some cases as
high as 90% of a property's value) than they can to buy stocks (typically limited to 50%). So the potential
returns -- and risks -- will be higher.
Besides real estate, the gold and precious-metals sectors have also run up, spurred by low interest rates
and speculative buying. Japanese investors have also been shifting money out of savings accounts into gold
as their government prepares to end blanket deposit insurance. The price of gold recently rose above $300
an ounce, its highest level in nearly two years.
As investments, gold and precious metals typically act as inflation hedges and are good ways to diversify
one's portfolio since there is slim performance correlation with the financial markets. However, experts noted
the sector is volatile and current performance should be viewed with caution.

Quarterly Mutual Funds Review---An Up Quarter For funds, Little Uplift for Investors
Quarterly Mutual Funds Review --- An Up Quarter For funds, Little Uplift for Investors
By James A. White
290 words
8 April 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
R6
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Stock mutual funds ended the first quarter in the plus column, but who could blame investors for feeling a little
underwhelmed by the three-month performance of their portfolios.
Diversified U.S.-stock funds eked out a 0.36% gain on average, according to Lipper Inc. But the fund-tracking
firm noted that there was less positive there than meets the eye when investors' assets are factored into the
equation.
For example, investors still have more money in growth portfolios than value funds these days, and it was a
down quarter for most growth funds. And tech funds, the most popular category of sector portfolios, also
slipped, by an average 7.61%.
On the flip side, the quarter's best-performing funds on average were those in the gold category, soaring
35.2%. But the often-volatile gold funds attract relatively few assets, meaning only a relative handful of
investors felt like they had benefited.
Also doing well in the quarter were emerging-markets funds, which rose 12.24% as developing economies in
Latin America, Asia, as well as Russia, achieved stock gains.
Indeed, only 59% of stock funds posted gains in their largest share class during the first quarter, according to
Lipper, compared with 98% that advanced in the 2001 fourth quarter.
Bond funds had to contend with rising U.S. interest rates and an improving economy-both factors that damp
bond prices. There were uncertainties in the corporate sector sparked by the widening fallout from the Enron
scandal. Still, the average taxable U.S. bond fund edged up 0.15%, compared with a modest 0.93% in the
2001 fourth quarter.
