Couch Tater Tots:In Battle for Toddlers,TV Networks ToutEducational Benefits---Nickelodeon and Disney TalkOf `Multiple Intelligences'To Win...
Couch Tater Tots: In Battle for Toddlers, TV Networks Tout Educational Benefits --- Nickelodeon and
Disney Talk Of `Multiple Intelligences' To Win Over Parents --- Lesson in the `Noodle Dance'
By Sally Beatty
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
2,156 words
1 April 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Every week, millions of tiny television viewers tune into Nickelodeon's hit "Dora the Explorer" to watch as a
peppy cartoon character rallies them to sing and stomp their feet to her signature song, "We did it!"
Little do the preschoolers know that they are exercising what a Nickelodeon executive calls their
"bodily-kinesthetic intelligence." When Dora consults her map, she is promoting "spatial" skills. And when
Dora asks her young viewers to help her count planks to build a bridge, she is building "interpersonal
intelligence."
Nickelodeon, a unit of Viacom Inc., borrowed these concepts from Harvard University Professor Howard
Gardner, the father of "multiple-intelligence theory." First advanced in 1983, the theory holds that there are
other types of intelligence, such as athletic prowess or musical ability, beyond the traditional math and verbal
skills enshrined in standardized tests. Many teachers, particularly those trying to reach problem students,
have since embraced the notion.
Now it's television's turn. In a bruising battle for the preschool audience, which logs more hours in front of the
tube than any other group of kids, media giants Viacom and Walt Disney Co. are furiously invoking
multiple-intelligence theory. By wrapping themselves in pedagogical virtue, the executives hope to have a
better chance of winning over the parents who control the TV dial for the under-six set.
Disney is launching a new channel, called Playhouse Disney, built around what it calls the "whole-child
curriculum" that promotes emotional, social and cognitive development. Prof. Gardner's theories are "one of
the founding principles" of the channel's basic learning plan, says Anne Sweeney, president of Disney's ABC
Cable Networks Group.
It's a precarious marketing pitch. Some child-development experts say flatly that watching TV isn't good for
young children. The American Academy of Pediatrics says television gets in the way of healthy brain
development and recommends that kids under two not watch TV at all.
Dr. Gardner says he has never watched Dora and doesn't have an opinion about the show. But even he says
"one has to retain a healthy skepticism when people say things like bodily-kinesthetic intelligence or spatial
intelligence or interpersonal intelligence can really be strongly realized by television as we know it." The
professor, who removed the TV set from his own house during his youngest child's first 10 years, adds: "A
week in the real world is worth 10 years watching television."
Brown Johnson, head of Nickelodeon's preschool programming, says it's unrealistic to expect that young
children won't be watching television, "so you might as well put stuff on that's really, really good." She adds,
"It's the 21st century. Media is in our lives."
Not long ago, commercial networks were content to cede the preschool market to public-television programs
such as "Sesame Street" and "Mister Rogers' Neighborhood." High-minded and commercial-free, the Public
Broadcasting System's shows had a vaguely academic aura that relieved parental guilt pangs. Perhaps more
to the point, advertisers weren't interested, since very small children don't tend to make purchasing decisions.
But now, preschoolers are hot. Even without much advertising, TV executives have learned to use their
programming to churn out a profitable stream of licensed products. Disney estimates that annual U.S. sales
of preschool products that could be branded, including everything from videos to one-piece pajamas, top $21
billion.
What's more, since media habits start early, hooking preschoolers can translate later into higher ratings
among older kids -- not just on cable, but on broadcast networks as well. "Dora" already airs not only on

--- Page 33 ---
Page 33 of 243 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
weekdays on Nickelodeon, but also Saturday mornings on Viacom's CBS network. Similarly, Disney could
rerun some of its preschool programming on ABC.
Nickelodeon, which last year captured 16% of the audience of 6- to 11-year-olds watching television during a
24-hour period -- against Disney's 9% -- has also been aggressive in pursuing preschoolers. One early hit,
"Blue's Clues," about a dog that barks out clues to questions from a TV host named Steve, has spawned a
merchandise empire ranging from sneakers to macaroni and cheese. Annual sales have topped $1 billion for
several years now. And "Dora the Explorer" has become the top-rated regularly scheduled show for
preschoolers on commercial television, reaching a total audience of 3.2 million preschool viewers in the first
week of November. (The most widely watched preschool show on PBS in that period was "Dragon Tales."
According to PBS, the show reached a total of 4.3 million viewers.)
Back in 1988, when Ms. Johnson was brought in as a production executive, preschool programming was a
relative backwater of European imports and other fare. Executives called it the "electronic sandbox," but the
shows were mostly a "place holder because we knew older kids were at school," Ms. Johnson says. Still,
executives resolved to boost viewership, because low preschool ratings were dragging down Nickelodeon's
daily ratings averages, a critical statistic the network uses to sell advertising time throughout its schedule.
It's not that the viewers weren't out there. Nielsen research suggests preschoolers watch an average of more
than 23 hours of television a week. But Nickelodeon knew that modern moms weren't about to deposit their
precious tots in front of just any show. And Nickelodeon would need to tread carefully if it wanted to compete
successfully with PBS.
The task was complicated by Nickelodeon's past success. Nickelodeon, after all, became a powerhouse in
kids television by creating a this-is-for-kids environment that thumbed its nose at anything that seemed as if it
was being spoon-fed by parents. Signature programs such as "The Ren & Stimpy Show," about a
smart-aleck cat and dog, were viewed by some parents as slightly "dangerous," recalls Ms. Johnson.
So Nickelodeon executives consciously began modeling their efforts after "Sesame Street." It was the "gold
standard," says Herb Scannell, Nickelodeon's president. Armed with the idea of a game show for very young
kids, they hired education consultants to recommend academic theories that could be included in the show to
boost "cognitive" skills, such as finding a circle amid a group of other shapes.
The result was "Blue's Clues" in 1996, which quickly became a major hit, challenging shows such as the
sticky-sweet "Barney & Friends." With Blue's Clues, parents felt they could see their kids learning right before
their eyes, as excited four-year-olds shouted answers to questions posed by TV host Steve. One recent
episode featured Blue barking out clues about a book she wanted to read.
In August 2000, Nickelodeon launched "Dora the Explorer." As with Blue's Clues, the original idea was to
create a game show that would reward kids verbally for figuring out answers to questions posed by an
onscreen host. In a nod to the void of shows aimed at the nation's fast-growing Hispanic population, Dora
became an adventurous Latina who spoke both English and Spanish. In a final twist that mirrored the
explosion of home-computer use, especially by children, Dora would live in an animated world inside a
computer. Kids watching at home would be prompted by a talking backpack to choose items like a talking
map that would help Dora solve problems, such as finding her way through a magic forest.
Education consultant Valeria Lovelace, who once ran research at "Sesame Street," was hired to create an
educational underpinning for Dora. She recommended using multiple-intelligence theory. "Multiple intelligence
really is inclusive," she says, meaning that the producers wouldn't have to compromise entertainment values
to provide education. Dr. Lovelace didn't face a hard sell with Ms. Johnson, who had already heard
enthusiastic stories about the notion from her brother, an educator in California.
Dr. Gardner's theory, as it happens, is conveniently suited to the needs of preschool television. Multiple
intelligence is "like reading your horoscope," says Marjorie Kaplan, head of kids' programming at the
Discovery channel. "You can read into it whatever you want and everybody feels comfortable with it."
To reassure parents concerned about Nickelodeon's edgier fare for older kids, Nickelodeon advertised Dora
in parenting magazines. The most common complaint among parents about commercial networks such as
Nickelodeon is the crush of ads for candy, fast food and toys. To address their concerns, Nickelodeon doesn't
interrupt Dora or its other preschool shows with ads, and limits commercials running near Dora to four
minutes an hour, aired between shows -- far fewer than the seven minutes of ads per hour during the rest of
the schedule.
Nickelodeon also looked for confirmation from educators not involved in the creation of Dora that the show
was indeed educational. Last fall, it paid a little more than $30,000 for a researcher at the University of
Kansas to test whether kids who watched Dora learned any Spanish. The study found preschoolers learned
an average of three new Spanish words during the three-month test period and learned more Spanish words
the more they watched the show. Nickelodeon distributed the results to the media and to educators.

--- Page 34 ---
Page 34 of 243 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Emphasizing education also benefits Nickelodeon in other, less obvious ways. Nickelodeon is welcomed into
schools and day-care centers around the country, where its own child-development experts preview story
boards of each episode before small groups of four- and five-year-olds. The idea is to make sure each show
will appeal to preschoolers well before it goes into production. About 70 kids review some part of each Dora
episode, and goodwill about the show is generated every time. Nickelodeon doesn't pay for access to the
schools.
Of course, "Sesame Street" has the most goodwill of all. But the crush of new preschool competitors means
Sesame Street's share of the preschool audience has slipped, and product sales from its last big hit, Elmo,
have slowed. So the nonprofit concern has given "Sesame Street" the first makeover in its 33-year history,
assigning a more prominent role to classic characters such as Big Bird and launching new segments
including Journey to Ernie, a hide-and-seek game that takes place inside a computer. The segment lays the
groundwork for the launch of educational computer software and interactive games.
Sesame Street has also teamed up with Nickelodeon to launch a commercial-free channel called Noggin that
runs old episodes of Sesame Street and other preschool fare all day.
PBS has also responded to the corporate onslaught, dramatically expanding its preschool offerings. It now
takes a percentage of revenue generated by the sale of licensed products spun off from shows airing on its
stations, reducing its reliance on government funding, grants and viewer donations.
To avoid the whiff of over-commercialization and to carefully build demand for Dora products, Nickelodeon
designs its merchandise rollouts to proceed slowly and methodically. In the fourth quarter of 2001, about a
year after Dora made its debut, Dora T-shirts and leggings, which feature Dora exclaiming "Hello!" and
"Hola!," went on sale in select department stores. Dora plush toys made by Gund also appeared in
November. Dora books, licensed through Golden Books, the kids' book giant, made their debut that same
month.
The Dora merchandising push widened at the beginning of this year, expanding to department stores such as
Sears and J.C. Penney. On its Web site, Nickelodeon sells Dora videos, books and a Dora "placement
activity pad." Next, Dora will move into the mass market through a licensing agreement with preschool toy
giant Fisher-Price, a unit of Mattel Inc. Hitting Toys "R" Us shelves this fall is a bilingual Dora doll who sings
the "We did it!" song in English and Spanish; a Dora Explore and Find playset, featuring a giant roll-out map
and plastic Dora figures; and a backpack carrying case modeled after the one Dora always wears.
Disney, for its part, has one of the best merchandising machines in the world for reaching children. But it
needs new franchises, so it's pushing ahead aggressively to develop new shows. Disney Channel, for
example, already airs eight hours every weekday of preschool programs, now sponsored by McDonald's
Corp.
Much of the programming is informed by multiple-intelligence theory, like "The Noodle Dance," a ritual on the
cartoon show "PB&J Otter," about three young otter siblings. Every time the otters do their signature dance,
they give children a way to develop "metacognitive awareness," according to the official Disney curriculum
guide. In the show, "both interpersonal (dealing with others and understanding differences) and intrapersonal
intelligences (self-awareness and self-control) are stressed," the guide explains.

Going Low-Flow---With Drought Worries Rising,Water-Saving Fixtures Boom;A Room-by-Room Guide
The Home Front
WEEKEND JOURNAL
Going Low-Flow --- With Drought Worries Rising, Water-Saving Fixtures Boom; A Room-by-Room
Guide
By June Fletcher
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
1,446 words
22 March 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
W14
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
HOPING TO SAVE a little water -- and money -- Carol Tanzi recently invested in a bunch of new
drought-busting features for her home, including a special new high-tech washing machine and fridge. So
how much did the save on her last water bill? Five dollars. "It's minuscule," says the interior designer from
Burlingame, Calif.
As homeowners all over face an unusually dry spring, more Americans are thinking green -- replacing old
water-wasting appliances and fixtures with everything from dishwashers with electronic dirt sensors to
split-second heaters. The result: Water-saving models, once slow to catch on, now make up 17% of
appliance sales at Sears alone, up from 10% last year.
All that's good news for the environment, but when it comes to saving money, the industry can still be, well,
green. The new models can cost as much as 10 times more -- far beyond what most buyers save, at least
initially. In one study, consumers who replaced not only their old clothes washers and dishwashers, but also
their faucets and showerheads, saved just $17 a month. At that rate, it would take the new gadgets 10 years
to pay for themselves -- longer than many washing machines actually last.
But water savers are the wave of the future. In two years, federal rules will require new kitchen faucets to use
half as much water per minute as they do now. Clothes washing machines will have to be 22% more efficient
and still spin laundry almost dry. Sure, the rules won't apply to existing equipment, but homeowners who do
major renovations will be affected. "We've spent millions and hired doctors of fluid dynamics," says Jim Kukla
of Kohler, a major fixture maker, one of many companies scrambling to meet the expected demand.
So what products to buy? We took at look at many of the new water-savers, and talked to contractors and
homeowners who've installed them. Below, a room-by-room guide:
Kitchen
Forget the humble dishwasher. The latest models now have built-in sensors that monitor just how dirty dishes
are and figure out how much water is needed to get them clean. Whirlpool says its Gold 1000 dishwashers
(about $550) save water through something called an Automatic Purge Filtration System that allows the unit
to fill and drain simultaneously while skimming the water for food particles; other sensors tell how many
dishes are actually being washed.
But if dishwashers are getting smarter about water use, many people aren't, says Ed Pollock, of the
Department of Energy. While new washers are designed so that dishes don't need to be pre-rinsed, 60% of
people go ahead anyway. "Much of the water savings are lost," he says.
Baths
About the only people who don't consider low-flow toilets a joke are builders and plumbers, who've had to
install them for nearly a decade. "The new ones work well," says Charles Clayton III, a Winter Park, Fla.,
builder. The proof: He's put them in his own house.
Makers say they've improved designs and some have added things like gravity-fed siphon jets or electric
pumps that cut water use even more. For instance, Kohler's Power Lite model has a two-horsepower pump
and a split-button flush. The water flow is calibrated to what needs to be swept away. The company says it'll
save the average household about 2,000 gallons of water a year. But at a cost: The toilets are as much as
$2,000 each, or about 10 times a standard fixture.

--- Page 36 ---
Page 36 of 243 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Meanwhile, those motion-sensitive faucets often used in public washrooms are turning up in homes. Delta's
"E-flow" electronic faucet uses "digitally calibrated infrared sensors" to control water flow -- a company
spokesman says it's "targeted at the Palm Pilot crowd." Cost: $400 and up. Meanwhile, showerheads that
aerate water can reduce waste by almost two-thirds.
But, of course, these things save water only if you don't stay in the shower longer to compensate. Joanne
Breger recently put in a low-flow showerhead when she remodeled her bathroom, but now her teenage son
takes such long showers, he uses up all the hot water. "I hate it," says the Vienna, Va., teacher. "By the time
it's my turn, the water's freezing."
Basement
Every time you turn on the faucet and wait for hot water to appear, two or three gallons go down the drain.
Some manufacturers are attempting to cut that time using high-speed pumps that get hot water running
faster. Energy engineer Gary Klein installed one, Metlund's D'MAND, in his 2,400-square-foot Sacramento
home. He says he used to wait four minutes for his shower to warm up; now it's under a minute. The
downside: Because the city charges a flat rate for water, Mr. Klein's $400 system, which he installed himself
in about an hour, doesn't cut his bill.
Of course, the most dramatic way water gets wasted in a house is when something breaks and leaks. Each
year, more than five million water heaters rupture, flooding basements across the land. DynaQuip Controls'
WaterCop, a small black box that attaches to the main water supply, automatically shuts off the water when it
detects a leak. It runs about $350 and can be hooked up to a home's alarm system, too.
Laundry
Clothes washers are some of the biggest water-gobblers in the house, using as much as 55 gallons a load.
Most of the new water-saving machines are front-loaders, which use about half the water of older models and
cause less wear and tear on fabrics.
But some users, like Patricia Schuler, says the machines can stink -- literally -- from mildew, which tends to
collect in some of them. "I walk into my laundry room and am knocked over by the smell," says the
Tyngsboro, Mass. homeowner, who recently bought a Maytag Neptune front-loader. She says the company
sent her a "refresher" kit and suggested running the machine empty once a week. Isn't that a waste of water?
It is "self-defeating," says company spokesman Dale Reeder, who says the problem was "pretty rare" and
that Maytag made some changes in the model to reduce the mildew.
Other makers have been improving front-loaders, adding tilted doors that allow water to sheet off, better
mechanics, and bigger doors and tubs. Among the most popular is the Kenmore's Elite He3t, which uses 16
gallons a load. It costs a hefty $1,450, but, a Sears spokesman says it doesn't seem to matter. "We can't
keep up with demand," he says.
---
Tapped Out
With more than two dozen states around the country facing drought,
some city, state and county governments have begun restricting water
use. Here, some of the measures they've taken.
Location: Santa Fe N.M.
Restriction: Residents can only water lawns three times a week and
can't wash cars
Comment: Reservoirs levels are down by two-thirds; a city water audit
showed that leaky municipal pipes were responsible for up to 20% of
lost water.
Location: Natrona County, Wyo.
Restriction: Water is doled out based on how old your claim to it is;
newer users aren't guaranteed water
Comment: Snowpack is about 40% below normal, meaning spring melt will
be low. Some towns may have to buy water from the U.S. government.
Location: Westchester County N.Y.
Restriction: Voluntary; businesses should cut use by 15%; homeowners
are to use "common sense"
Comment: Homeowners are being asked to take shorter showers, repair
leaks, run full loads in dishwashers. Restaurants only serve water on
request.
Location: Georgia
Restriction: Depending on location, homeowners can water only at

Comment: The state has been in a drought since 1998; farmers were

Afghan Economic Adviser Has His Work Cut Out for Him---Ex-World Bank OfficialStarts With Few FundsAnd No Bank System
International
Afghan Economic Adviser Has His Work Cut Out for Him --- Ex-World Bank Official Starts With Few
Funds And No Bank System
By Ahmed Rashid
Special to The Wall Street Journal
925 words
21 March 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
A19
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
KABUL, Afghanistan -- Apart from Osama bin Laden, Ashraf Ghani may be the only man in Afghanistan more
harried than interim leader Hamid Karzai. The 58-year-old former anthropology professor and World Bank
official is Mr. Karzai's senior economic adviser and head of the newly formed Afghan Assistance Coordination
Authority, which is to oversee the $4.5 billion pledged to the country in January by wealthy nations meeting in
Tokyo.
Mr. Ghani, like his boss, has his work cut out for him. So far, none of the Tokyo money has arrived, and the
government is living hand to mouth to meet its running costs. The government has received grants of as
much as $10 million each from India, Pakistan, Saudi Arabia and the United Arab Emirates -- which Mr.
Karzai carried back in suitcases because there is no banking system in Kabul. The government also has
$100 million in cash and $120 million in gold unfrozen by the U.S. in January (the Clinton administration froze
those assets to punish the Taliban regime).
Like much else in Afghanistan, the monetary system is in shambles. Three local currencies issued by various
warlord factions are creating havoc and fueling inflation. Until a new currency is established, the IMF
recommends the temporary use of dollars for key sectors such as project aid and government salaries. Mr.
Karzai suggests the Euro, but no decision has been made.
On city streets, thousands of unemployed crowd ministries and United Nation offices looking for jobs.
Beggars have staked out houses where foreigners live, and Afghans holding university degrees work as
drivers for foreign agencies. Most cities are utterly destroyed, with no running water, sewerage or central
heating, and only intermittent electricity. Kabul still lies in thousands of acres of rubble. In the countryside
roam 700,000 armed men who must be lured with jobs, education and aid to give up their weapons.
For all that, Mr. Ghani is hopeful, even a little audacious. At a critical meeting with donors and U.N. agencies
on Feb. 28, he issued a tough ultimatum: Kabul, not the donors, would set priorities and development
agendas. "We are fully committed to seeking partnership in this long-term process," he said, "but we must
demand that it be a partnership of equals." While that miffed some donors, others understood. "He was pretty
ruthless," says a U.N. official. "But now everyone agrees that what he said makes a lot of sense."
Mr. Ghani, a former professor of anthropology at Kabul University, left Afghanistan after the 1979 Soviet
invasion and joined John Hopkins University. For the past eight years he has worked in the World Bank's
Russia department, learning how donor countries and multilateral lenders operate. He asked for a leave of
absence from the World Bank after Sept. 11, so he could help the U.N. Special Representative to
Afghanistan, Lakhdar Brahimi, prepare for the Bonn conference in December that established the interim
government. He has since left the World Bank and U.N. to join Mr. Karzai's government.
"The Authority will be the focal point until we can empower the different ministries to take over the task and
make them self-sufficient," says Mr. Ghani. "The purpose of this Authority is to work itself out of a job."
The Authority's first challenge will be establishing the financial transparency needed to win the confidence of
donors, no easy task in a country where everything from jobs to investment is secured with kickbacks and
tribal patronage. "Procurement of raw materials lies at the heart of transparency," says Mr. Ghani. "We are
hiring an international firm for procurement, and donors must agree on unified rules for procurement."
Mr. Ghani, who despite ill health works 20-hour days, throws out ideas by the minute. He wants to use solar
energy and mini-hydroelectric projects as the driving force for village development in order to save fuel and
distribution costs. He is trying to set up a $100 million fund for the reconstruction of Kabul University with the

--- Page 39 ---
Page 39 of 243 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
help of the Carnegie Endowment and several U.S. universities. He is creating an Afghan Peace Corps to
allow young, educated Afghans in the West to teach for six months in Afghan villages.
That's not all he wants from expatriate Afghans. Given their money, expertise and technology, he wants them
to take over ruined state industries and corporations, which he says will be privatized as quickly as possible.
A senior Afghan pilot with a U.S. airline has arrived with American partners to take over the national airline,
Ariana, which now has only one functioning plane. An Afghan engineer has come from Germany to advise on
rebuilding destroyed canals, and a group of Afghan archeologists has arrived from the U.S. to help revive the
Kabul Museum.
On a recent, typical day, Afghan carpet importers waited to see Mr. Ghani in the musty, marbled former
presidential palace that is his office. They have arrived from Europe to revive the country's carpet industry,
and bring back weavers exiled in Pakistan and Iran. Also in line are tribal elders, Western CEOs, foreign-aid
delegations, Afghan expatriates and assorted golddiggers, all looking for investment opportunities or just a
chance to help their country. "Everyone gets about 10 minutes with him," says a secretary." That is all he has
time for."

Review / Books:All the Colors of the Rainbow and Then Some
WEEKEND JOURNAL
Review / Books: All the Colors of the Rainbow and Then Some
By Alexander Theroux
1,135 words
15 March 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
W8
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
BRIGHT EARTH
By Philip Ball
(Farrar, Straus and Giroux, 382 pages, $30)
WHEN I discovered to my surprise last summer in Vermont that one of my favorite painters, Sarah Son, often
used only a four-color palette -- the primary colors plus white -- I was astonished at how much she got out of
them, such dramatic power in skies and clouds, hills and valleys, water and trees . . . landscapes that came
alive without the full use of Newton's rainbow scale. It seemed, to my mind, such a narrow aperture,
considering the wide and subtle spectrum of color that is available to any artist these days -- a Shaker sense
of color economy. But it worked, to marvelous effect.
Arguably there is something classical about Ms. Son's work. It is believed that the Greeks, for instance,
austerely used only four colors in their art (black, white, red and yellow), and for centuries thereafter the color
range of artists may have been almost as limited. But what a kaleidoscope of choices have been given us in
the modern age, especially with the technological advances of the early 19th century and the dramatic
innovations in pigment manufacturing! The list of colorants industrially manufactured nowadays in the Colour
Index International, that bible of dyers and colorists, runs to 9,000 pages in nine volumes.
How did this riot of possibilities come about? "Bright Earth" attempts to answer that question, and many
others. The book is an informed story of color, from the ancient world's earth dyes of basic browns and reds
to the metallic flakes and fluorescent flashes of modern chemistry. Philip Ball, a science writer whose
previous books have had to do with the natural world -- notably "The Self-Made Tapestry: Pattern Formation
in Nature and Life's Matrix" -- knows how to present an esoteric subject in accessible prose.
Here he chronicles how colors evolved through art and science to become what they are today. He examines
not only the nature of color and its origin but its use by artists, from the crude and elemental tones of Apelles,
the court painter of Alexander the Great, to Andy Warhol, the court painter of his own court, with his displays
of bold pink, citron, mad black and apple green.
The arc of the book is the history it presents -- color as the practical side of various and surprising materials,
discovered over time. What a world of colors we live in! And what a grab bag of strange ones there are!
Cochineal, a scarlet-red from an insect. Coal-tar yellow. Fuchsin, which is close to purple. Rose Bengal.
Purpurin, a reddish purple from a root. Gold phosphor. Scheele's green, with its slightly dirty tone.
Chrysocolla ("gold glue"), a green silicate often used as an adhesive for gold-leaf.
Along with explaining color's psychology, traditions and prejudices, Mr. Ball reminds us that the modern
chemical industry was nurtured by the demand for color. One of the first synthetic pigments, tartrazine yellow,
was patented in 1884. So-called quinacridone pigments, going from orange-red to violet, were discovered in
1896 but commercially made (by DuPont) only in the 1950s, when they were quickly adopted by the Abstract
Expressionists, who loved their strong hues. In 1983 new organic pigments called diketopyrrolopyrroles --
DPPs -- were patented, and their sharp colors became popular in the automobile industry.
One could profitably pursue blue alone. "Blue gives other colors their vibration," Paul Cezanne once said.
There is ultramarine, with its rich touch of purple, originally from lapis lazuli. Smalt, a deep blue from silica.
Indigo, always with its slightly greenish tinge. Verditer (verd de terre), a cheap option that approximated
azurite, that ground-up natural mineral that, when mixed with a water-soluble gum, the Egyptians used to
such beauty -- a sweet rich light blue -- on papyrus and wall paintings more than 3,000 years ago.
Mr. Ball is wonderful on how the concern to match colors to nature placed great demands on artists who,
seeking novelty in place of traditional formulas, were hungry for new colorants and eager to spend their

--- Page 41 ---
Page 41 of 243 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
ingenuity in mixing them to consequence. Renaissance patrons and clients who could not afford the brilliant
blue of expensive ultramarine might choose, for example, a synthetic "copper blue," essentially an artificial
approximation of azurite (basic copper carbonate). In the 17th century, "bys" was only an adjective denoting
dark in the color blue, but 100 years later "bice" actually became a color, one indicating, oddly, both green
and blue.
With all these blue notes, I was brought back to a visit I once made to Giotto's Arena Chapel in Padua, Italy,
and saw again the heavenly blue of that matchless church, with its ceiling that Proust said was "so blue that it
seems as though the radiant day has crossed the threshold with the human visitor."
But of course color is often far from ethereal. One discovers in "Bright Earth," among other things, that Indian
yellow (puri), a 19th-century color sold in foul balls, was created from the urine of cows fed on a diet of
mango leaves. And it is possible that Napoleon died of poisoning from the arsenical fumes exuded by toxic
green paint on the damp walls of his house on St. Helena.
Mr. Ball is fascinating on the details of art history. We learn, for example, that Botticelli laced his goddess's
hair with gold -- the metal, that is -- in "The Birth of Venus." We hear how Constable's patron, Sir George
Beaumont, undermined the artist's urge for more color by expostulating: "A good picture, like a good fiddle,
should be brown." And it is revealing, if sad, to be told that Frank Stella, in the 1970s, used household
industrial paints straight from the can. He confessed: "I just took what the regular paint stores had to offer."
Who can easily process the fact that Mr. Stella, along with Mark Rothko and Robert Rauschenberg, applied to
their canvases metallic pigments meant for radiators and boat hulls?
Mr. Ball does not limit himself to color from the artist's hand. He discusses art in reproduction and offers
illuminating accounts of early attempts at full-color printing, from lithography to photography. And he captures
the new horizons of color on computer screens. "I cannot imagine," he writes, "that digital technology will fail
to be valorized as a means of creative expression once there exists a generation of artists more accustomed
to holding a mouse than a paintbrush." It's a long way from Giotto and Proust, but there is no reason to feel
blue about it.
---
Mr. Theroux is the author of "The Primary Colors" and "The Secondary Colors," among many other books.

Business World:The New Business Casual: Prison Stripes
Business World: The New Business Casual: Prison Stripes
By Holman W. Jenkins Jr.
1,161 words
13 March 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
A19
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Correction
As a matter of clarification, we would like to make it plain that two former, not current, executives of Indus
International are under indictment. Former chairman and CEO William Grabske and former chief
administrative officer Robert Pocsik were indicted in September on securities-fraud charges. The indictments
were mentioned in Tuesday's Business World column on this page, "The New Business Casual -- Prison
Stripes."
(WSJ March 15, 2002)
About now it would be right to say of Enron and all its works, ho hum. Companies rise and fall, which is why
investors are advised to diversify. Some companies even go bankrupt, and sometimes fraud plays a role.
Happily, the economy and stock market are reviving sooner than anyone expected. Happily, too, Republicans
are in office, living out the great Republican tradition of refraining from hysterical overreaction. George W.
Bush has set forth a typically GOP plan to hold executives liable to give up their ill-gotten gains. (They
already are.) Treasury Secretary Paul O'Neill demands that miscreant CEOs should serve time. (They
already do.) SEC Chief Harvey Pitt says superficial adherence to accounting norms won't get fraudsters off
the hook. (It never did.) How's that for conservative?
Enron may be an occasion for the born-yesterday types of the higher media to discover and become
hysterical about accounting flim flams. History, though, will probably record the Enron scandal as an end not
a beginning, the culmination of a cycle of bad accounting and efforts to clean up. Consider an outpouring of
recent indictments and convictions:
Ian Wilson, once the vice chairman of Coca-Cola and odds-on CEO candidate, pleaded guilty in September
to concealing expenses at Aurora Foods, maker of Lender's Bagels and Log Cabin syrup. Paul Polishan,
CFO of dressmaker Lesley Fay, was sentenced last month to nine years in prison for a variety of
sales-padding ploys.
Richard Rubin of Donnkenny, another fashion group, was sentenced in September 2000 to three years'
supervision and ordered to pay $111 million in restitution on similar charges. Two top officers of swimwear
maker Sirena Apparel copped pleas last April to various revenue-inflation schemes, including resetting a
computer clock so sales could be moved to an earlier period.
Silicon Valley has been a fruitful vineyard for prosecutors. David Thatcher, president of once-lauded e-mail
outsourcer Critical Path Inc., pleaded guilty in February to faking or backdating sales to meet quarterly
revenue targets. And the CEO and CFO of Indus International, maker of asset management software, were
indicted last month for allegedly shipping two bottles of wine to a prospective customer and then using the
FedEx slip to convince a bookkeeper that a nonexistent software sale had been made.
Coming soon are trials of Walter A. Forbes and E. Kirk Shelton, former top officers of CUC International,
accused of fabricating $500 million in bogus operating income before selling their company to Cendant. Also
on the docket are Jay Gilbertson and Albert Bergonzi, on charges arising from the sale of medical software
maker HBOC to McKesson Corp.
Last month, Steven M. Lash, chief financial officer of FPA Medical Management, once a market darling in the
business of physician-practice rollups, was indicted on 28 counts of bookkeeping fraud. Perhaps worse, he
faces a horde of angry docs who sold their practices for FPA stock just before the company collapsed in
1998.
We could blather on and on. An astonishing number of business execs face jail or indictment for bookkeeping
fraud. Federal prosecutors, weighed down as they are with drug cases, once left such behavior to the civil

--- Page 43 ---
Page 43 of 243 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
courts. Change was coming even before SEC Chairman Arthur Levitt began rallying opinion in the late
Clinton years with speeches against "accounting hocus-pocus."
U.S. attorney's offices in New York and San Francisco led the way with the Cendant and Silicon Valley
prosecutions. Such cases entail a great deal of scutwork and took years to reach court. But now their forensic
teams are up and rolling. We wouldn't be sleeping too well if we were, say, Linda Wachner, ex-CEO of
Warnaco, the clothing manufacturer that entered bankruptcy in June amid questionable use of "special"
charges to wipe away routine expenses. Nor can Rite Aid executives be happy campers to find themselves in
the sights of David Barasch, U.S. attorney for the Middle District of Pennsylvania, who honed his forensics on
the investigations that produced a nine-year sentence for Lesley Fay's Mr. Polishan.
History suggests that when these convictions start reaching the appellate level, some will be thrown out as
misguided or overreaching. But the larger point is that the institutional web was sensitive enough to pick up
the vibrations and attack an outbreak of sloppy accounting before anybody noticed Enron's iniquities.
We won't thumb through the list of factors contributing to the epidemic (see our 6/13 column). But to blame
management stock options and Wall Street's relentless profit expectations (the media's favorite analysis) is
an unhelpful redundancy. You might as well blame the gold medal for Olympic cheating. Drawing a goal line
is tantamount to inviting somebody somewhere to try to cross it fraudulently.
Yet now Congress proposes to mandate dubious accounting of its own, forcing companies to deduct the
"cost" of executive stock options from reported profits. Fans quote Warren Buffett to the effect that "If options
aren't compensation and compensation isn't an expense, what is it?"
Well, management's stock options clearly are not an expense, because they don't consume cash generated
by the underlying business. Ideally, stock options belong not on the company's balance sheet but on
shareholders' individual balance sheets. After all, shareholders have contracted a liability in the form of an
agreement to transfer a portion of their ownership rights to management if certain targets are met. It's up to
the board and shareholders themselves to make sure these targets adequately compensate them for giving
up a stake in their firm.
No less illogical is blanket criticism of options "repricing" -- that is, rewriting the terms if business setbacks
have put the targets hopelessly out of reach. If management is failing, the solution is to replace it. But let's not
confuse the goal of stock options, which is behavior modification for the hired help. If this incentive makes
sense before a company's stock price falls apart, it makes sense after.
That's a choice individual companies and their shareholders can make. As in every human endeavor, some
managers will try to cheat and some will delude themselves that changing numbers on a ledger can alter
economic reality. Indeed, every case mentioned above represents one tendency or the other (and often both).
That doesn't disprove the argument for stock options. Folly and perversity are eternal. So is the battle to keep
them in check.

Thinking Things Over:Monetary Machismo in Mexico
Thinking Things Over: Monetary Machismo in Mexico
By Robert L. Bartley
1,090 words
11 March 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
A19
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
ALAMOS, Mexico -- For a generation the big issue in Mexico has been the peso exchange rate; the
inauguration of President Vicente Fox was the first since 1976 not involved with a currency crisis. But today
Mexico has a new worry, that the peso is not too weak, but too strong.
"We have the only currency in the world that's appreciated against the U.S. dollar," one savvy Mexican
remarks. Indeed, the peso gained some 5% against the dollar during 2001, trading at 9.16 to the dollar at the
end of the year compared to 9.65 a year earlier. The loudest complaints come from exporters, traditionally
advocates of a weak currency. But coming to this remote Sonoran village for an annual gathering of
economists, I suspect for the first time that their concerns are valid.
Especially so since the peso strength certainly doesn't reflect strength in the economy. When GDP figures are
finalized for 2001, they will show an outright decline, somewhere between zero and 1%, contrasted with 6.9%
growth in 2000. Political expectations have similarly collapsed. President Fox won a place in history by
ending 71 years of one-party rule, but his immediate standing has now confronted reality. He does not
command a majority in a divided and newly powerful Congress, and his reform proposals were shredded in
the December budget negotiations.
The recent gyrations in the Mexican economy obviously reflect a similar roller-coaster in the United States.
Mexican officialdom calculates that a peso officially floating against the dollar provides immunity from a
precipitous decline like the 1994-95 crisis or Argentina's current debacle. With even Alan Greenspan now
saying American recovery is under way, Mexico expects to be pulled back to prosperity by the big locomotive
north of the Rio Grande.
Now that the economy here is dominated by something close to free trade with the world's biggest market,
political problems no longer have as much economic fallout. Yet exchange rates, fixed or floating, are
basically a reflection of monetary policy; currency crises come from getting monetary policy wrong.
To maintain a fixed exchange rate against the dollar, or a crawling band like Mexico officially pursued in 1994,
a central bank has to target the exchange rate, issuing less money if its currency declines and more if it
rises. A floating exchange rate means that the central bank targets something else, gauging its issue of
money according to some measure of the money supply, some estimate of the inflation rate, the value of
gold or some set of sensitive commodities.
Under Governor Guillermo Ortiz, the Banco de Mexico has been targeting the inflation rate; its explicit
objective is to converge with the U.S. consumer price index by 2003. It's well along toward this goal; Mexican
inflation hit a record low in 2001, with prices up 4.4%, against 8.9% in 2000 (and 52% in post-devaluation
1995).
Inflation targeting has so far served Mexico well, but in my view a trap lies ahead. Yes, Mexico, given its
experience, has much to fear from inflation. But there is no particular reason it should have the same rate of
consumer price increases as the U.S., and trying to drive it to that level would be a serious mistake.
Even with the same currency, a rapidly growing region will show faster price increases than a slowly growing
one. That is how the price system works; higher prices serve to attract the resources growth requires. If the
two regions have different currencies, this price effect shows up as different rates of "inflation." During
Japan's great growth years after World War II, for example, the yen was stable against the dollar but internal
consumer prices rose rapidly.
Ireland has just been through this experience in transition to the euro. Ireland cut taxes to turn itself into a
European tiger economy. But while monetary growth throughout Euroland was set in Frankfurt, Ireland
recorded rapid price increases. It had to fight against pressure to curb this "inflation" with fiscal policy -- that
is by reducing Irish growth rates.

--- Page 45 ---
Page 45 of 243 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Similarly, I once inquired why Hong Kong showed rapid increases in consumer prices though its currency was
rigidly linked to the U.S. dollar by a currency board. The most prominent local economist explained that
ultimately a consumer price index is composed mostly of wages, and as Hong Kong became a financial
center its wages had to be arbitraged to world levels. The measured "inflation," that is, represented Hong
Kong citizens getting rich.
Mexico is like Ireland or Hong Kong, or at least should aspire to be. As its economy becomes integrated with
the U.S. many things will converge, but for Mexico the ultimate convergence should be in wage rates. If the
convergence instead is in inflation rates, there is no way Mexican workers can catch up. Indeed, if the
"inflation" rates were identical, it would be a sign of something curiously amiss -- what economists would call
a disequilibrium.
My guru on exchange rates is the Nobel Prize economist Robert Mundell, who thinks in terms of a hierarchy
of monetary targets. To curb hyperinflation, target the money supply. As prices come under control, an
inflation target can promote stability -- though if pushed too far inflation targets will result in an "overvalued"
currency inviting a speculative attack. For a small open economy the real coup de grace is to target the
exchange rate of a large stable neighbor.
Fixing to the dollar is not a fashionable idea at the moment because of the chaos in Argentina. Yet in fact
floating the Argentine peso has only made matters worse, and the currency board there did provide a few
years of stability, the first in generations of Argentine experience. Squandered opportunity is often
compounded by the wrong lessons.
Mexico has also had a series of unhappy experiences, but has managed to learn from them. It's become an
open economy, shedding a history of autarkic development. It's become much more democratic; President
Fox's budget problems mean a president needs to learn how to persuade rather than command. Its
once-notorious monetary management has improved; Governor Ortiz has tamed inflation and also
established central bank independence. I only hope he sees this as accomplishment enough, because his
monetary targets between now and 2003 look like, well, dangerous machismo.
(See related letter: "Letters to the Editor: Pesos, Dollars and the Mexican Worker" -- WSJ Mar. 21, 2002)

Hollywood Journal:Most Screen Time in a Supporting Role
WEEKEND JOURNAL
Hollywood Journal: Most Screen Time in a Supporting Role
By Tom King
1,655 words
8 March 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
W1
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
IN "TRAINING DAY," actor Ethan Hawke is in almost every single scene of the movie. You might think that
means he played a leading role.
But not at Academy Awards time.
In a little-noticed but potentially embarrassing development this Oscar season, a few nominees for
"supporting" actor could well have been picked for the leading actor or actress slots. Think Jennifer Connelly
deserves the trophy for best supporting actress? Maybe so, but she's on the screen as much as best actress
nominee Sissy Spacek. Mr. Hawke? Though he's up for best supporting actor, he's on 13 minutes more than
co-star Denzel Washington, the best actor nominee.
Chalk it up to growing Oscar hunger. While this kind of award deflation has happened before, people in
Hollywood say it's happening more now because of overheated jockeying for the awards -- any award.
Studios are expected to spend a record $50 million campaigning for Oscars, including, some now say, on
trying to make sure some of their actors get bumped down. One studio even had a meltdown when it
discovered that Ms. Connelly was a best actress nominee in another contest because of a "clerical" mistake.
The worry: Academy voters might be confused.
"Apparently `supporting' has taken on a whole new meaning these days," says Cheryl Boone Isaacs, a
longtime studio publicity executive who sits on the Academy's board of governors.
With two weeks until the Oscar ceremonies, we couldn't resist pulling out our stopwatches and comparing the
onscreen time for this year's supporting nominees with those in the leading category. Along with Mr. Hawke
and Ms. Connelly, we discovered that Jim Broadbent, who plays the caring husband of novelist Iris Murdoch
in "Iris," is onscreen 60% of the time -- more than much-touted best actress nominee Halle Berry. Judi Dench,
his wife in the movie, has two more minutes onscreen than he does, which apparently she used well. She
could take home the best actress statuette.
And there's bad news for Jon Voight. His hammy performance as sportscaster Howard Cosell in "Ali" won
mostly favorable reviews, but clocked in at just 13 minutes. Rival Mr. Hawke had an hour and 48 minutes to
impress Academy voters.
How does this happen? Unbeknownst to most moviegoers, there are no rules governing which category an
actor or actress can be nominated in. When the nomination ballots go out, they're blank, simply accompanied
by "reminder lists," naming the casts of all eligible pictures. Only the 1,315 members of the Academy's actors'
branch nominate actors, although all 5,739 members of the Academy can vote for the winner.
The process has given voters the chance to occasionally downgrade actors with leading-size roles, such as
George Burns, who won the Oscar for best supporting actor in "The Sunshine Boys" (his co-star, Walter
Matthau, was nominated for best actor). Yet studios were once more willing to allow leading actors in the
same film to compete against one another in the same category. Shirley MacLaine and Debra Winger vied for
Best Actress in "Terms of Endearment," as did Susan Sarandon and Geena Davis in "Thelma & Louise."
But this year, Hollywood's Oscar campaigning has been particularly intense, because so many races are wide
open. You might call it the Marcia Gay Harden effect: Last year, she was the leading lady in "Pollock,"
working onscreen for more than half of the movie, yet the studio succeeded in getting Academy voters to
think of her as a supporting player. (She won.) A little-known name can have a better chance of winning in the
supporting category, says Tony Angellotti, an Oscar-campaign strategist. "It changes the voters' attitude
toward the performance," he says.
Apparently, even in a town with rampant credit inflation, the actors don't seem to mind. People close to the
film "Iris" say Mr. Broadbent asked Miramax to promote him only for best supporting actor, which it did. The

--- Page 47 ---
Page 47 of 243 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
actor declined to comment, but voters at last month's British Academy Awards didn't agree, and nominated
him for best actor. (He lost.) Similarly, New Line Cinema originally ran ads promoting Ian McKellen in the best
actor category for "The Lord of the Rings," until the actor asked them not to; Mr. McKellen didn't want
Academy voters to think he was trying to steal the spotlight from an ensemble cast, says Russell Schwartz,
New Line's president of domestic marketing.
According to our stopwatches, in his supporting role, Mr. McKellen was in 41% of the movie -- which,
interestingly, is more than any of the women nominated in the supporting category. Indeed, our survey found
that women generally had a lot less screen time than men. Mr. Hawke's supporting role, for instance, involved
more screen time than four of the five best actress nominees. Another interesting finding: Ms. Spacek,
considered by many a favorite for best actress, has the shortest time onscreen of any of the nominees in the
leading category, male or female. But Renee Zellweger can't complain about her time; she's onscreen a
staggering 97% of the time in "Bridget Jones's Diary."
Of course, in most cases, leading nominees still have the most screen time. And screen time is just one factor
in determining which category performances ought to be considered in. While everyone can probably agree
that Ms. Dench's nine-minute, Oscar-winning turn in "Shakespeare in Love" was definitely a supporting role,
other cases are less clear-cut. Hollywood executives note that in some movies, several actors have lots of
screen time, but often only one is the character whose "journey" the audience takes in watching the film. Still,
even under that scenario, Mr. Hawke's nomination has raised some eyebrows. "It's his training day," notes
one Academy voter.
"It's a tough call to make," says Dawn Taubin, president of marketing at Warner Bros., which released
"Training Day," about a rookie being shown the ropes by a corrupt cop. (Both actors had their names above
the film's title, giving them star billing.) "I think that Ethan's character is more reactive to Denzel's character.
Denzel is sort of the motivator for what happens in the movie."
While the studios say actors such as Messrs. Hawke and McKellen are simply trying to avoid upstaging their
co-stars, critics say such jockeying creates at least the impression that actors today are just trying to assure
they land Oscar gold. It also raises other delicate questions: With almost three times as much screen time as
one of her rivals, for example, should Ms. Connelly really be evaluated against her fellow best supporting
nominees? Is it fair to Helen Mirren, whose 16 minutes onscreen in "Gosford Park" truly make her a
supporting player?
In Ms. Connelly's case, Universal doesn't deny that "one of the factors" for campaigning for her in the
supporting category was simply that it thought she'd have a better chance of winning there, instead of duking
it out against Ms. Spacek and Ms. Berry in the best actress category. "We knew from the day we saw the
movie that she was a supporting actress," says a spokeswoman for the studio.
Still, not everybody apparently knew. Late last year, Universal submitted Ms. Connelly's name -- as best
actress -- for the Screen Actors Guild Awards, which will be presented Sunday. That submission? The studio
says it was a clerical error.

questions in this space.

Productivity Growth Surges Despite Recession
Economy
Productivity Growth Surges Despite Recession
By Russell Gold
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
703 words
8 March 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
U.S. workers and companies are emerging from the economic contraction leaner and more productive than
earlier thought, according to new data from the Labor Department.
Nonfarm labor productivity -- a measure of output per hour worked -- grew by an annualized 5.2% in the final
three months of 2001, up from a previous estimate of 3.5%, the department said. For all of 2001, productivity
grew by 1.9%, down from 3.3% in 2000 and 2.6% in the late 1990s, but still regarded by economists as an
impressive performance in a year marked by a downturn.
"Typically, in a recession, you have at best flat productivity and it's usually down 1% or 2%; for it to be up 2%
on a year-over-year basis is a spectacular performance," said Stan Shipley, senior economist at Merrill
Lynch. He added that, despite the bursting of the tech-stock bubble, the Internet and other computer
advances have "changed how business operates," creating a more productive economy.
Shortly after the productivity report was released, Federal Reserve Chairman Alan Greenspan cast some
doubt on the numbers. Appearing before the Senate Banking Committee, he responded to a question by
saying the 5.2% figure was "not only remarkably robust, but very unlikely." He didn't dispute the overall trend
toward greater productivity, however.
Mr. Greenspan has argued that productivity gains have fundamentally changed the economy, but some
economists were reserving judgment until the economy went through an entire business cycle -- a period of
expansion and then contraction. Now, the data suggest that gains in productivity experienced in the late
1990s are here to stay.
"We wanted to know would we give back in the slump what we gained in the upswing," says Robert
Lawrence, an economics professor at Harvard University's John F. Kennedy School of Government. "What
we've learned is, we didn't give it back in the slump, and that is heartening."
Increased worker productivity, however, means that employment gains will lag behind as the economy
expands again, as companies won't need to rehire laid-off workers as quickly as they might otherwise.
Meanwhile, the Labor Department reported new claims for unemployment insurance fell 5,000 to 376,000 last
week. The four-week moving average, which is watched more closely as it smoothes out fluctuations,
dropped 1,250 to 372,750, its lowest point since August.
In data released separately, there were reminders of the downturn's lingering economic damage. U.S.
household net worth declined in 2001 for the second straight year, as losses in the stock market more than
offset rising home values, according to the Federal Reserve's "flow of funds" report. The Fed reported that
household net worth -- a measure of total assets, such as houses and pensions, minus total liabilities, such
as credit-card debt -- declined 2.4% year over year to $40.3 trillion at the end of 2001. Before this current
two-year decline, household net worth had increased every year for more than half a century.
The decline last year was driven by an increase in value of $576 billion in liabilities, led by a rise in home
mortgages. Assets decreased $414 billion, mostly because of stock-market losses.
In another report issued yesterday, the Fed reported that U.S. consumer credit expanded $12.8 billion in
January to a seasonally adjusted $1.668 trillion. This followed a revised $1.8 billion increase in December, a
considerable swing from a previously reported decrease of $5.1 billion for the month. With the most recent
monthly data, consumer credit is growing at an annual rate of 9.3%, slightly up from the annual rate of 8.5%
in the last three months of 2001.

Tepid Rebound Has Begun, Fed Suggests---View of Beige Book ReportIs Dimmer Than Others;Factory Data Strengthen
Economy
Tepid Rebound Has Begun, Fed Suggests --- View of Beige Book Report Is Dimmer Than Others;
Factory Data Strengthen
By Russell Gold
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
854 words
7 March 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
The economy appears to have bottomed out and is beginning a lukewarm recovery, a Federal Reserve
survey of regional economic conditions suggests.
There are widespread "signs of improvement," notes the survey, known as the beige book, which covered
January and February. While neither the manufacturing nor service sectors have completely shaken off
recent sluggishness, both appear to be on the mend. On the downside, widespread weakness in the
commercial real-estate market persists, the Fed said.
Separately, a government report provided fresh evidence of a broad-based pickup in factory orders.
On the whole, the beige book report paints a more tepid picture of the economic recovery than do strong
gains in other recent indicators, such as construction spending, consumer spending and sentiment among
manufacturing executives.
The survey appears to be consistent with recent comments of Fed officials. Yesterday, Philadelphia Federal
Reserve Bank President Anthony Santomero said he was "cautiously optimistic" about the economy and
expected a mild recovery.
Federal Reserve Board Chairman Alan Greenspan said last week that a relatively weak economic recovery
appears to be under way; Mr. Greenspan made his comments in testimony accompanying his semiannual
monetary-policy report to a House committee. Mr. Greenspan is scheduled to address a Senate committee
today. His testimony is usually the same to both panels. It is possible the Fed chief will tweak his testimony
today, given that several upbeat economic reports have been released since his House appearance. He
made such an update to his testimony in February 2001, but the changes were slight.
The findings in the beige book are used by Fed policy makers to determine whether to tweak interest rates.
While the Fed was quick to slash the federal-funds rate by 4.75 percentage points over the past 14 months,
the report's suggestion of a slow emergence from the downturn suggests that the Fed will not raise rates
anytime soon, says Paul McCulley, managing director of Pacific Investment Management Co. "Mr.
Greenspan was clear last week that he wants to nurse this thing along and doesn't have any intention of
throwing ice cubes in the punch bowl just as the party gets going," he says.
The beige book reported that wage pressures across the country ranged between "subdued" and "largely
nonexistent," largely because labor markets were slack. There were signs, however, from temporary
employment agencies that upward pressure on wages could emerge in the coming months as demand picks
up.
Both the Richmond, Va., and Atlanta districts reported improvements in labor placement by temp agencies,
leading the Fed to report that "the worst seems to be over." As for pressures on pricing, manufacturers
reported flat or declining costs for material and energy, but health-insurance costs were rising.
The report cited better conditions in industries and regions that were flat or in decline until only a few weeks
ago. The software industry in the Boston district reported improved business, as did the trucking industry in
Cleveland, hotels in Atlanta and high-tech manufacturers in San Francisco.
But the St. Louis district reported a weak manufacturing climate, while too much capacity continues to
hamstring the petrochemical industry around Houston and in Louisiana. The commercial real-estate markets
in Memphis, Tenn., Louisville, Ky., and Denver suffered from excess office space.

--- Page 52 ---
Page 52 of 243 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
A majority of the districts reported "modest improvements" in retail sales when compared with the year-earlier
period and expects small gains throughout 2002. One executive in the Boston district predicted "something
between dead flat and the beginning of a very modest recovery."
Yesterday, the Commerce Department reported that factory orders rose 1.6% in January, marking the third
consecutive increase, including a 0.7% increase in December. Manufacturing orders for January were a
seasonally adjusted $325.8 billion, nearing but still shy of the $332.4 billion level reached in August before the
terrorist attacks.
Manufactured durable-goods orders were up 2.0% to $178 billion, revised downward from 2.6% as orders
from the defense industry weren't as strong as previously thought. However, nondurable orders rose a
larger-than-expected 1.1%, led by strong orders for food products.

Imminent Deposit-Insurance Cap Spurs a Mini Gold Rush in Japan
International
Imminent Deposit-Insurance Cap Spurs a Mini Gold Rush in Japan
By Ichiko Fuyuno
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
571 words
6 March 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
A13
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
TOKYO -- Last month, Isamu Suzuki walked into a small Tokyo gold shop with a black purse tucked tightly
under his arm. He sat at the counter, opened the purse and plunked down a thick wad of bills. A few minutes
later the 71-year-old, still-working sailor walked out with a 1.3 million yen ($9,832) gold bar in his purse.
As fear of a financial crisis grows, thousands more Japanese have conducted this simple transaction in
recent weeks, sending the retail price of gold up about 12% since Jan. 7, to 1,381 yen a gram yesterday at
Tanaka Kikinzoku, Japan's biggest gold company. This rise has enticed more investors into the gold game.
Some see this mini gold rush as a first sign of panic among savers eager to protect their assets before April
1, when the government will end its unlimited guarantee on time deposits, capping coverage at 10 million yen
per depositor, per bank. Many predict a headlong rush from the nation's bad-loan-ridden banks and into gold,
foreign currency and foreign stocks -- a prospect that has the media in a froth and brokers licking their chops.
But is the prediction on target? There's evidence of some individuals moving their money in preparation for
April 1, but the bulk of the country's savings looks set to stay in banks, and in Japan. A September
central-bank survey showed that 99% of individual accounts held less than 10 million yen, meaning that the
insurance cap will expose only a small slice of depositors to a potential loss.
To be sure, some analysts believe that sudden withdrawals by large-lot savers -- namely companies and local
governments -- whose accounts exceed the new insurance limits pose a bigger threat to weak banks than do
consumers. And some consumers themselves may indeed be shopping for alternatives: In December, the
balance of time deposits at domestic banks slid 0.5% from a year earlier to 265 trillion yen, while regular
deposits -- which are exempt from the insurance cap for another year -- climbed 14% to 127 trillion yen. Large
gold shops in Tokyo claim they had five to 10 times as many customers in February as a year earlier.
But such investments remain just a nibble in the overall savings pie. According to the World Gold Council,
Japanese individuals purchased roughly 70 billion yen worth of gold in 2001, a fraction of their nearly 1,400
trillion yen in assets. They hold 716 trillion yen in banks and the nation's postal savings system, 407 trillion
yen in insurance or pension assets, and four trillion yen in foreign currency.
Most investors are keeping their money in Japan, just spreading it out a bit. Mr. Suzuki, for one, is building up
a small store of gold, but sees it only as a minor form of insurance. Most of his nearly 30 million yen in
savings sits comfortably in several small bank accounts, earning a mere 0.02% annual interest. He has five
million yen in a credit association, just under 10 million yen in a major regional lender, and 10 million yen in
postal savings, all in regular savings accounts.
April 1 is "no problem," he says, "as long as I make deposits at different banks."

Nonmanufacturing Index Surges,Another Economic-Recovery Sign
Economy
Nonmanufacturing Index Surges, Another Economic-Recovery Sign
By Russell Gold
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
420 words
6 March 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
In another indication the economy is recovering more rapidly than expected, nonmanufacturing business
activity surged ahead in February, as new orders grew in sectors as diverse as retail, mining and legal
services.
The purchasing and supply executives' index maintained by the Institute of Supply Management, formerly the
National Association of Purchasing Management, jumped 9.1 to 58.7, exceeding the most buoyant forecasts
and reaching the highest reading since November 2000. A reading above 50 indicates that the
nonmanufacturing sector of the economy is growing.
The index has been seesawing in recent months, reaching 50.1 in December before retreating somewhat to
49.6 in January. The strong February numbers indicate that "we have broken out and will continue to grow,"
says Ralph Kauffman, chairman of the survey committee.
The executives surveyed reported surprisingly strong confidence in an emerging economic recovery, Mr.
Kauffman says. The nonmanufacturing index conforms to a similarly strong showing by the same group's
manufacturing index released Friday. The manufacturing index rose 4.8 to 54.7, the first reading in 19 months
indicating growth.
The survey also included another sign that executives are growing more comfortable. Led by retail trade,
nonmanufacturing companies reported an increase in inventory for the first time in 16 months, as companies
began building stockpiles in anticipation that consumer spending will remain strong.
Resumed economic growth doesn't appear to be generating jobs, however, at least for now. The survey
component that tracks employment dropped to 43.6 from 44.5, indicating an acceleration in work-force
contraction. "Companies want to wait and see if this is for real before they add a permanent employee or
even a temporary employee," says Mr. Kauffman.
The combination of increased business activity without new jobs means that a more productive economy is
emerging from the recession. "It's turning out to be a high-productivity, low-inflation recovery," says Maury
Harris, chief U.S. economist for UBS Warburg. He predicts such a recovery will make the Federal Reserve's
Open Market Committee more tolerant of the surprisingly strong growth and thus less likely to raise interest
rates. The Federal Reserve cut interest rates aggressively during the downturn, and observers are looking
for signs of whether rate increases are likely.

reports, in the Online Journal at WSJ.com/JournalLinks.
