Trading Charges:Lawsuit SpotlightsJ.P. Morgan's TiesTo the Enron Debacle---Insurers Balk at Paying BankUp to $1 Billion in ClaimsOn Complex...
Trading Charges: Lawsuit Spotlights J.P. Morgan's Ties To the Enron Debacle --- Insurers Balk at
Paying Bank Up to $1 Billion in Claims On Complex Transactions --- Update in a Glass Room
By Jathon Sapsford and Anita Raghavan
Staff Reporters of The Wall Street Journal
2,573 words
25 January 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Corrections & Amplifications
AN OFFSHORE ENTITY called Mahonia Ltd. was set up by Chase Manhattan Bank before its merger with
J.P. Morgan & Co. to become J.P. Morgan Chase & Co. A page-one article on Friday incorrectly said
Mahonia was the creation of J.P. Morgan & Co. before its merger with Chase.
(WSJ Jan. 28, 2002)
When J.P. Morgan & Co. set up an energy-trading business in the British Channel Islands a decade ago, the
tiny venture barely caused a ripple at the giant bank.
The operation, called Mahonia Ltd., consisted of just a small office with lots of phone lines. But the
Jersey-based business grew over the years to transact billions of dollars of natural-gas contracts with other
energy companies. Mahonia's trading followed a simple pattern: Many of its transactions took place just
before year-end. Often, the deliveries of natural gas and oil were sold right back to those who delivered them
through complex derivative transactions. And about 60% of Mahonia's trades were with just one company:
Enron Corp.
The point of the choreographed trading? People familiar with Mahonia say Enron used the transactions to
manage tax liabilities by transferring losses in one financial reporting period to another. As Enron's troubles
mounted, the Houston company eventually turned to Mahonia as a sort of surrogate bank, these people say,
using it to raise at least $2 billion in financing over the years.
For J.P. Morgan, the arrangement was lucrative -- at least at first. The bank received as much as $100 million
in revenues. It also thought it had insurance in place to cover any default by Enron.
But in the wake of Enron's collapse and bankruptcy-court filing, Mahonia could cost the nation's
second-largest bank as much as $1 billion. Several insurers have alleged in a lawsuit in New York federal
court that the trading transactions were shams, thereby negating the insurance contracts. The bank, now
known as J.P. Morgan Chase & Co., disputes the court allegation. Credit-rating concern Standard & Poor's
cited J.P. Morgan's overall exposure to Enron as one reason it is reviewing the bank's credit rating for a
possible downgrade. J.P. Morgan, which acted as a lender, underwriter and merger adviser to Enron, says
the energy concern owes it a total of $2.6 billion.
The Mahonia arrangement -- which J.P. Morgan hadn't disclosed to investors until last month's suit --
represents just a sliver of the many complicated ventures Enron participated in. But unlike the hundreds of
partnerships Enron constructed on its own to keep debt off its books, this venture was conceived, launched
and operated by J.P. Morgan. Though many Wall Street firms helped finance Enron -- acting as traditional
lenders, underwriters and advisers -- the fact that J.P. Morgan set up the partnership suggests that Wall
Street may have played a more active role in the Enron scandal.
J.P. Morgan won't comment on some key aspects of the dispute, citing pending litigation. A spokesman says
that "many companies routinely raise funds using pre-paid commodity forward contracts. The benefits vary
from client to client, including pricing advantages and diversity of credit sources."
Enron spokesman Mark Palmer says the trades were "perfectly legitimate and proper transactions" made as
part of the normal course of trading commodities.
Many things about the operation remain mysterious. It is unclear, for instance, who owns Mahonia. According
to records from the Jersey Financial Services Commission, the company was incorporated on Dec. 16, 1992.
It has two nominee shareholders, Lively Ltd. and Juris Ltd., who represent undisclosed owners.

--- Page 81 ---
Page 81 of 243 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
"The question is: Was Mahonia a conduit on behalf of Enron or a conduit on behalf of J.P. Morgan?" says
Manfred Knoll, a managing director for Germany's Westdeutsche Landesbank, which issued a $165 million
letter of credit to J.P. Morgan to guarantee against losses. He says Mahonia legally was a conduit of J.P.
Morgan. But in practice, "it was a conduit that was set up to transact a variety of financial transactions for
Enron."
On Tuesday, a New York bankruptcy-court judge ruled that Enron will have to make available documents
relating to Mahonia to the German bank, people familiar with the matter say.
As part of its broad investigation into Enron, the Securities and Exchange Commission is reviewing J.P.
Morgan's multifaceted relationship with Enron, people familiar with the matter say. Among other things,
investigators are examining whether the bank, through vehicles such as Mahonia, helped Enron draw a
misleading financial picture for investors.
People close to the matter say Enron told J.P. Morgan the trades were for tax purposes. Tax experts say it is
common for companies to manage tax liabilities by, for instance, deferring certain losses from a bad year,
when the tax bill might be low, to a future period when they can be used to offset high earnings. There's
nothing inherently illegal about trying to minimize corporate tax bills. Enron hasn't paid corporate income tax
in four of the past five years, a spokesman says.
The Mahonia maneuvers may draw additional scrutiny now, however, in light of admissions by Enron that it
used a series of outside partnerships to hide losses.
Whatever the ultimate goal, the transactions worked like this: J.P. Morgan would pay Enron between $150
million and $250 million for the future delivery of natural gas or crude oil. This was constructed as a "trade,"
not a loan. So Enron would report this as earnings that would cancel out, temporarily, losses on Enron books.
But Enron had to eventually deliver the oil or gas, usually in regular installments with the value of $10 million
to $20 million, the people familiar with Mahonia say. With each delivery, the losses began again to appear on
Enron's ledger. These deliveries would begin the following year, so the losses were carried from one year to
the next, without showing up clearly on Enron's books.
The result: Enron kept those losses in reserve in case Enron had any profit windfall on which it might pay tax,
the people familiar with the matter say. If it did, it would use those losses to cancel out profits, and thus lower
its tax burden. Or if Enron didn't have big profits to hide, it would just roll the losses over again to the next
fiscal year -- by going back to J.P. Morgan and selling it another gas contract. Two tax experts contacted for
this article described the technique as unusual but potentially very effective. "It certainly makes sense as a
tax strategy," says Doug Carmichael, a professor of accounting at New York's Baruch College.
The whole process fed on itself. As one Wall Street banker put it, the arrangements "practically guaranteed"
Enron would come back to J.P. Morgan for more.
What was in it for Morgan? The deals generated, over the decade, fees and interest measuring as much as
$100 million. In paying for future delivery of gas to Mahonia, J.P. Morgan got the gas at a discount --
reflecting the interest rate Enron would have paid were it getting a straightforward loan. In the summer of
1999, this amounted to somewhere between 7% and 8%, or roughly $7 million to $8 million for every $100
million J.P. Morgan channeled to Enron under the Mahonia arrangement. (That revenue, of course, was
offset in part by the bank's funding costs.) The bank often got a small fee for arranging the financing.
The arrangements were for years a source of pride within the bank's small commodities division, which
directed the trades. Dinsa Mehta, one of J.P. Morgan's senior commodity traders, praised the deals to
colleagues, saying that while Enron put out its other commodity financing needs for all of Wall Street to bid
on, Enron kept coming back to J.P. Morgan for trades that would carry its losses forward. Mr. Mehta,
contacted through a spokeswoman, declined to comment.
In a basic way, the trading pact is a throwback. Prepaying for future delivery of a commodity is known as a
"gold trade," because it is the way gold bullion has been trading for centuries. In recent years, trading
companies, whether from Houston or Wall Street, have been making more use of this structure to buy and
sell oil, natural gas and other commodities. Some commercial banks, including Chase Manhattan, a
predecessor of J.P. Morgan, had to set up part of these trades overseas because their banking charters
wouldn't allow them to take delivery of commodities.
J.P. Morgan also bought commodities contracts from a number of other energy companies. Yet by far
Mahonia's biggest customer was Enron, accounting for roughly 60% of its business, people familiar with the
matter say.
Over the years, the size of the transactions grew and the repayment periods stretched out further and further
into the future.

--- Page 82 ---
Page 82 of 243 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Mahonia's business with Enron jumped sharply in 1999. Oil prices were weak, causing concerns over the
future profitability of the energy industry. The stock and bond capital markets had become reluctant to finance
energy companies, leaving J.P. Morgan's offshore arrangements one of the few places this industry could
raise money.
In the summer of 1999, Enron officials contacted Morgan with requests to do bigger and bigger trades,
including a large arrangement of $650 million in one trade. It was a far cry from earlier trades in the range of
$150 million, and suggested to some people within the bank that Enron was no longer merely interested in
tax avoidance, but was actively using the arrangement to meet its financing needs.
J.P. Morgan officials couldn't do the business without hedges. The firm would be on the hook for a large
chunk of cash if Enron defaulted before it delivered the natural gas. These arrangements, after all, presented
the same default risk as any loan to Enron. J.P. Morgan effectively had been paying a portion of its earnings
to other banks in exchange for their guaranteeing portions of the arrangement. This move shifted some of the
risk to other banks like ABN Amro Holding NV or West LB.
It wasn't enough. By this time, companies including Enron wanted to raise more through Mahonia than the
banking syndicate was willing to handle amid the oil-price slump. So Enron, if it wanted more money, needed
to find new players to share the risk of financing the gas payments.
Enron turned to 11 insurance companies -- including National Fire Insurance Co., Safeco Insurance Co., St.
Paul Fire & Marine Insurance Co. and Citigroup Inc.'s Travelers unit -- to issue "surety bonds." These are
financial guarantees insurance companies commonly issue to ensure a project is completed, whether it's a
bridge or Hollywood movie. Enron arranged these contracts for J.P. Morgan -- and paid the insurance
companies for it -- so that the bank would feel more comfortable making increasingly large trades with the
energy company, according to a person familiar with the arrangement.
As Enron's trades grew bigger and bigger, the bank was also financing other energy companies, and the
accounting on these trades became a source of concern within the bank. On Aug. 5, 1999, Vice Chairman
Marc Shapiro and senior credit officer David Pflug convened a meeting in a glass room off the bank's
commodity trading floor.
As part of that briefing, the group went through a lengthy history of the bank's trading with energy companies.
The managers were told one reason companies like Enron were entering the complex trades was to carry
forward losses and lower tax burdens, a person familiar with the briefing said. This person said Mr. Shapiro
reviewed the trades and said they were fine. Mr. Shapiro declines to comment. Mr. Pflug, confirming the
meeting, said it was called to discuss another client and commodity derivative contracts in general.
Two years later, the arrangement was still functioning as Enron's troubles deepened. Both Enron and J.P.
Morgan kept looking for other institutions to share the risk as Enron kept running new trades through
Mahonia.
That's when Enron and Morgan turned to the German bank for more comfort. On Sept. 10, 2001, Enron and
Morgan arranged to obtain a $165 million letter of credit from West LB to guarantee derivatives trades
between Mahonia and Enron North America, according to Mr. Knoll, the bank managing director.
In an unusual move underscoring Morgan's keen interest in the letter of credit, the legal documents were
reviewed by Philip Levy, Morgan's associate general counsel. Mr. Levy didn't return a call seeking comment.
Enron's woes deepened further. After a planned merger with rival Dynegy Inc. fell through, Enron filed for
Chapter 11 bankruptcy protection on Dec. 2. After the filing, Morgan requested to be paid under the letter of
credit, Mr. Knoll says. So far, West LB has refused to pay, depositing the $165 million in an escrow account
which it says it will make available when the Mahonia transactions underlying the lending facility are proved
proper.
It was only after the bankruptcy filing that investors first got a whiff of Mahonia. Morgan's insurers, due to
make a payment on the surety bonds by a Dec. 21 deadline, refused to pay. Morgan sued in New York
federal court. The insurers filed a counterclaim, alleging that Mahonia was a fabrication meant to disguise
loans in the forms of commodity trades.
In court papers, the insurers say they were led to believe the arrangements were meant to "actually supply
natural gas and crude oil by Enron to Mahonia." But the insurers refuse to pay the guarantees because the
arrangements "were not intended to be fulfilled," the insurers' complaint alleges. It adds that Mahonia was a
"mechanism to obtain surety bonds to secure loans to be made to Enron in the guise" of trades.
J.P. Morgan says the insurers' claims are without merit, noting that the surety contracts say the insurance
liability is "absolute and unconditional."

--- Page 83 ---
Page 83 of 243 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The case is pending. But the spat already has dented Morgan's credibility. Morgan Chief Executive Officer
William Harrison called his board shortly after the Enron bankruptcy filing and told them the bank had some
$500 million in unsecured exposure and some other secured exposures, including loans of $400 million
backed by pipeline assets.
But after the insurers refused to honor their commitments on the surety bonds, Mr. Harrison had to hit the
phones again to directors, and raise the number to $2.6 billion -- with roughly $1 billion of the additional
exposure directly related to Mahonia.
Morgan has been defending its position ever since. Last week, the bank reported a fourth-quarter loss of
$332 million, partly because of its exposure to Enron.
Meanwhile, Mr. Shapiro, the vice chairman, asserts that Morgan has known all along the extent of its Enron
vulnerability. "It's not an issue of what we knew," he said late last month "but what was appropriate to
disclose."

U.S. Plans to Unfreeze$217 Million in AssetsOf Afghan Central Bank
U.S. Plans to Unfreeze $217 Million in Assets Of Afghan Central Bank
Dow Jones Newswires
208 words
25 January 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
No Page Citation
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
WASHINGTON -- The U.S. Treasury authorized the Federal Reserve to release $217 million in blocked
assets belonging to the Afghan Central Bank.
About $193 million in gold and $24 million in other assets have been blocked since 1999 under an executive
order affecting funds associated with the Taliban regime in Afghanistan. With the Taliban no longer in power,
the U.S. plans to release the assets to the new Afghan Interim Authority.
The Treasury acted late Wednesday, following a determination by the U.S. secretary of state that the Afghan
Interim Authority has "recognized legitimate authority" to operate the account, the Treasury said.
Earlier this month, a United Nations Security Council panel removed the Afghan Central Bank from its list of
sanctioned entities. "This is how the blocking system was designed to work," said Treasury Secretary Paul
O'Neill. "The blocked assets are held until a recognized regime is in place and the funds can be directed back
to the legitimate holders."
Mr. O'Neill said the Afghan government now will be able to use the released funds to aid its economy and
strengthen the operations of the country's central bank.

U.S., Others Pledge Afghan Help,Despite Fears of Factional Unrest
U.S., Others Pledge Afghan Help, Despite Fears of Factional Unrest
By Neil King Jr.
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
905 words
22 January 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
TOKYO -- The U.S. and other big donors pledged billions of dollars to rebuild Afghanistan despite fears that
the country's fledgling government isn't prepared to handle significant flows of aid money.
Led by large donations from the U.S, the European Union and Japan, the nearly 50 countries gathered here
for a two-day conference said they are prepared to give more than $1.5 billion this year to begin
reconstruction. Longer-term but less-certain pledges are expected to exceed $4 billion, still well below the
$10 billion the United Nations says is needed during the next five years.
Few governments appear ready to sink cash directly into the budget of the month-old Afghan interim
administration, though, despite pleas for money to pay salaries and get ministries up and running. U.N.
Secretary-General Kofi Annan told the gathering that Afghanistan desperately needs an immediate infusion of
$1.3 billion for recovery projects, humanitarian aid and day-to-day expenses. Donors appear to have offered
less than $100 million of direct money to the Afghan government, though firm numbers won't be known until
later today.
For this year, the U.S. offered $296 million, all of which will be directed through humanitarian organizations
working to rebuild the country's shattered farms, roads and schools, and persuading farmers to grow crops
other than opium poppies. U.S. Treasury Secretary Paul O'Neill said the Bush administration is leery of giving
money directly to the Afghan government -- a rare practice for the U.S. government anywhere in the world.
"There is no structure there yet" to properly control monetary flows, he said.
Mr. O'Neill said the Bush administration has tracked down more than $200 million of Afghan assets frozen in
the U.S. -- mostly gold held by the Federal Reserve -- and expects to turn the assets over to the Afghan
central bank soon.
Among the top donors, the countries of the European Union offered slightly less than $500 million for 2002,
while Japan said it would spend $250 million this year on bilateral projects such as agricultural rehabilitation
and the removal of land mines. Saudi Arabia, one of the few Arab states attending the conference, said it is
offering $220 million during three years. Perhaps the day's biggest surprise was Iran, Afghanistan's neighbor
to the west, which pledged $120 million each year for the next five years.
In addition, the World Bank said it is proposing to offer $500 million in long-term, interest-free loans. A
number of other countries, including China, Kuwait and Turkey, pledged smaller amounts.
Hamid Karzai, the interim Afghan prime minister, was the star speaker at the Tokyo assembly. Dressed in a
traditional blue-and-green striped robe, Mr. Karzai laid out an ambitious plan to build a trustworthy
government in Kabul to oversee a "prosperous and secure Afghanistan." He said he plans to establish a
procurement board and financial-monitoring system to handle government contracting, and to hire an auditing
firm to keep an eye on spending. He hopes to have as many as two million children enrolled in schools by the
end of March, he said.
But Mr. Karzai made clear that the country has a long way to go. "I stand before you today as a citizen of a
country that has had nothing but disaster, war, brutality and deprivation against its people for many years," he
told the assembled ministers and delegates.
Several European diplomats, speaking privately, said the EU is prepared to invest heavily in Afghanistan
during coming years, but only if the country proves it can move away from factional fighting and drug
smuggling. "If Afghanistan continues to cultivate poppies, oppress women and allow the country to be run by
warlords, we will think again about our support," said one European official. U.S. officials were less stern in
laying down conditions, but said they would steer assistance to those regions that are cooperative and stable.

--- Page 86 ---
Page 86 of 243 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
"We will not do work in places where warlords are looting," said Andrew Natsios, director of the U.S. Agency
for International Development, which will distribute most of the U.S. aid money for Afghanistan.
The United Nations Development Program and the World Bank have estimated that rebuilding Afghanistan
will require at least $15 billion over 10 years. Far from simply rebuilding roads and providing jobs for a
devastated economy, the international aid effort must stitch together a country deeply divided among rival
warlords. Most important, the interim government in Kabul -- with just five months to go before it is succeeded
by a longer-term administration -- must prove it can provide security, get food to the countryside and pay
government workers.
Several governments said they are reluctant to make longer-range offers until Afghanistan's specific needs
become clearer. There also has been apprehension that some of the pledges made at the conference could
evaporate once Afghanistan's plight loses its prominence. In a brief chat with reporters, however, Mr. Karzai
gave an upbeat appraisal of the day's events. "This is going very well," he said. "I will have good news to
report to the people of Afghanistan."

EUROPE
EU Fears of Biotech
Are Costing It Jobs,
Growth, Report Says
Europe's fears about biotechnology are costing it in terms of jobs, growth and prosperity, the European
Commission says in a strategy paper scheduled to be adopted this week. The report marks a bid by the
European Union's executive body to be more of an advocate in an arena where it often has been perceived
as blocking such deals. For years, the commission has written laws that treated biotech products as a
potential threat, and several more such laws are in the pipeline. Now the panel is saying the 15-nation EU
can no longer afford to heap suspicion on biotechnology as a whole. Environmental groups in Europe have
billed genetically modified foods as "Frankenfoods" and accuse them of polluting the environment. But
biotech inventions also have potential applications in health care, manufacturing and the environment, and
those industries together will amount to a world market exceeding two trillion euros ($1.77 trillion) in 2010,
according to commission estimates.
Most of that revenue looks likely to be generated outside the EU. The biotech industry in the U.S., where
public concerns regarding biotechnology are more muted, produces more than three times the revenue of the
European sector and employs almost three times as many people.
British Appeal Court Upholds Cloning Law
The Court of Appeal upheld on Friday cloning regulations passed by Parliament last year, which made Britain
the first country to specifically authorize human cloning. The decision, which focused on the legal definition of
an embryo, overturned a High Court ruling that invalidated the government-sponsored legislation designed to
regulate human cloning. In a global first, Parliament passed regulations in January 2001 under a 1990 act to
permit cloning to create embryos for stem-cell research. Producing cloned babies remained outlawed.
Germany Seeks Shipment of Tainted Veal
German authorities searched Friday for a consignment of imported veal contaminated with an antibiotic that
can halt production of human blood cells, the federal Ministry of Agriculture and Consumer Protection said. A
mistake in a Dutch laboratory led to Dutch calf meat with the antibiotic chloramphenicol being cleared as safe
and exported to Germany, France and Austria. In Germany, the meat was sold to buyers in the states of
Lower Saxony and North Rhine-Westphalia. Officials in Germany, Denmark, Poland, Romania and Austria
also were tracking down a shipment of animal feed containing fish ingredients from the Netherlands tainted
with chloramphenicol, which is restricted because of a risk it could cause a lethal form of anemia.
Glencore Confirms Joint Talks With Exxon
Swiss minerals and mining firm Glencore International confirmed speculation Friday that it and mining
heavyweights Anglo American and BHP Billiton are in discussions with Exxon Mobil to buy out the U.S. oil
concern's interest in Cerrejon Zona Norte, a coal-mining complex in Colombia. Analysts say the stake could
fetch from $550 million to $600 million. Officials at London-based Anglo and BHP Billiton, an Anglo-Australian
conglomerate, declined to comment. Exxon Mobil would say only that negotiations are continuing.

Powell Promises U.S. Support to Afghans---Secretary Says CommitmentIncludes Urgent Funding,Longer-Term Assistance
International
Powell Promises U.S. Support to Afghans --- Secretary Says Commitment Includes Urgent Funding,
Longer-Term Assistance
By Guy Chazan
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
502 words
18 January 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
A8
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
KABUL, Afghanistan -- U.S. Secretary of State Colin Powell said the U.S. would make a "significant
contribution" toward rebuilding Afghanistan and stay committed to the country well after the military campaign
against Taliban and al Qaeda forces was over.
Speaking after talks in Kabul with Hamid Karzai, the head of the new interim administration, Mr. Powell
promised full U.S. support for Afghanistan as it struggles to recover from more than two decades of conflict.
"We will be with you in this current crisis and in the future," he said.
His visit to Kabul, the first by a U.S. secretary of state since a trip by Henry Kissinger in 1976, took place on
the eve of an international conference in Tokyo, where donors will be asked to pledge billions of dollars in aid
to Afghanistan. The United Nations says it will cost $15 billion over 10 years to mend a country whose
infrastructure has been almost totally destroyed by war.
But officials say Afghanistan can't even wait for the Tokyo meeting, which begins early next week. With no
revenues and central bank reserves looted by retreating Taliban, Mr. Karzai's government is bankrupt, and
owes $70 million to 235,000 civil servants and police who haven't been paid in six months. The U.N. has
launched an emergency appeal for $100 million to keep the administration afloat, but has so far only received
$7 million.
The U.S. has offered to help by unfreezing blocked Taliban assets said to be valued at more than $200
million. Mr. Powell said some of those assets, which include gold reserves, would be made available to the
government "within a matter of days."
Mr. Karzai said the U.S. was now in Afghanistan for the long haul. "[In] all our dealings with the Afghan
people, they ask us: Is the United States committed? Will they stay with us? And now I can tell them `yes',"
he said.
The secretary of state's promises of a long-term commitment will reassure many here who fear the U.S. might
lose interest in Afghanistan once the military campaign is over, as it did after the fight against the Soviets in
the 1980s.
But it is clear U.S. assistance will not go as far as many Afghans would like. The country has been plagued
by lawlessness since the fall of the Taliban, with highway robbery rife on the roads and local warlords with
their own heavily armed militias seizing control of parts of the country. Some government officials would like
to see U.S. troops deployed throughout Afghanistan as part of a multinational security force.
But Mr. Powell said Afghans themselves should take responsibility for law and order. "Ultimate security will
come from the creation of an Afghan national army committed to the new Afghanistan," he said.

Gold Prices Increase, Primarily on Fresh Buying
Commodities
Gold Prices Increase, Primarily on Fresh Buying
By David Bogoslaw OsterDowJones Commodity News
580 words
11 January 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
C11
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Follow-through buying by speculative commodity funds propelled gold prices higher on the Comex division of
the New York Mercantile Exchange, but the gains actually occurred during European trading hours, with
additional profit-taking in New York capping the rise.
After being lifted above $289 in overnight trading, the benchmark February contract opened higher, shuffled
back and forth in the mid $280s and finally settled $3.70 higher at $287.40 a troy ounce.
"One [investment] house with a big bullion operation in London was a big buyer this morning," said Rhona
O'Connell, a market analyst for the World Gold Council in London. "More gold was wanted in the morning fix
than there was on offer. That indicated strength, so it fixed at $287."
The London fix is a daily setting of the benchmark spot gold price by specialist bullion banks.
The spot market ran out of steam, however, after testing $290 within the next hour and a half, apparently
capped by profit-taking, Ms. O'Connell said.
Geneva-based analyst Zahia Chanan at MKS Finance SA confirmed in a market report that producer selling
on the London highs had pressured the market lower.
The fact that gold lease rates haven't changed indicates most interest has been from fresh buying rather than
short covering, or the canceling of bets on lower prices, Ms. O'Connell said. Had the buying been mostly
short covering, lease rates would have eased, as short covering is associated with a rise in lending activity,
she said.
Most participants see the rally as technically-oriented, not based on any change in the supply-and-demand
outlook for gold. Part of that had to do with the market's stabilizing above a cushion of support at $277 an
ounce since the beginning of the year, Ms. O'Connell said.
But other market watchers referenced robust fundamental factors that they saw undergirding the gains.
Iamgold Corp., a Canadian gold-mining company, announced Wednesday it had converted all of its available
U.S. dollar cash holdings, save one month's corporate operating expenses, into gold bullion, as it regards
gold as undervalued at the current price.
Analyst Jim Steel at Refco Inc., New York, said more bullion buying in Asia, particularly in Japan, and rising
safe-haven buying due to the Argentine currency crisis also contributed to gold's stronger tone.
But the assistant vice president of a New York precious-metals trading firm predicted the price would be back
down below $280 if gold follows its pattern of drifting lower after the expiration of Comex options on Friday.
In other commodity markets:
HOGS: Chicago Mercantile Exchange lean hog prices gained amid rising hopes for cash-market demand.
The February contract increased 1.42 cents to 55.90 cents a pound, while the April and May months scored
new contract highs. People in the meat industry and on the CME floor forecast a 70,000 to 80,000-head
Saturday slaughter, which further fueled ideas that processors are running short of supplies.
SOYBEANS: Prices fell at the Chicago Board of Trade as speculative funds sold ahead of Friday's key
estimates from the U.S. Department of Agriculture. The March future dropped seven cents to $4.325 a
bushel. Forecasts of rainfall in southern Brazil and Argentina through the weekend dented the case for
potential yield loss in the region because of drought.

A Corporate Crime Wave?
A Corporate Crime Wave?
By George Gilder
1,317 words
11 January 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
A10
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Crime may have declined in the streets but, by the recent inflammation of the pundits, you would think there
has been an outbreak of corporate criminality. The Internet, communications, and stock-market booms of the
1990s, it seems, were based on a pervasive series of felonious acts. A wide array of businesses, from Global
Crossing to Loral, from General Electric to Enron, artfully inflated the worth of their shares through the
creation of Potemkin businesses. If you believe the news coverage, corporate leaders are racing to despoil,
mulct, defraud, poison, pillage, and ruin their own businesses, their nation's soils and waters, their retirement
funds, and the world economy.
This flood of factitious crimes, this parade of snaffled fat cats and scapegoats, happens every recession.
Rather than ruing economic reverses as effects of public policy errors and miscalculations, public officials turn
the tables and treat bankruptcy and crash as culpable schemes of particular white-collar criminals.
Some of these alleged crime lords are familiar. Gary Winnick was party to a previous potlatch at Drexel
Burnham, where he played a key role in financing the communications infrastructure through MCI,
Telecommunications Inc., Turner broadcasting, and McCaw Cellular. Then he and his associates were
alleged to have ravaged savings and loans by inducing some of them to buy those companies' high-yield
securities before a government ban briefly destroyed their value. Finally Mr. Winnick boldly launched the
world-wide fiber-optic networks of Global Crossing, with, it is implied, the intention of bilking investors and
crashing bandwidth prices in what Fortune calls "perhaps the greatest executive ripoff in the history of
enterprise" but what is better described as the most efficient global buildout in telecom history.
Jeffrey Mohammad Skilling, Osama Ken Lay, Mohammad Atta Gates, Mad Mullah Welch. The names stream
together, the rap sheets blur, but the statistics mount along with the unemployment numbers, the
bankruptcies, the environmental cleanup costs. Behold these corporate predators wrecking vast pension
plans, sowing deadly cancers in the Hudson River, monopolizing the computer market, blighting the steel
industry with dumped scrap, wantonly disrupting energy markets, insidiously subverting the global climate.
Recessions, however, spring chiefly from government mistakes. Compared to these policy errors, crime in the
suites is never a significant factor. That was true of the savings and loan and Mexican crises of the early
1980s and of the global crisis today, reaching from Japan and Indonesia to Turkey and Argentina.
After a decade and a half of favorable policy, from the capital-gains tax cut of 1978 to the general tax
reductions of the 1980s, from the deregulation of transport and communications to the collapse of inflation to
the downfall of global communism, politicians began taking prosperity for granted. They allowed tax rates to
drift back up to record levels. They transformed telecommunications rules into a regulatory sclerosis that
wreaked a telecom depression. They ignored the implications of the global reign of the dollar at a time of
steady dollar appreciation and growing Third World debt. They embarked on a crusade against chemical
industries vital in both war and peace.
In the face of this long siege of policy blunders, politicians today charge Enron with concocting subsidiaries to
conceal debt and self-dealing. But company structure is almost always chiefly a response to kaleidoscopic tax
and regulatory law and the resulting threat of litigation. Launching innovations in arenas as diversely
regulated and taxed as natural gas and broadband communications, Enron inevitably contrived a complex
structure of subsidiaries and financial instruments difficult to explain under Securities and Exchange
Commission rules that require simultaneous disclosure (or, more safely, non-disclosure) to all.
A trading company such as Enron or a long-term infrastructure play such as Global Crossing is no stronger
than the confidence of investors and customers in its solvency. In an environment of SEC-enforced
ignorance, mere rumors of crime and default can bring a company down.
The key to the debacle, though, was the debacle of money. Buffeting the Enron staples of fuels and
bandwidth and afflicting all commodity prices -- from coffee (at an all-time low) to cotton (at 15 year bottoms)

--- Page 94 ---
Page 94 of 243 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
to scrap steel (down 55% in four years) -- deflation crashed the stock market and stifled every large company
and every Third World country with its debt denominated in dollars.
K-Mart, Pacific Gas & Electric, Exodus, Globalstar, Enron, Argentina, Turkey, Indonesia -- all made serious
and unique errors from time to time; all countries and companies do. But what these cases had in common
was heavy debt. Taking 40% of incremental income from investors through tax hikes, and tightening
monetary policy until entrepreneurial debtors have to repay 40% more than they borrowed, is a sure route to
ruin.
When all debtors are afflicted, the scientific method points not to a hunt for particular infractions but to the
isolation of a common source. And that common source is a dangerously deflationary monetary policy in the
U.S.
While the dollar has been surging since 1996 against the deflated yen, the euro, commodities, and gold, the
Federal Reserve adhered to its view that dollars were too numerous and sucked them out of the most fertile
frontiers of the economy. The dollar dutifully rose, and strangled the Third World countries with dollar
denominated debt, the telecom infrastructure projects supported by debt, the car and computer-leasing
companies funded by debt, the farmers, the steel producers, the energy prospectors who rely on debt finance
and a stable standard of value.
Rather than addressing the fundamental problem of deflation, the administration enacted broad protection for
the steel industry, thus spreading the damage to all companies that use steel and all companies dependent
on trade. Rather than lowering tax rates on investments afflicted with deflation, policy makers obsessed about
such rearview figments as "consumer confidence," an utterly meaningless statistic from the demand side.
A supply-side crisis requires the supply-side remedies of a stable currency, lower marginal tax rates, and
deregulation of technology. These measures offer the only way to raise the tax revenues that will be needed
to fund a war against terror and support a wave of retiring workers.
Money is a standard of value, a code for transmitting information about the supply and demand for goods and
services. For a decade, Alan Greenspan seemed to adhere to a fixed standard of value, guided by a price
rule based on gold and commodities. But since 1996, he went astray, citing as policy guides the "irrational
exuberance of the stock markets," the productivity explosion, the Internet bubble. Without guidance from gold,
currency markets lack any objective means to differentiate the "news" (a change in monetary conditions) from
the white noise of a thousand clamorous markets. When the standard of value itself becomes a commodity,
traded like any other on currency markets, the most vital investment information is lost amid the froth.
In essence, Mr. Greenspan blamed business for the errors of government, including his own. He created the
context for the "crime wave." To save his exalted reputation, he must now return to a price rule.
Meanwhile politicians must stop tinkering with the structure of law and regulation under which entrepreneurial
plans play out. Businesses find themselves operating amid the turbulence of constant legal and monetary
change. In extremis, caught in a baffling web of often conflicting bankruptcy, tax, regulatory, and securities
laws, many executives make decisions that in retrospect can be interpreted as incriminating.
Bankruptcy, though, is not a crime but a punishment. Virtually no one plans for its concussive effects or
expects them at all. The real source of the "crime wave" is the undulation of policy and the babel of alibis from
politicians and bureaucrats searching for scapegoats.
---
Mr. Gilder is author of the Gilder Technology Report.
(See related letter: "Letters to the Editor: Corporate Black Sheep Are No `Scapegoats'" -- WSJ Jan. 17, 2002)

EUROPE/AFRICA
Norsk Hydro Agrees
To Buy E.On's VAW
In $2.8 Billion Deal
Norsk Hydro has agreed to buy the VAW unit of E.On for 3.1 billion euros ($2.8 billion) to create the world's
third-largest aluminum producer. With this deal, Norsk Hydro takes the top spot among European aluminum
producers and moves right behind global leaders Alcoa and Alcan. The new company, to be called Hydro
Aluminum, will account for about 5.5% of primary metal production world-wide. It will generate annual
revenue of 10 billion euros and about 1.13 billion euros in earnings before depreciation and amortization.
E.On is an energy and utility group based in Duesseldorf, Germany. The Norwegian government owns about
44% of industrial conglomerate Norsk Hydro. VAW Chief Executive Helmut Burmester said he expects the
new entity to achieve a scale that neither company could on its own, enabling the combined firm to produce a
broader range of products for important clients, especially the auto industry. Mr. Burmester and Hydro
Aluminum CEO Jon-Harald Nilsen will serve as joint chief executives.
South Africa Is Asked to Probe Rand's Fall
Amid mutterings of conspiracies by speculators, the South Africa Chamber of Business submitted a request
to President Thabo Mbeki for an official inquiry into the country's plunging currency. The move follows
accusations last week by the nation's leading companies and business executives against unnamed
institutions that the chamber said had been using "dubious financial methods" to manipulate the rand. The
slide slowed after central bank Gov. Tito Mboweni and Finance Minister Trevor Manuel on Dec. 21
expressed concern about the currency's weakness and pledged to take "remedial measures if warranted."
BT May Become Integrated Broadcaster
BT Group's chairman, Sir Christopher Bland, raised the possibility that BT may reinvent itself as an integrated
television broadcaster to rival British Sky Broadcasting Group. The plan has been cautiously welcomed by
analysts, who have been grasping for a growth element in the stock since BT spun off its mobile-phone
operation mmO2 in November. They see the London company facing ever-tougher competition from
cable-TV concerns, which can offer interactive TV, phone services and broadband Internet access with one
wire and one bill, the so-called "triple play." Analysts like the idea of upgrading the network to allow carrying
high-speed data streams necessary for quality TV images, but moving into making its own TV programs
represents a step in the dark.
U.K. Body Delays Cruise-Merger Decision
The United Kingdom's Office of Fair Trading gave itself more time to weigh the planned merger of P&O
Princess Cruises and Royal Caribbean Cruises, but said investors shouldn't read anything into the move. The
OFT, which had been expected to make its recommendation today on the agreed GBP 4.8 billion ($6.9
billion) union to the U.K. Department of Trade and Industry, extended its consideration period by 15 working
days, to Jan. 29. The planned tie-up announced in November sparked a hostile bid for Princess from
Carnival. Separately, Germany's competition body, the Federal Cartel Office, approved the marriage of
Princess and Royal Caribbean.

ASIA/PACIFIC
Carlyle to Buy Unit of Japanese Retailer
U.S. private-equity firm Carlyle Group has agreed to purchase the security-services division of a big
Japanese retailer, according to a person familiar with the negotiations. The deal, which could be announced
as early as today, is Carlyle's first buyout in Japan, a market that has turned out to be tougher than many
potential investors expected. Carlyle will spend about 3.6 billion yen ($27.5 million) to purchase a 90% stake
in A.S.S., a subsidiary of supermarket-operator Daiei that provides security systems and guard and
cash-transport services, primarily for shops and small businesses. Carlyle also will assume more than five
billion yen of the company's debt, the person said.
Philippine Chip Firm Shares Slide 36%
Philippine semiconductor group Music's stock sank 36% amid market fears its U.S. unit is in difficulty.
Lawyers for Music confirmed in a letter to the Manila stock exchange that an emergency motion was filed in a
California court in late December to bring in an outside trustee to manage the company under Chapter 11 of
the U.S. Bankruptcy Code. The court has appointed an examiner to look into a petition brought by Cadence
Design Systems' Tality unit against Music's U.S. arm, Music Semiconductors, on concerns the parent plans to
transfer its U.S. assets to the Philippines. Music executives weren't available for comment.
Malaysian Carrier Details Recovery Plan
Loss-incurring Malaysian Airline System disclosed a recovery program, involving the sale and leaseback of
6.1 billion ringgit ($1.6 billion) of assets, including eight planes and its headquarters. Buried under eight billion
ringgit in debt, the flagship carrier's plan to get back into the black by 2004 was set back by the slump in
global travel following the Sept. 11 terrorist attacks on the U.S. MAS Managing Director Mohamad Nor Yusof
said MAS would sell 2.2 billion ringgit of properties, including complexes at the Kuala Lumpur International
and Subang airports, to a state special-purpose vehicle to repay borrowings and boost working capital.

Venezuela Production Climbed in October
Venezuela's industrial output in October jumped 16.8% from a year earlier and was up 12% from September,

doesn't include the oil sector, rose 15.1% from a year ago. Caracas has been trying to boost output as a way
to balance the country's reliance on petroleum, which accounts for about 80% of exports and half of
government income. But local industrialists complain they are hampered by an overvalued bolivar that makes
their imports cheaper and exports less competitive.

Quarterly Mutual Funds Review---Some Small-Cap Value Funds Say, `No More Money, Please'---More Investors Want to ShareIn Gains of These...
Quarterly Mutual Funds Review --- Some Small-Cap Value Funds Say, `No More Money, Please' ---
More Investors Want to Share In Gains of These Portfolios, But Doors Are Shutting
By Allison Bisbey Colter
Dow Jones Newswires
2,015 words
7 January 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
R21
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
TWO YEARS AGO, Franklin MicroCap Value co-manager William Lippman was awash in promising stocks.
The bubble in big technology stocks had left plenty of bargains among the tiny, mostly nontech, companies
he follows.
The only problem was that he couldn't buy them. The long-term track record of the fund, then with about $125
million in assets, wasn't so good, and no new money was coming in from investors.
But in the second half of 2000, small value stocks turned the corner and money started pouring in. By the end
of 2001, the fund had nearly tripled in size and Mr. Lippman was having trouble putting the money to work. So
Franklin Templeton Investments, a subsidiary of Franklin Resources Inc., San Mateo, Calif., decided to close
MicroCap Value to new investors once its assets reach $300 million.
Mr. Lippman said there just aren't that many good candidates left among the companies he likes to buy --
those with a market capitalization of less than $300 million that are trading at a discount to book value. "When
you find companies that are below book value, there's frequently a cloud over them, and the question is if it's
a cumulous cloud that will blow away, or is there a storm coming," he said.
If you are thinking of buying a small-cap value fund, you may not want to wait very long. That isn't necessarily
because there is going to be a huge rally for these funds -- they have already outperformed the overall stock
market by a huge amount during the past two years, so you may be too late on that score. Rather, the reason
is that some of the best-performing funds in the group have been closing their doors to new investors.
In addition to Franklin MicroCap Value, Turner Small Cap Value, American Century Small Cap Value,
Wasatch Small Cap Value and Boston Partners Small Cap Value II and State Street Research Aurora funds
have either closed to new investors or announced plans in 2001 to start turning away new money.
Five other funds, Longleaf Partners Small-Cap, Berger Small Cap Value, Dreyfus Small Company, Bridgeway
Ultra-Small Company and Artisan Small Cap Value were closed to new investors prior to 2001.
While almost all money managers face limitations on how much money they can effectively run, small-cap
managers run up against those self-imposed limits faster than others because the stocks they buy are so
illiquid. Most small-cap funds invest in companies with a market capitalization of less than $2 billion, while
those who focus on the micro-cap area of the market tend to stick with stocks that have a capitalization of
less than $500 million.
"There's no optimal level of assets you can apply across the board, but a few hundred million is typically the
maximum for micro-cap funds, while most small-cap funds top out at $1 billion to $2 billion," said Russel
Kinnel, an analyst at Chicago mutual-fund research firm Morningstar Inc.
Small-cap funds that focus on value stocks -- companies that are considered bargains compared with their
sales or profits -- have been hitting their capacity limits especially fast lately because their strong performance
in 2001 attracted big inflows from investors.
The average small-cap value fund was up 16.39% for 2001, according to Lipper Inc., compared with a loss for
the overall market of 11.87% as measured by the Standard & Poor's 500-stock index, including dividends. By
comparison, the average U.S. diversified equity fund fell 10.89% during the same period. (The only category
of mutual funds to perform better than small-cap value was gold funds, which rose 18.78%.)
Small-cap value stocks were out of favor during the go-go 1990s. As long as the entire market was rising,
there was no need to look for bargains in thinly traded stocks; investors could make a lot of money simply by
buying shares of the largest companies.

--- Page 99 ---
Page 99 of 243 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
But as the economy slowed, making big-caps appear increasingly overpriced, investors began to seek refuge
in shares of smaller companies. Because of their size, small companies are perceived as being quicker to
respond to changing market environments. Many of them operate in niche markets that are relatively
insulated from downturns and they tend to benefit from lower interest rates because they don't have as easy
access to financing through the capital markets as bigger companies do.
Strong performance of their stocks -- while always welcome by managers -- can still bring problems in
running portfolios. If investors start pouring money into the fund, the manager must decide whether to let the
cash pile up, buy more of the stocks already in the portfolio or put money in bigger companies or in a larger
number of stocks. All of these approaches have limitations, however, which is why some -- though far from all
-- managers in hot-performing categories resort to at least temporarily closing their funds to new investors.
Wasatch of Salt Lake City, for example, believes in running fairly concentrated portfolios, so spreading the
growing assets of its Small Cap Value fund among a bigger number of stocks wasn't considered an option.
"You can only cover so many stocks at a time," said Jim Larkins, co-manager of the fund, which closed to
new accounts in May when its assets reached $600 million.
However, the fund's managers did give themselves leeway to invest in bigger companies last year. Since Jan.
31, 2001, Wasatch Small Cap Value has invested primarily in companies that had a market cap of less than
$1.5 million at the time of purchase, according to a filing with the Securities and Exchange Commission.
Before then, the fund's stated policy was to invest primarily in companies with a market cap of less than $300
million at time of purchase.
Last year put the Turner Small Cap Value Fund on the map. The fund, launched five years ago by Clover
Capital Management, Pittsford, N.Y. with just $600,000, was acquired in May by Turner Investment Partners,
Berwyn, Pa. The combination of Turner's better-known brand name and a strong track record created
marketable appeal. By year end, assets in the fund and in some parallel separate accounts managed for
wealthy investors had risen nearly 11-fold to around $400 million.
Clover Capital, which continues to manage the fund under a contract with Turner, prefers to diversify the
portfolio across a relatively large number of stocks in a range of industries. For a time, co-manager Lawrence
Creatura accommodated inflows primarily by buying additional stocks, and during the course of the year, the
number of holdings crept up slightly to about 165. The firm has a policy of keeping no more than 5% of assets
in cash, under normal circumstances.
Mr. Creatura said he could run a lot more money -- as much as $1 billion in total -- without negatively
impacting performance. Nevertheless, Turner Small Cap Value was closed to new accounts early as a favor
to existing investors, many of whom contribute through their 401(k) plans.
With small-cap value funds continuing to do well, are more fund closings in the category likely to follow?
Perhaps, but some in the group also are holding out.
While most of its growth in assets has been through the rising value of its portfolio, the $1.8 billion T. Rowe
Price Small Cap Value Fund, for one, has already blown past the asset level at which it has previously closed
to new investors. Manager Preston Athey has bought more shares of existing holdings, allowed cash in the
portfolio to rise to 7% from 4% a year ago, and increased the number of stocks in the portfolio to 230 from
220. He also may consider buying slightly bigger companies. "I'm still at a market cap well below the Russell
2000, but instead of saying I'll never buy a stock with a market cap of over $500 million, now I may buy one
with a market cap of up to $600 million," he said. The T. Rowe Price Small Cap Value Fund, which Lipper
classifies as a small-cap core fund because it holds a mix of value and growth stocks, was up 21.84% in
2001.
While acknowledging that "there will be a point at which it's too big," Mr. Athey is confident of his ability to
keep up with new money being added to his portfolio at the moment. Although he believes the small-cap rally
still has some way to go, the relative appeal of small value stocks could fade if the economy picks up and
stocks of small growth companies take the lead over small bargain stocks.
At more than $11 billion in assets, Fidelity Low Priced Stock portfolio is by far the biggest small-cap value
fund, and it last closed in 1998 when assets were pretty close to where they are now. The fund's girth didn't
prove to be an impediment last year as it gained 26.71%.
The secret: While most of Fidelity Investments' research staff is deployed along industry lines, the Boston
fund company decided three years ago to devote several analysts to following smaller companies on a
full-time basis. Tapping these additional resources allowed Manager Joel Tillinghast to spread assets among
more stocks than the typical fund.
Fidelity Low Priced Stock held a whopping 765 names as of Sept. 30, but at 16%, the fund also had a fairly
high level of cash. The fund took in in more than $3 billion in new money in 2001, according to Financial

--- Page 100 ---
Page 100 of 243 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Research Corp. Still, Fidelity says it has no plans to close Low Priced Stock, although a spokesman said the
fund's size is reviewed regularly.
The $297 million Third Avenue Small Cap Value Fund might also seem like a good candidate to close if only
because manager Curtis Jensen has been carrying a lot of cash -- 27% as of Sept. 30. Although the market
rout after Sept. 11 allowed him to go on a buying spree, the fund has continued to take in new money. Now
that valuations are no longer as cheap as they were two months ago, he has let the cash build back up again.
Mr. Jensen, whose fund was up 15.26% last year, has no problem with big cash positions in principle. In fact,
he thinks small-cap funds need extra flexibility because the stocks they invest in are so illiquid. Normally, the
fund's cash level runs between 8% and 10% of assets.
While the fund's cash holdings currently are much higher, the manager says it is premature to conclude that
he won't be able to put the money to work. "On one hand, it's a nice cushion for the portfolio; the other side of
the coin is that to the extent we do get pricing dislocations, I've got a nice war chest," Mr. Jensen said.
There is also a raft of tiny small-cap value funds that for whatever reason -- a low profile, small advertising
budget or short track record -- haven't attracted much new money from investors this year despite strong
returns. The $45.8 million Aegis Value Fund rose 42.66%; the $62.5 million JohnsonFamily Small Cap Value
rose 29.20%; the $13.0 million CornerCap Small Cap Value rose 23.55%; the Kinetics Small Cap
Opportunities fund rose 30.4%; and the $8.8 million-in-assets TCW Galileo Small Cap Value I rose 20.14%.
But don't jump in just because you expect a big return. A fund's performance often slackens just after it
closes. That is because funds usually close after a big rally, and it is natural for performance then to ebb.
Document j000000020020107dy170000n

--- Page 101 ---
Page 101 of 243 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Quarterly Mutual Funds Review---Strategies of Winning Funds Pay Off Amid Troubling Times---One Victor's Rise Is FueledBy Energy and Oil S...
Quarterly Mutual Funds Review --- Strategies of Winning Funds Pay Off Amid Troubling Times --- One
Victor's Rise Is Fueled By Energy and Oil Stocks; Avoiding `Void of Hope'
By Kara Scannell
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
1,355 words
7 January 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
R3
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
For stock investors, the new millennium hasn't been a picnic.
The recession that arrived in 2001 spoiled corporate earnings and battered the stock market. The Sept. 11
terrorist attacks dimmed the picture further.
While stocks soon rallied to reduce their full-year declines, it wasn't enough to offset the earlier losses,
leaving major indexes in the red for the year. The outlook for 2002 isn't great, either. While many
prognosticators expect the economy to pull out of the recession by midyear, there's little expectation that the
market will return to the double-digit gains of the '90s bull market.
How mutual-fund managers have coped with these tough conditions can offer lessons for individual investors.
Each quarter, The Wall Street Journal conducts a competition for the best-performing mutual funds in the
latest one-year, five-year and 10-year periods. The competition -- this one for periods ended Dec. 31 --
requires that the funds have the same portfolio manager for the entire period for which they are in contention.
All results are based on data provided by fund-tracker Morningstar Inc., Chicago.
Black gold greased the wheels of the best-performing mutual fund for the latest 12-month period. The Pilgrim
Russia Fund, managed by Samuel Oubadia and Michiel Bootsma, soared 80.3%. With about 51% of its $57
million in assets in energy and oil stocks, the fund gained on the rise in crude-oil prices for much of last year
before oil prices retreated.
Among its best-performing stocks were shares of Yukos Holdings, an oil exploration and production company
that soared 205% last year. Mr. Bootsma, 29 years old, said they took profits in the stock as it climbed, but
bought more before the company announced in November that it was doubling its dividend rate.
Financial reporting is still a challenge in many emerging markets and more so in Russia, making it tougher to
find good companies to invest in. But conditions are improving, according to Mr. Oubadia, 37. "Management
is becoming more transparent and more open to shareholders in terms of letting them know their strategy and
timeliness and accuracy of financial reporting," he said.
Because so few stocks listed on the Russian exchange are liquid enough for the fund firm to trade in and out
of, the managers tend to hold on to investments with a long-term outlook. Also, with much of the Russian
stock market dominated by oil and utility firms, there isn't much room to shift assets around if the sectors fall
out of favor.
But given the uncertain outlook for oil prices, Mr. Bootsma said he likes fixed-line telephone companies
because of the coming government-sponsored consolidation among the many small regional companies into
super regional firms.
Richard Lane and Glenn Primack like to invest in companies that are trading below what a competitor might
pay to acquire it or that could benefit by some catalyst or event. That investment philosophy helped their $295
million FMI Focus Fund win the trophy for best-performing fund in the five-year category with a 35%
annualized return since its inception in December 1996.
Among the fund's biggest winners was an investment made just a few weeks after its launch. Mr. Lane
bought shares of Quest Diagnostics Inc., a clinical laboratory that had been spun off by Corning Inc. He liked
the management team and thought the company would benefit from the aging population.
Mr. Lane started buying shares of Quest when it was trading around $13 a share. Over the years they sold
stock, but again increased their position when Quest acquired the lab-testing business from SmithKline
Beecham PLC, now GlaxoSmithKline PLC. Shares of Quest finished last year at $71.70.

--- Page 102 ---
Page 102 of 243 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Mr. Lane, 46, said the fund's motto is simple: Avoid the void of hope. "If you're hoping things get better, they
probably won't," he said. And conversely, the managers believe it's important to take profits when the
valuation of a company no longer makes sense. They sold a lot of technology stocks in 1999, in some cases
missing out on future gains but also avoiding many pitfalls.
As a result, the fund's turnover of shares is high at 165% for the fiscal year ended Sept. 30, though that's
lower than previous years. Mr. Lane says he tries to do well by his investors and offset gains with sales of
stock toward the end of the year to minimize taxes. His interests are aligned with the fund: He has much of
his net worth in the fund and is also managing money for his friends and relatives.
As a "focus" fund, it is allowed by its charter to concentrate its investments more than a diversified mutual
fund. At one point more than 50% of the fund's assets were invested in financial stocks. Before the terrorist
attacks, the fund had 20% of its assets parked in cash, but after, when stocks across all industries were
pummeled, the investment opportunity became wide open, so Mr. Lane began putting some of the fund's
cash to work. For the first time in years, Mr. Lane bought stock in two airlines: Midway Airlines and Alaska Air
Group. He has since sold half his stake in Alaska Air after it rebounded to preattack levels, and still owns
Midway stock.
Mr. Lane also shifted much of the fund into financial stocks -- anticipating still lower interest rates --
insurance companies, select technology names and industrial concerns. "We felt that the valuations were too
compelling to pass up," said Mr. Lane. The fund's insurance holdings ballooned to 10% of the fund, nearly
double the fund's preattack levels, but insurance is now back to about 4% of assets. Prior to the attacks,
technology stocks made up about 19% of assets, then rose to 30% in late November and now represent
about 24% of assets.
Mr. Lane said they keep tabs on stocks they like but think are too expensive. For instance, they bought
shares of Aspen Technology Inc., a maker of software designed to manage factory production, when it dipped
below $10 a share, under the industry average of three- to seven-times revenue.
For 2001, FMI Focus Fund had a return of just under 3%, compared with a 29.3% loss at the end of the third
quarter and besting the Dow Jones Industrial Average, which lost 7.1% last year. The key, said Mr. Primack,
37, was that "we continued to look forward instead of looking at present day and getting bummed."
Diversification and a couple of stand-out winning stock picks last year helped propell Samuel D. Isaly to a
20.8% annualized return over the past decade, making the $948 million Eaton Vance Worldwide Health
Sciences Fund the best-performing fund in the 10-year category.
Mr. Isaly, who was superstitious about discussing his performance until the final numbers were tallied Friday,
has managed the fund since 1989.
He said he takes a contrarian approach to investing. He bought shares of Immunex Corp, maker of drugs for
autoimmune disorders and cancer, after it faced a setback with the clinical trials of its blockbuster arthritis
drug Enbrel in March. The stock price tumbled, so Mr. Isaly, 56, began buying shares at an average price of
$13.55. Last month, Immunex agreed to be acquired by biotech firm Amgen Inc. for about $29.15 a share.
The big gains in Immunex, which make up 4.5% of the fund's assets, offset some less-than-stellar bets. The
fund lost about 70% on its small investment in chip-maker Caliper Technologies Corp., which was hurt when
big pharmaceutical firms cut back on research and development budgets.
About 65% of the fund's assets are invested in U.S. pharmaceutical and biotech companies with the
remainder in European and Asia-based firms.

Economic Forecasters Expect Moderate Recovery in 2002---Wesbury Led a GroupOf Bears Who CalledThe Recession of 2001
Economy
Economic Forecasters Expect Moderate Recovery in 2002 --- Wesbury Led a Group Of Bears Who
Called The Recession of 2001
By Jon E. Hilsenrath
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
1,215 words
4 January 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
It was a rough year to be an economic forecaster. Few economists anticipated a full-fledged recession. Fewer
still expected the aggressive response of the Federal Reserve that sent short-term interest rates to their
lowest level in 40 years. Then the Sept. 11 terror attacks occurred, forcing everyone to revise their forecasts
all over again.
But for a small group of bearish economists, everything seems to have gone as expected, leading them to the
top spots in The Wall Street Journal's economic-forecasting survey for 2001.
Atop the pack was Brian Wesbury, chief economist at Griffin, Kubik, Stephens, & Thompson, a Chicago
investment bank that specializes in fixed-income securities. Long before the National Bureau of Economic
Research finally declared last month that the U.S. had fallen into recession in March, Mr. Wesbury had
factored recession into his forecasts. He called for meager economic growth in the first half of 2001 and then
a contraction by the third quarter. As it turns out, he was right on. The Department of Commerce reported that
gross domestic product, the value of the nation's output, adjusted for inflation, grew at an annual rate of
1.3% in the first quarter and 0.3% in the second quarter, then contracted 1.3% in the third quarter.
Mr. Wesbury was followed by several other bears, all of whom called for slow growth or a moderate
contraction during the first nine months of the year. Among them, Tracy Herrick with Jefferies & Co., a Los
Angeles-based institutional trading company, was especially sharp with his GDP forecasts. He predicted a
contraction in third-quarter GDP back in December 2000.
Not every economic bear finished on top. One perennial bear, A. Gary Shilling of Shilling & Co. in Springfield,
N.J., finished in the back of the pack, because he overshot his recession call, projecting a 3% contraction in
the first half of 2001. Mr. Shilling also called for recessions in 1999 and 2000.
Finishing dead last this year was Gail Fosler, chief economist at the Conference Board in New York. Ms.
Fosler topped The Wall Street Journal's economic-forecasting survey twice in 1999 for her bullish predictions
about the economy at a time when growth was surging. But she, like other bulls, underestimated how much
the economy would slow in 2001. Ms. Fosler acknowledges she was "uniformly too optimistic about" 2001,
and says the big surprise was how severely bank lending to businesses dried up last year. That corporate
credit crunch, she says, hampered business activity, causing the economy to unravel much faster than she
expected.
To pick this year's winner, The Wall Street Journal looked at average growth forecasts for the first nine
months of the year, based on January and June surveys. Economists were also ranked based on their June
predictions for interest rates and exchange rates through year's end, and inflation and unemployment rates
through November. Economists also were asked another question back in June: Was the economy already in
a recession? While the NBER had not yet declared a recession, two key economic indicators, payroll
employment and industrial production, had started to fall, signaling that the sharp economic slowdown might
actually be something worse. Yet only nine of 52 economists surveyed said that a recession had started.
Nearly two in five said the chances of a recession were still less than 30%.
"The whole year has actually been a nightmare for economists," says James Bianco, founder of Bianco
Research LLC, who did his own study of 19 years of Wall Street Journal economic surveys and found
economists failed to predict the right direction of long-term interest rates 71% of the time. Even Mr. Wesbury
was off on interest rates. Like many economists, Mr. Wesbury greatly underestimated the degree to which
the Federal Reserve would ease short-term interest rates. Mr. Wesbury expected the bond-equivalent yield
on three-month Treasury bills to end the year at 3.45%. Instead, yields on bills ended much lower, at 1.69%.
He was a little low on his forecast for the 10-year Treasury note, calling for a 4.75% yield when it finished at

--- Page 104 ---
Page 104 of 243 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
5.02%. But he was close to the mark by projecting a sharp weakening in the Japanese yen to 135 against the
dollar; it finished at 132.
"Economic forecasting is like hitting in baseball. Just hitting some of the pitches is good," says Mr. Wesbury,
who has been taking swings at the economy since he took an introductory economics class as an
undergraduate student at the University of Montana in 1978. He was a fast study, winning a six-month
subscription to Business Week magazine for accurately predicting the direction of interest rates, gold prices,
the stock market and inflation during that class.
More recently, he served a brief stint as the chief economist for the Joint Economic Committee of Congress in
1995 and 1996, when the government ground to a halt amid budget battles between Congress and the
Clinton administration.
Mr. Wesbury says his main clue for 2001 was the sharp upward movement in inflation-adjusted interest
rates in mid-2000. When the federal-funds rate, controlled by the Federal Reserve Board, went near 4.5%
after adjusting for inflation in 2000, he says he knew the economy was heading for trouble. The last time the
inflation-adjusted federal funds rate was that high was just before the 1990 recession. Such a high rate
implies that companies are having a harder time obtaining credit.
Today, the inflation-adjusted federal-funds rate is close to zero, thanks to 11 interest-rate cuts in 2001. Mr.
Wesbury says that implies that a recovery is on the way. But he doesn't think it will be a strong one.
Corporate profits have fallen so sharply and manufacturers have so much excess capacity, he believes that
investment in both inventories and equipment will be slow to recover in 2002, holding back the rebound.
Longer term, though, he is among the biggest bulls in the survey. Mr. Wesbury was one of only five
economists in the poll to say the nation's potential for long-term productivity growth is greater than 3%. Most
see it between 1.5% and 3%. Alan Greenspan, chairman of the Federal Reserve Board, has said that robust
productivity growth was one of the key underpinnings for the economic boom of the 1990s.
"We're going to come out of this relatively slowly, but the economy will build momentum behind it," says Mr.
Wesbury. He sees the economy contracting in the first quarter, but reaching a 3.8% annualized growth rate in
the fourth quarter, which is still below the late 1990s' rate.
