Afghan Finances,As They Are, PoseProblem for Minister
International
Afghan Finances, As They Are, Pose Problem for Minister
By Guy Chazan
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
458 words
4 January 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
A6
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
KABUL, Afghanistan -- Hedayat Amin-Arsala has one of the toughest jobs in finance: trying to balance the
books in a country with no taxes, no exports and no budget.
As finance minister of Afghanistan's new interim administration, he serves a government so badly stretched it
can't pay its own staff, let alone rebuild an economy shattered by 22 years of war. "We basically don't have
any resources to speak of," says Mr. Amin-Arsala, a 59-year-old former World Bank economist. "We have
very little reserves, and our revenue streams have all dried up. We're in really bad shape."
There are few gold reserves to speak of and no functioning banks. Taxes aren't being paid, and customs
duties are pocketed by local warlords. The government owes $66 million to civil servants, some of whom
haven't been paid for six months.
"We're going to be dependent on international support for even our current budget," says Mr. Amin-Arsala.
Even setting up his office would have been impossible without outside help: The United Nations supplied Mr.
Amin-Arsala's desk, computer and satellite phone.
The national currency, the Afghani, is also in crisis. No exports mean no hard-currency revenues to back up
the Afghani. Yet the presses have been churning out notes, fueling inflation. One official says the previous
administration of Northern Alliance leader Burhanuddin Rabbani printed 300 billion Afghanis a month.
Even the strengthening of the Afghani since the new government came to power on Dec. 22 -- from 70,000 to
$1 to 25,000 now -- is causing trouble, by swelling dollar-denominated debts.
Many of Mr. Amin-Arsala's woes are the result of Taliban mismanagement. The Islamic militia cared little for
fiscal policy: Its war budget was a tin trunk stuffed with money and stashed under the bed of leader Mullah
Mohammed Omar. Most revenue came from illegal opium exports and smuggling. At the Taliban-run finance
ministry, mullahs educated at madrassas, or traditional Islamic schools, replaced qualified economists and
bankers.
Mr. Amin-Arsala hopes the new regime will provide enough stability to revive long-dormant plans to build
roads and export pipelines across Afghanistan, binding it more tightly into the regional economy.
Much speculation has centered on California-based Unocal Corp., which shelved plans for a gas pipeline
linking Turkmenistan and Pakistan across Afghan territory after the U.S. launched a 1998 cruise-missile
attack against Osama bin Laden's terrorist training camps in Afghanistan. Mr. Amin-Arsala says he's heard
nothing so far from Unocal. But, he adds: "I'm sure they'll turn up."
Document j000000020020104dy140000e

--- Page 106 ---
Page 106 of 243 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Vital Signs:A Few Economic CuesShould Show WhenRecession Is Ending---Watch if Costly Homes Sell,Profit Warnings Decrease,Smelters Start S...
Vital Signs: A Few Economic Cues Should Show When Recession Is Ending --- Watch if Costly Homes
Sell, Profit Warnings Decrease, Smelters Start Smelting --- The Gary Condit Indicator
By Greg Ip
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
2,157 words
4 January 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
The economy could be near a turning point.
After slipping into recession early last year, then taking another hit Sept. 11, it is showing signs of healing,
with the worst economic fears from the terror attack unrealized.
It's too soon to declare that a recovery is under way. Some bits of evidence suggest it is while others show
the opposite, such as a just-reported jump in new claims for unemployment benefits.
But the picture is always muddy at turning points. This slump is a particularly tough one to read because it's
peculiar, with consumer spending holding up unusually well.
When the economy does begin a comeback, there will be telltale signs. Here are six to watch for:
When businesses start investing again, especially in technology.
When Manugistics Group Inc., a maker of supply-chain-management software, two weeks ago reported a big
loss for the Nov. 30 quarter, investors drove its stock up sharply. Not only was the loss narrower than
analysts had expected, but company executives said they were more confident about their order backlog.
Manugistics had closed some big contracts and was being invited to bid on more deals. "It's not just people
talking about buying software out there," Greg Owens, chief executive of the Rockville, Md., company told
investors and analysts. "They're actually signing contracts and buying software."
A downturn in business, not consumer, spending has driven this recession. Since the recession began in
March, consumer spending has yet to show a single quarter of decline. Consumption might fall in the current
quarter, says Peter Hooper, chief U.S. economist at Deutsche Bank, but offsetting that, "the negative trend in
business spending is easing significantly."
One sign of an upturn is fresh spending on pricey software applications such as Manugisitics' since they were
among the first things deferred by strapped corporations, says Steve Milunovich, technology strategist at
Merrill Lynch & Co.
Orders for high-tech equipment, which plunged more than 50% between May 2000 and last September, rose
in both October and November. Price increases on semiconductor chips are sticking. "That's what's different
from April, when we had a rally in the stocks and fundamentals weren't bottoming," says Mr. Milunovich. "Now
they are."
High-tech spending may be stabilizing simply because companies have to buy more just to replace
equipment that's wearing out or becoming obsolete. Other areas of investment are still weak. Ford Motor Co.
yesterday reported a 2% gain in December auto sales from a year ago. But within that, fleet sales were down
23%, says George Pipas, Ford's manager of sales analysis. "No amount of incentives can make [a rental
company] need more cars than they have business travelers arriving at the airport," he notes.
Spending on networking telecommunications equipment, pummeled by the collapse of upstart dot-com and
telecom companies, is still tumbling. But the "telecom spending bust is almost over," predicts Sanford C.
Bernstein & Co. analyst Paul Sagawa, who was among the first analysts to predict the bust. Big phone
companies, exlcuding wireless, have succeeded in chopping capital spending to about 15% of their sales,
historically the lowest it gets before "the bottom of a spending cycle."
When companies start warning of better, instead of worse, profits.

--- Page 107 ---
Page 107 of 243 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Back in October, Goodrich Corp. announced that the slowing economy and the terror attacks' effect on
aerospace would force it to cut thousands of jobs, close plants and record profits below analysts'
expectations. On Dec. 13, the company had somewhat better news: Although the jobs still weren't coming
back, profits would be better than analysts now expected.
Goodrich thus joined a faint but potentially significant trend: a possible end to the corporate profit gloom that
has seen margins shrink by a third from their peak in 1997. The margin squeeze has been a major factor
behind cuts in jobs and capital spending. Figuring that analysts' estimates give a quick read on what
corporate executives are anticipating, Fed Chairman Alan Greenspan watches analysts' changing estimates
of profits closely for clues on when the capital spending slump may end.
About 25% of corporate preannouncements of fourth-quarter earnings say results will be better than
expected, rather than worse or the same. That's not much, but it's better than the 17% recorded in the
previous quarter.
"In the last three weeks, it appears there may be a start of a deceleration in the rate of warnings," says
Charles Hill, director of research at Thomson Financial/First Call. The picture is still grim. Mr. Hill thinks that
earnings in the current quarter will still be down about 16% from a year earlier. But that would be an
improvement from the year-to-year earnings decline he estimates for the fourth quarter, which is 22%.
One encouraging phenomenon: A few companies that were warning of worse results a few months ago are
doing the opposite now. Goodrich, a Charlotte, N.C., company once known for tires but now primarily in
aerospace, had twice warned of lower profits last year. But in December, it said that despite cutbacks from
manufacturing customers, its services and spare-parts businesses were doing better than it anticipated right
after Sept. 11. Similarly, Cendant Corp., the travel, real-estate and financial-services company, cut its
fourth-quarter profit estimates shortly after the Sept. 11 attacks, then raised them twice.
To be sure, many are still issuing profit warnings, among them Ford, Gap Inc., Gannett Co., Lucent
Technologies Inc., McDonald's Corp. and American Express Co., according to First Call. But Mr. Hill thinks if
the signs of stabilization hold, the second quarter of this year could mark the beginning of an upswing in
profits.
When stores and dealers start complaining inventories are too low.
This recession has been driven partly by companies curtailing production so they could fill sales by drawing
down inventories. This is despite much past speculation by economists and Fed officials that better
management of inventories would diminish their role in economic cycles. In fact, inventories have played as
big a role in this slump as in past ones, if not bigger.
In the fourth quarter, James Glassman, economist at J.P. Morgan Chase, estimates inventories fell at a 7% to
8% rate, the steepest in decades. They're now at the lowest level relative to overall sales on record. At some
point, this will force companies to increase production, putting people back to work.
That moment may be close. CNW Marketing Research Inc., in Bandon, Ore., estimates that at least 70,000
auto sales didn't occur in December because dealers didn't have the stock, forcing customers to wait until this
year for the cars they wanted.
Soaring sales of cars and light trucks in the past few months, amid zero-percentage-rate financing, left Bryant
Henrie, vice president of Larry H. Miller Automotive in Murray, Utah, with too few of some popular models. "In
Chevrolet, we're out," he says. At his five General Motors dealerships, stocks of extended-cab Silverado
pickups and big Suburban and Tahoe sport-utility vehicles are down to about a 15-day supply from 90 days a
year ago. "You're in danger when you get too low because you're missing sales," he says.
Though car makers' December sales didn't match the incentive-driven November pace, Mr. Glassman thinks
production will rise in the next few months to replenish inventories, giving the economy a fillip. Ford's Mr.
Pipas concurs. In one week of January 2001, 18 of Fords' 22 North American plants were idle. Next week,
they're all scheduled to be up. "I think we'll have a much more stable operating pattern in 2002 because the
inventory correction is behind us," Mr. Pipas says.
Of course, before companies actually begin to add to inventories, they want to see sales picking up. Outside
of the car industry, signs of that remain scant. Lazard analyst Todd Slater estimates Gap has slashed
inventory per square foot by 15% in its latest quarter from a year earlier, but sales per square foot are down
even more, an estimated 20%. Even chains with better sales performance aren't about to start bulking up on
inventories, Mr. Slater says. "They will wait for business to improve before they turn on the spigot."
When sales of high-end homes strengthen in high-tech hotbeds.

--- Page 108 ---
Page 108 of 243 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The bear market has been a major depressant on consumers, whose stock wealth had helped finance a lot of
spending during the late 1990s boom. One of the most pronounced impacts was on the price of luxury
houses in places like Seattle, Silicon Valley and Austin, Texas, hotspots of the Nasdaq boom and bust.
Elsewhere, the housing market has stayed strong, buoyed in part by low mortgage rates.
The stock market, though still 27% below its early 2000 peak, has risen 21% since its Sept. 21 trough. That
hasn't triggered a new boom in conspicuous consumption, but it may be putting a floor under sales of
high-end homes.
In Seattle, sales of such homes have been sluggish since April 2000, when the dot-com bubble burst, says J.
Lennox Scott, president of John L. Scott Real Estate. But he is beginning to see signs of a rebound. In
October, Mr. Scott says, sales were concentrated in homes priced below $350,000. In December, strength
shifted to those priced at as much as $450,000.
"It's not the euphoric wealth effect we saw during the dot-com bubble, but a moderate wealth effect that is
going to be driven by the improving stock market," Mr. Scott says. "We're through the low ebb of the cycle,
and the stock market will anticipate the turnaround in the economy."
In Austin, at the height of the tech-stock craze "our sales were 80% high-tech and 20% Old Economy," such
as doctors, lawyers and small-business owners, says Douglas Clark, vice president of Russell Eppright
Custom Homes. "That's flipped now. We're in a kind of normal, pre-2000 type of market."
When aluminum smelters switch to making aluminum again.
It seems a distant memory, but just a year ago the economy was pummeled by soaring prices for electricity,
natural gas and gasoline. The recession helped reverse most of those spikes. Energy costs are back to
early-2000 levels, though a recent agreement by OPEC to trim oil output suggests more relief isn't in store.
One symptom of the energy squeeze was that early last year, many aluminum smelters in the Northwest
agreed to shut down and furlough their employees. The plants were better off if they resold the electricity they
had agreed to buy from Bonneville Power Administration than if they used it to make aluminum. This made
sense when electricity was over $500 per megawatt hour on the spot market, as it was in December 2000,
says Peter Moritzburke, an analyst for Cambridge Energy Research Associates. Now it's about $30, not much
above what the smelters need to be profitable making aluminum.
A resumption of aluminum production would be a bullish sign for the economy. But it isn't happening yet.
Bonneville and the smelters could renegotiate contracts so that the smelters can reopen, but that isn't
imminent, primarily because of the weakness in demand for aluminum as a result of weak global growth.
"None of those we have an agreement with have indicated they're starting up any time soon," a Bonneville
spokesman says.
When Gary Condit returns to the headlines.
Before Sept. 11, the news media were preoccupied with the California congressman and the disappearance
of an intern, Chandra Levy, he had befriended. It was a sign of how little some Americans had to worry about.
The attack on the U.S. changed all that. Now the war in Afghanistan has turned decisively in the U.S. favor,
consumer concerns about terrorism are easing, and, perhaps as a consequence, hotels' occupancy rates and
airlines' passenger loads are improving. The number of people who consider another terror attack "very likely"
fell to 23% in an Associated Press poll in late December from 48% in October, though 47% still think one
"somewhat likely."
The world isn't back to where it was. Even a police raid on O.J. Simpson's home in connection with a drug
investigation in early December barely made a splash. When celebrity gossip crowds out headlines about
layoffs, bomb threats on airplanes and anthrax in mail-sorting machines, it will be a sign that consumer
confidence is on the mend and with it the U.S. economy.

Judge Allows Drug Rebates In Florida Law
Judge Allows Drug Rebates In Florida Law
By Russell Gold
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
692 words
3 January 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
A3
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
A federal judge in Tallahassee let stand a Florida law that seeks rebates from drug makers in exchange for
inclusion on a list of preferred Medicaid drugs.
The ruling, a major setback for the pharmaceutical industry, is likely to prompt other states to follow the lead
of Florida, which passed the law in an effort to restrain its sharply rising drug spending. Already, Michigan has
implemented a program partly modeled on Florida's.
"As the legal hurdles melt away, I think more and more states will pay attention" to Florida, said Greg Vadner,
the Missouri Medicaid director and vice chairman of the National Association of State Medicaid Directors.
"The precedent appears to be leaning our way," he said, referring to Friday's ruling.
The Pharmaceutical Research and Manufacturers of America, a Washington-based trade group known as
PhRMA, had brought the suit to block the Florida initiative. PhRMA, which represents the nation's
brand-name prescription drug manufacturers, is planning to appeal the case to the Eleventh U.S. Circuit
Court of Appeals in Atlanta.
At stake is a portion of the $25 billion states spend on prescription drugs each year through Medicaid, a joint
federal-state program to provide health care to the poor and disabled.
Faced with tight budgets and double-digit inflation in pharmaceutical costs, states have been looking for
ways to control expenditures. In turn, this has prompted PhRMA to use statehouse lobbying and an
aggressive legal strategy to quell such efforts.
In May, Florida lawmakers approved an innovative effort to slow Medicaid spending increases by creating a
list of preferred drugs. To get on the list, manufacturers had to offer the state a 10% supplemental rebate on
top of a federal rebate, which averages 15.1%. If a drug isn't on the list, doctors must get verbal authorization
from a phone bank of pharmacists and pharmacy technicians before the prescription can be filled.
This added inconvenience discourages doctors from prescribing drugs not on the list and shifts patients
toward the preferred drugs. For example, after the preferred list went into effect, the market share for Imitrex,
GlaxoSmithKline PLC's popular antimigraine drug that isn't on the list, dropped to 6% from 60%. The share of
Merck & Co.'s Maxalt, an antimigraine drug that is on the list, rose to 89% from 16%, according to a
consultant hired by PhRMA. As a result of the supplemental rebates, the state expects to save at least $100
million this fiscal year.
Pfizer Inc. and Bristol-Myers Squibb Co., both based in New York, cut deals with Florida to get all of their
drugs on the list without discounts. Instead, they created programs to improve the health of chronically ill
patients and guaranteed cost savings to the state. Despite this favored status, the companies, both of which
are members of PhRMA, say they support the goals of the lawsuit.
PhRMA filed the suit in August in U.S. District Court in northern Florida, claiming the state's law broke a
federal rule that all prescription drugs be available to Medicaid recipients unless the drug offers no clinically
meaningful benefit. The court disagreed, finding that Florida's list steered doctors and patients toward certain
preferred drugs, but didn't prevent access to nonpreferred drugs, which would be illegal under federal law.
Jan Faiks, PhRMA's assistant general counsel, said in a statement that she disagreed with the judge's ruling
and worried that the Florida law would strip Medicaid recipients of access to needed drugs and "could
seriously harm the health of these patients."
Mark Striker, a pharmaceutical analyst at Salomon Smith Barney in New York, says the case was an
important part of the "pharmaceutical industry's attempt to slow state momentum" to obtain price discounts. In
another closely watched case, PhRMA is suing to block a similar preferred drug list in Michigan. Details of the

--- Page 110 ---
Page 110 of 243 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Michigan program were finalized last month. That case is filed in state district court in Lansing, Mich. A
hearing is set for later this month.

THE AMERICAS
Mexico Approves
Budget, With Blow
To Fox's Tax Plan
Mexico's congress approved a 2002 budget, but only after dealing President Vicente Fox a blow by passing a
watered-down version of his tax-reform initiative. After a marathon session that ended in the morning hours of
the new year, lawmakers approved a spending bill for this year at about 1.45 trillion pesos ($159 billion), up
slightly from 2001. The spending level holds to Mexico City's target of a budget shortfall of 0.65% of the
country's total economic output. Final approval of the budget was held up by wrangling over new taxes, which
include levies on some telephone and Internet services, as well as cigarettes and soft drinks made with
high-fructose corn syrup, a move to help the nation's ailing sugar industry. The changes, aimed at boosting
Mexico's paltry tax revenue, are seen adding about 60 billion pesos to government coffers, about half the
levels the administration wanted in its original proposal. The most controversial part of Mr. Fox's initial plan,
extending a 15% value-added tax to food and medicine, was dumped by opposition parties amid a public
outcry. Economists said the smaller measures were unlikely to persuade ratings agency Standard & Poor's to
grant Mexico a long-coveted "investment" grade rating on its foreign debt.
Pembina Completes Alberta Oil Purchase
Pembina Pipeline Income Fund's Pembina Pipeline unit has completed the acquisition of Alberta Oil Sands
Pipeline from a subsidiary of Alberta Energy for 225 million Canadian dollars (US$141 million). Pembina said
the purchase price may be adjusted for working capital and other items. Some of the money is being used to
pay off existing pipeline debt. It used some of the proceeds from its debenture and share financing to pay for
the acquisition. For the rest, it used a new C$255 million syndicated credit facility arranged with a Canadian
chartered bank. Pembina said the acquisition diversifies its pipeline interests and will boost stable long-term
returns.
Ecuador's Joblessness Averages 10.9%
Ecuador's unemployment rate averaged 10.9% in 2001, up from 9% the previous year, the National Statistics
and Census Institute said. But underemployment, the percentage of people who are employed in the informal
sector or work on an occasional basis, averaged 57.3%, down from 65.9% in 2000. Separately, the Andean
nation's gross domestic product fell 0.1% in the third quarter of 2001 compared with the previous period,
according to a central bank report. GDP rose 1.2% in the second quarter.
Oil Law Goes Into Effect in Venezuela
President Hugo Chavez celebrated a contentious new petroleum law that went into effect, telling supporters
that Venezuela is beginning the New Year with a "new oil strategy." The Hydrocarbons Law gives the state
greater control over the petroleum industry, an initiative Mr. Chavez argues will allow Caracas to better
spread the nation's oil wealth among the poor. Critics say the law will alienate investors by imposing the
world's highest royalty rates on companies operating the country's petroleum fields, of 30%, from 16.7%.
Mexico Hails OPEC Decision to Cut Output
Mexico welcomed OPEC's decision to lower oil production and reaffirmed it would join the effort to bolster
world prices by reducing its own exports by 100,000 barrels per day in the first half of 2002. The energy
ministry said it was confident the supply cuts by the Organization of Petroleum Exporting Countries and major

--- Page 112 ---
Page 112 of 243 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
non-OPEC producers "will achieve a healthy balance between supply and demand, which will allow a halt in
the fall of prices and assure the necessary investments to maintain a sure and reliable supply of oil."
Separately, Russia cut its crude-oil exports by 150,000 barrels a day as of yesterday, Itar-Tass reported. The
Russian cabinet of ministers believes the per-barrel price range of $20 to $25 would be acceptable for the
country.

Normandy Tells Holders to Take No Action
Normandy Mining advised its shareholders Monday to take no action regarding rival offers by South Africa's
AngloGold and Denver-based Newmont Mining. Normandy said it will provide further advice to holders on
both bids by the end of the week and indicated it still is awaiting further details from AngloGold on its revised
offer announced last week that included references to a global partnership with Canada's Barrick Gold. Last

Faster MAS Restructuring Is Sought
Malaysian Prime Minister Mahathir Mohamad has appointed his chief economic troubleshooter, Nor
Mohamed Yackop, to accelerate the restructuring of ailing state-controlled Malaysian Airline System, or MAS.
Mr. Nor Mohamed's assignment, government officials say, includes speeding up sales of MAS assets and
trimming the carrier's bloated work force, measures officials and bankers say have been stalled by squabbles
among MAS's directors and senior managers. Officials say Dr. Mahathir, fed up with the slow pace of

Mohamed to oversee a rescue plan for the carrier, which is struggling under 9.2 billion ringgit ($2.42 billion) in
debt. Mr. Nor Mohamed couldn't be reached for comment.
China Raises Foreign Banks' Deposit Cap
China has issued new regulations governing the operations of foreign banks in the nation, incorporating
existing business practices and changes in line with WTO commitments, state media said. Foreign banks had
been keenly awaiting details on how the country would implement World Trade Organization pledges, eager
to tap the huge pool of domestic savings held mainly at state banks. Foreign banks in China will be allowed to
take foreign-currency deposits totaling as much as 70% of their foreign-exchange assets, from an earlier cap
of 40%.

EUROPE/MIDEAST
Israel's GDP Contracted 0.5% Last Year

--- Page 113 ---
Page 113 of 243 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Israeli gross domestic product fell a preliminary 0.5% in 2001 after growing 6.4% the prior year, taking a triple
blow from the U.S. recession, collapse of the global technology market and political violence, the Central
Bureau of Statistics said. The contraction, the first for the Israeli economy since a 1.4% drop in 1953, was
largely because of the Sept. 11 terrorist attacks in the U.S., which bureau officials said reversed output
growth. The bureau previously had forecast 0.5% expansion in GDP for last year. The preliminary figures for
2001 are based on data for the first nine to 11 months of the year.
U.K. IPOs Saw Worst Quarter in Decade
The United Kingdom market for initial public offerings ended 2001 with its worst quarter in more than 10 years
after an extremely tough year, a survey published by KPMG Corporate Finance indicated. According to the
study, GBP 184 million ($268 million) was raised in the U.K. in the fourth period last year, down 93% from a
year earlier. During all of 2001, GBP 9.9 billion was raised by new issues, down 7% from the prior year. A
total of 78 companies, including venture-capital and investment trusts, listed on the London Stock Exchange
in 2001. Of these, 57 raised money, but only nine were trading firms. In 2000, 138 concerns joined the bourse
with 119 of the businesses raising funds, and 72 of these were trading companies.
Czech Republic's Total Foreign Debt Rises
The Czech Republic's total amount of foreign debt increased to $21.9 billion at the end of the first nine
months of 2001 from $20 billion a year earlier, the Czech National Bank said. The data are preliminary. The
total now represents 39% of gross domestic product. The central bank cited continued heavy foreign direct
investment in the nation as the major reason for the widening of the net investment deficit position for the
year measured.

The Scattered Case Against an Alleged Terror Accomplice---U.S. Says Youssef Nada AidedAl Qaeda, but It Will BeVery Difficult to Prove It
International
The Scattered Case Against an Alleged Terror Accomplice --- U.S. Says Youssef Nada Aided Al Qaeda,
but It Will Be Very Difficult to Prove It
By Roger Thurow
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
1,406 words
27 December 2001
The Wall Street Journal
J
A4
English
(Copyright (c) 2001, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
LUGANO, Switzerland -- As Swiss police raided the office of Youssef Nada on the morning of Nov. 7, they
pushed through a door emblazoned with this bold, red-lettered sign: "Ask yourself!!! Are you the problem? . . .
If you know the answer COME IN."
Later that day, President Bush identified Mr. Nada as part of the problem, saying his Al Taqwa constellation
of companies moved money for Osama bin Laden's al Qaeda terrorist network. The U.S. hasn't file charges
against Mr. Nada, but hopes Swiss authorities will. Swiss investigators, though, are still struggling to put the
puzzle together.
The pieces are widely scattered. The Swiss raid netted documents from a raft of business activities around
the globe, as well as Mr. Nada's phone index, which he says has more than 10,000 business contacts. During
a simultaneous search of Mr. Nada's house in an Italian enclave within Switzerland, Italian police found a
photo of Mr. Nada and Iraqi leader Saddam Hussein. In a third, coordinated raid, Liechtenstein authorities
discovered a nest of companies stashed behind the nameplate of a local trust company. In the Bahamas, the
troubles of now-defunct Bank Al Taqwa came under renewed scrutiny.
What it all adds up to, if anything, is still shrouded by the fog of the war on terrorism and fragmented by
separate national investigations that leave each country with a piece of the puzzle and no one with a
complete picture.
The Swiss, for example, say they didn't provide any information to prompt Mr. Bush's Nov. 7 comments. Nor,
they say, do they know what the U.S. has to back them up. "Mr. Bush is the president of the United States. I
don't know who writes his speeches," says Valentin Roschacher, the Swiss attorney general. "I don't know
what all information and intelligence he has, and how it is valued by the courts." Still, he adds: "It would be
fatal if one country has information that could be helpful but doesn't share it."
"The opinion of President Bush isn't of interest for the Swiss criminal investigation," says Claude Nicati, the
Swiss prosecutor on the case. "Our job is to find criminal elements if they exist. If we have no elements we
have to end the investigation." Says Liechtenstein's chief prosecutor, Robert Wallner, whose own puzzle
pieces include a few paper companies: "What we'll never be able to establish without help from other
countries is the connection to terrorism."
In the middle of all these investigations is Mr. Nada, a 70-year-old Egyptian-born businessman who got his
start in international trade shipping oranges, tomatoes and peanuts to Europe. From his mansion high above
Lake Lugano, he proclaims his innocence. "People can say anything they want about me, but not this, that I
am involved in terrorism," he says. "It's an injustice. If they had something on us, we should be arrested."
So far, his bank accounts, and those of several companies fingered by the U.S., have been frozen in various
countries. U.S. intelligence officials, who provide few details, say much of their evidence comes from
intelligence intercepts that they say were damning enough to put Al Taqwa on the White House target list for
financial sanctions. And, apparently, enough to place Mr. Nada on an immigration watch list of people
suspected of terrorist links. He says that in December 1999, he was prevented from entering the U.S. when
he stopped in Atlanta on the way to the Bahamas.
But the Swiss, who also had intelligence reports raising suspicions about the Al Taqwa network over the
years, say those reports aren't necessarily enough to prove a criminal case here. Indeed, Mr. Nada argues
that he is a victim of circumstance, that his is a case of guilt by association.

--- Page 115 ---
Page 115 of 243 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
But the circumstances and associations are plentiful. Take, for instance, the picture of Mr. Nada with Saddam
Hussein. "They found the crime!" Mr. Nada says sarcastically. "Everyone knows I was [in Iraq] to convince
Saddam to pull out of Kuwait."
Mr. Nada speaks openly of being a member of the Egyptian Muslim Brotherhood since he was 17, and he
grows animated when recounting how he was rounded up by Egyptian police and held for two years with
other Brotherhood members in the mid-1950s. He volunteers that the Brotherhood was banned in Egypt
decades ago, but notes that it isn't on the U.S. list of terrorist organizations. He says it has nothing to do with
al Qaeda.
U.S. intelligence officials cite Bank Al Taqwa's links to Yusuf Qaradawi of Qatar, spiritual leader of the Muslim
Brotherhood who has publicly praised Palestinian suicide bombings against Israeli civilians. Mr. Nada says he
has known Mr. Qaradawi for decades, and adds that he heads the bank's Islamic legal panel, which reviews
investments to assure they comply with Islamic business practices. "But does this mean I'm a terrorist?" Mr.
Nada asks.
Mr. Nada's name has also surfaced in various intelligence reports and media stories in Europe and the Middle
East over the years linking his companies with groups throughout the Muslim world, including Hamas, the
militant Palestinian faction. Two of his close business associates have been involved in Islamic centers in
Munich and Milan that German and Italian officials have been investigating.
Mr. Nada dismisses the significance of those connections. "At first they say I'm supporting Hamas and the
Palestinians," he says. "Then they jump to Algeria, and the Jamaa Islamiya and Jihad in Egypt. Then they
jump to bin Laden and the Taliban. As if I'm sitting on a hill of gold and financing everyone."
Still, in Lugano, Mr. Nada is closing down the well-appointed offices of Nada Management Organization,
formerly known as Al Taqwa Management. Pictures and mementos of his business activities in cement, steel
and grain hang on the walls. Seventeen people once worked here, he says; on a recent afternoon, he is
alone.
This office was the administrative and internal auditing center for Al Taqwa companies, including the
Bahamas-registered bank. Mr. Nada says the company here received about $1.7 million a year from the bank
for these services, which included feasibility studies on the bank's investments. This money, he says, went to
pay the expenses of the Lugano office.
At the same time he was opening this office in the late 1980s, he was also founding Bank Al Taqwa in
Nassau, which attracted more than $200 million from shareholders and investors, including, he says, six
members of the vast bin Laden family. The bank, he says, became the repository of all of his businesses and
operated as a mudarabah, which he describes as a kind of Islamic mutual fund.
By the end of the 1990s, the businesses were doing badly. Mr. Nada says the bank was hit hard by Asia's
1997-98 financial crisis. At the same time, investors and shareholders began pulling out after European
media reports linked Al Taqwa to Hamas and other extremist organizations. In April of this year, the Bahamas
Central Bank revoked Bank Al Taqwa's license. Mr. Nada says he had already begun to liquidate the bank
by that time and had relinquished the license.
Although the Lugano company is closing, the Bahamas bank has no license, and some of the Liechtenstein
holdings seem long dormant, the very constellation of these companies in once-secretive money havens has
piqued investigators' suspicions. Mr. Nada, in turn, echoes the question on his office door: Is this a problem?
"Why shouldn't I operate in the Bahamas and Liechtenstein and Switzerland? Only others can do this, and
not me?," he asks. "Are they all bad, or just me?"
---
Glenn R. Simpson in Washington contributed to this article.

Argentina's Beleaguered Government Collapses---President de la Rua Resigns,Unable to Quell ViolenceFueled by Finance Crisis
International
Argentina's Beleaguered Government Collapses --- President de la Rua Resigns, Unable to Quell
Violence Fueled by Finance Crisis
By Michelle Wallin and Pamela Druckerman
Staff Reporters of The Wall Street Journal
2,117 words
21 December 2001
The Wall Street Journal
J
A9
English
(Copyright (c) 2001, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
BUENOS AIRES -- Argentine President Fernando de la Rua resigned amid deadly rioting and the collapse of
his embattled government, in the grim denouement of a four-year-long struggle with recession, crushing debt
and political paralysis.
Buenos Aires now appears increasingly likely to abandon its one-to-one currency peg with the U.S. dollar and
default on much of its $130 billion debt, economists said. Meantime, well-heeled lenders like the U.S. and the
International Monetary Fund said they were in no mood to help, as Latin America's third-largest economy now
faces a pitched battle not only on the streets, but also with angry foreign bondholders looking at stiff losses.
The damage, however, appears to be limited largely to Argentines and those bondholders, many of whom
have acted to cap their exposure to Argentina in recent months. While Latin American currencies, for
instance, slipped on the news of Mr. de la Rua's departure, they did so only modestly. The currency of
neighboring Brazil, in fact, is near a six-month high. The U.S. dollar rose on world markets, as many investors
tend to view the currency as a haven in times of economic turmoil.
U.S. Treasury Secretary Paul O'Neill brushed aside any suggestion of economic contagion from Argentina,
which he described as "insolvent," saying events in the nation of 37 million had been building toward crisis for
months and markets had largely discounted the expected implosion.
One foreign venue where the Argentine crisis is a bit more palpable: the floor of the Madrid Stock Exchange.
Marquee Spanish firms have plowed more than $30 billion into Argentina over the past decade. The local
share prices of Spain's five largest companies -- banks Banco Bilbao Vizcaya Argentaria SA and Banco
Santander Central Hispano SA, electricity producer Endesa SA, oil company Repsol-YPF SA and telephone
operator Telefonica SA -- have fallen precipitously this year because of Argentina's woes.
Power in Buenos Aires now appears likely to shift to the opposition Peronist party, whose standard-bearer,
the flamboyant former president Carlos Menem, deepened the budget profligacy that put Argentina on
economic tenterhooks in the first place.
Just who will succeed Mr. de la Rua isn't immediately clear. But one question the Peronists may ask
themselves as they prepare to retake power: Do they really want it? The incoming president faces a passel of
unpleasant economic policy options, from currency devaluation to debt default, not to mention a punishing
recession that has put nearly one out of every five Argentines out of work.
Dollarization -- the outright adoption of the greenback -- has been proposed. Tiny Ecuador most recently has
given it a go, and it has helped tame inflation and bring down sky-high interest rates there. By contrast,
Argentina has allowed its pesos to circulate alongside dollars, leaving doubts in the minds of investors as to
whether the one-to-one peg would always hold.
Yet adopting the dollar and junking the peso printing presses wouldn't solve Argentina's most immediate
problem: the need to stoke confidence in lenders and its own people. They had been emptying their bank
accounts for weeks, in favor of stuffing their mattresses, before the government imposed harsh bank
withdrawal limits at the beginning of the month, now somewhat eased.
Besides, say analysts, the government might not have enough U.S. currency in its vaults to dollarize right
now. When Ecuador dollarized, barges loaded with U.S. coins had to steam south to meet the demand.
Currency devaluation, meanwhile, is unlikely to offer the kind of impetus to exports that it did for Mexico in
1995. That's because Argentina sits far removed from big consumers in Europe and North America and is

--- Page 118 ---
Page 118 of 243 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
notoriously poor at selling its goods abroad. The country exports a feeble 8% of its total economic output.
Only a few countries, like Rwanda, Burundi and Haiti, export less as a percentage of gross domestic product.
If Argentina runs too low on the hard-currency reserves, such as dollars and gold, that are the backbone of
its peg with the dollar, it might have no choice but to let the peso float freely against other currencies. That
would almost certainly cause a sharp devaluation in the peso. Allowing the peso to devalue against the dollar,
as Brazil did in 1999, would be especially painful for ordinary Argentines. Most people earn money in pesos
but carry mortgages and car and business loans in dollars, so their debts would instantly become more
expensive to repay after a devaluation.
The government's own debt crisis would be compounded by a devaluation, since almost all of its $95 billion in
bonds are denominated in U.S. or European currencies. Even short of a devaluation, bondholders say, the
chances of a full-fledged default on debt have risen exponentially, since the government will find it politically
difficult to pay interest to its foreign investors while Argentines are rioting for food.
In the streets, angry citizens demanded change. Mounted police wielding truncheons charged protesters --
ordinary Argentines beleaguered by the economy's meltdown and by controls on their bank accounts
imposed just as Christmas approaches. At least 22 people were killed in two days of violence and more than
2,000 arrested. Water cannons and tear gas dispersed thousands of protesters in the leafy capital.
Similar protests over the past few days appear to be what finally pushed the headstrong economy minister,
Domingo Cavallo, to resign late Wednesday. (It was also on Wednesday that Mr. de la Rua declared a
30-day state of emergency on Wednesday that suspends a broad range of constitutional guarantees,
including freedom of travel, the press and labor organization.) The 55-year-old, Harvard-trained economist
took the reins of Argentina's economy in March, coasting on his reputation for having created the dollar-peso
peg in 1991 during a previous stint as economy minister, saving a grateful people from the ravages of
hyperinflation. Suddenly, the government could no longer print pesos at will, and inflation was limited.
But his newer prescriptions, including a blend of tax increases and cuts in public-sector wages, failed to
reverse the country's deep slump. The popular frustration came to a head after he put a partial freeze on
withdrawals of deposits this month to stop a run on the banks and then declared that the government would
make additional budget cuts to gain access to loans from the IMF, which had required fiscal discipline as a
condition of its lending.
With Mr. Cavallo gone, economists say his currency peg isn't likely to last, either. "While Cavallo was in
power, we knew that he was dead set against devaluation and would default only if he simply ran out of
money," said Lawrence Krohn, chief Latin American economist at ING Barings. "Now all of that has changed."
Argentina recently lightened its debt load by pressuring local investors to turn in their bonds for new debt with
lower interest rates and a longer payment period. It has been planning a separate swap for bonds held
abroad. Even before the social upheaval intensified this week, Argentine officials had been hinting that the
foreigners would probably get worse terms that the locals.
"I would not be surprised at all" to see a cessation of payments, said Hans Hume, a fund manager at Van Eck
Investments, which holds Argentine bonds. "I anticipate that this is going to be a much nastier and drawn-out
process than most bondholders expect."
Fitch Ratings Inc. said it now expects a "broad and disorderly" default on all of Argentina's bonds, in which
creditors will have to accept at least a 50% reduction in the principal amount of the bonds. The default would
be triggered if Argentina misses any of the $600 million in debt payments still due this month. The biggest
hurdle is whether investors will buy $451 million in short-term treasury bills set for next week. Governments
sell such debt to fund their needs.
A bond restructuring would affect a wide swath of foreign investors, ranging from U.S. hedge funds and
insurance companies to a Dutch metalworkers group. Of the total, roughly $18 billion is owned by U.S.
investors, estimates economist Christian Stracke of Commerzbank Securities Inc.
Among the firms that say they have small positions in Argentine debt are MetLife Inc., John Hancock
Financial Services Inc. and the Teachers Insurance and Annuity Association College Retirement Equities
Fund, better known as TIAA-CREF.
About $23 billion of Argentina's bonds are denominated in European currencies and held in Europe. Around
half of this is stashed in the private accounts of small, individual investors, Mr. Stracke estimates. The rest is
held by firms like Mn Services NV of the Netherlands, which invests for the metalworkers. An additional $4.5
billion of Argentine debt is held by Japanese investors.
For now, the bond restructuring will take a back seat to the political battles in Buenos Aires. "It really should
be the least of the country's worries until they get the social and political situation under control," said James

--- Page 119 ---
Page 119 of 243 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Barrineau, an economist at Alliance Capital Management Inc., which recently liquidated its large holdings of
Argentine bonds.
As thousands gathered outside the presidential palace calling for his resignation, Mr. de la Rua made a
last-ditch appeal for his political opponents to join a coalition government that would fill the vacuum left by the
resignation of Mr. Cavallo.
But leaders of the Peronist party, which controls both houses of Congress, rejected that offer out of hand.
Congress must now meet to accept Mr. de la Rua's resignation, but that move, expected today, is largely a
formality.
Leaders of the Peronists had lashed out at Mr. de la Rua throughout the day, flatly rejecting his offer to form a
coalition government. "Either the president changes or we have to change presidents," said Sen. Eduardo
Duhalde, the former governor of Buenos Aires state, who lost to Mr. de la Rua in the 1999 presidential race.
Another opposition leader, Cordoba Gov. Jose Manuel de la Sota, added: "It's clear that de la Rua is
incapable of governing . . . the country is in a state of anarchy."
Mr. de la Sota is seen as one potential presidential contender. And though Mr. Menem's power within the
Peronist party has diminished, he too has his eye on the presidential palace. The 71-year-old was recently
released from house arrest, after arms-trafficking charges against him were dropped.
As Mr. Menem's dubious ambitions suggest, the question of presidential succession will likely be a prickly
one. Gregorio Badeni, a professor of constitutional law at the University of Buenos Aires, said the constitution
specifies that the head of the Senate -- currently Ramon Puerta, a Peronist -- would assume power for the
time being.
Within 48 hours, Congress would then have to call a special joint session to elect a new president to serve
out Mr. de la Rua's term. Mr. Badeni said only sitting deputies, senators and provincial governors are eligible
to serve as interim president.
Mr. Puerta, 50 years old, is a successful tea magnate from the northern province of Misiones. He became
head of the Senate earlier in the month after the Peronists scored big victories in October midterm elections.
He served as governor of Misiones from 1991 to 1999, during which time he privatized more than a dozen
state companies, invested heavily in public works projects and reduced public payrolls.
Peronists yesterday said they preferred calling elections to choose the next president, although a decision on
how to handle Mr. De la Rua's succession will be made by Congress over the next several days. "The next
president must be the product of popular support," said Humberto Roggero, a Peronist deputy. Elections,
though, cost time and money -- commodities in increasingly short supply.
Meantime, the Argentine central bank said it declared today a bank holiday, severely limiting financial
transactions. In the past, bank holidays meant that all banks closed their doors and the government often
took drastic economic measures, such as seizure of deposits.
But the central bank said in a statement that banks would be open for limited transactions: the payment of
pensions, withdrawals from accounts within limits established at the start of the month and the payment of
bills and taxes.
Before resigning, Mr. de la Rua put key areas of the economy under the control of his cheif of staff, Chrystian
Colombo.

EUROPE
Britain Can Detain
Foreign Suspects
For Indefinite Period
Britain's law-enforcement authorities won new powers to jail foreign terrorist suspects at a time when the
country is braced for the possibility of attacks from Islamic militants. The act, which took effect Friday,
authorizes police to detain indefinitely and without trial any noncitizen of the United Kingdom who is
suspected of terrorism but can't be deported because of fears he would be executed or tortured in his home
country. Home Secretary David Blunkett has said he expects the detention power to be applied to a couple of
dozen people now in the U.K., but a Home Office spokeswoman said Sunday that no one had yet been
arrested under the act.
Meanwhile, the Observer newspaper reported yesterday that it had found plans indicating the al Qaeda
terrorist group was considering an attack in London. The plans, the newspaper said, were found at an al
Qaeda compound in Kandahar, Afghanistan. They were written in a notebook in fluent English and gave
step-by-step instructions to assemble a large bomb of the kind used against U.S. embassies in Kenya and
Tanzania in 1998. The bomb was to be loaded in a van and detonated in London's financial district, the
Observer said. The Home Office spokeswoman said authorities expect to receive the notebook and will
investigate it. She said security in London has been increased, as it always is around the holidays.
Brandes Builds 6.3% Stake in Zurich
Brandes Investment Partners, a U.S.-based asset manager, has built a 6.3% voting stake in Zurich Financial
Services, the Swiss insurance group said Friday. Analysts interpreted the stake, which according to Thomson
Financial data made Brandes Zurich's biggest shareholder, as a financial investment rather than a precursor
of a strategic approach or an attempt to meddle with management. Brandes specializes in buying companies
it thinks are undervalued and selling them when market sentiment turns.
Banco Bilbao CEO Expected to Step Down
Chief Executive Pedro Luis Uriarte of Spain's Banco Bilbao Vizcaya Argentaria is expected to step down,
possibly as early as Tuesday. Mr. Uriarte decided to resign after deteriorating market conditions forced the
bank last month to cut profit projections and cancel a three-year growth plan due to be completed in 2002,
said people familiar with the situation. His departure, coinciding with the scheduled retirement of
Co-Chairman Emilio Ybarra, will leave Francisco Gonzalez as sole chairman. Mr. Uriarte will be succeeded
by Jose Ignacio Goirigolzarri, 47 years old, currently director-general of Banco Bilbao's retail banking in Spain
and Latin America.
IMF, Turkey Reach Preliminary Loan Deal
The International Monetary Fund and Turkey reached preliminary agreement Friday on new loans to back a
government austerity program aimed at ending the country's economic crisis, the IMF said. An "agreement in
principle" was reached on a three-year loan program to start in 2002. The deal would help close a financing
gap projected at $10 billion through the end of 2002. To secure new loans, Turkey's government must speed
up structural reforms before the IMF's executive board clears the deal, which will probably occur in January.

Canadian National Railway chief executive Paul M. Tellier called for a public debate in Canada on the

Mexico's Aggregate Demand Falls 3.2%
Mexico's aggregate demand for goods and services fell 3.2% in the third quarter, pressured by lower
government spending and weak capital investment. Aggregate demand comprises private consumption, fixed
capital formation, total exports and government consumption. The Finance Ministry said in a news release

the only segment to show growth in the period. Exports fell 8.2% in the third quarter, pulled down by
segments such as maquiladora manufacturing plants, mining, crude oil and natural gas.

EUROPE/MIDEAST/AFRICA
Reserves in Russia
Fall Over $1 Billion
For Another Week
Russia's central-bank reserves fell by more than $1 billion for the second week in a row, to $36.1 billion,
weakening the ruble and perplexing the market, which had grown accustomed to watching Russian reserves
soar. The bank gave no reason for the fall, leaving analysts to speculate. Some said the central bank had
spent the money to offset a recent rise in ruble-selling prompted by seasonal factors and uncertainty about
the Russian economy amid lower oil prices. Others said Moscow might have used the funds to make an
external debt payment. Analysts said they weren't overly worried by the drop but would be if the trend
persists. The last time Russian reserves fell by more than $1 billion in a week was August 1998, the
beginning of the nation's financial crash.
Bank reserves in the last week of November slid by $1.3 billion as Russia paid off a Eurobond and in the first
week of December fell by an additional $1.2 billion.
EU Ministers Agree on Three Directives
European Union finance ministers agreed on rules concerning market abuse, international accounting
standards and collateral, an EU spokesman said. The accord came during an economic and finance ministers
meeting in Brussels. The three rules are part of the EU's Financial Services Action Plan, which was endorsed
by EU leaders in March 2000 and again in June 2001. Passage of the plan, which aims to create a single
market for financial services by 2005, is essential for the development of the EU's internal market and for the
region's competitiveness in the financial-services market, which is responsible for about 6% of gross domestic
product and represents about 2.5% of employment.
Payments for Iraqi Invasion Are Approved
A United Nations panel approved $132.7 million in compensation payments to people and businesses that
suffered losses as a result of Iraq's 1990 invasion of Kuwait. The U.N. Compensation Commission
recommended that about $57 million of this should go to non-Kuwaiti corporations; Kuwaiti companies are
being treated separately. It said nearly $76 million should be distributed to individuals who lost more than
$100,000; those claiming smaller amounts already have been compensated. The awards are funded through
the U.N. oil-for-food program.
AngloGold Vows to Fight for Normandy
South Africa's AngloGold isn't giving up the fight for Australia's largest gold miner, Normandy Mining, despite
rival Newmont Mining's higher offer. AngloGold said it plans to appeal the dismissal of its Australian legal
challenge against Newmont's bid and warned it may take similar action in Canada. But with no sign of
AngloGold's sweetening its offer, the move appears a last-ditch attempt to unravel the pact between
Newmont and Normandy's largest shareholder, Canada's Franco-Nevada Mining. Newmont, of Denver,
raised its paper-and-cash bid Monday to 1.90 Australian dollars (99 U.S. cents) a share, valuing Normandy at
more than A$4.2 billion. The offer's per-share value has since fallen to about A$1.83; AngloGold's bid is
A$1.70.

Taiwan delayed a critical decision on whether to allow its semiconductor industry to invest in China,

China's Productivity Cited in Report
China needs to focus on boosting the economy's productivity and lowering barriers to the service sector in
order to create jobs for millions of new workers, a World Bank report says. The nation needs to create from
eight million to nine million new jobs annually for the next decade, as its population swells and workers leave
farms and failing state firms. But economic growth is set to slow from the 8% average of recent years to 6%
to 6.5% a year, just as global competition will increase with China's entry into the World Trade Organization.
Separately, the country's urban unemployment rate could jump to 4.5% next year, the largest rise in more
than five years.
Pakistan Secures Pact With Paris Club
Pakistan secured an agreement with the Paris Club of bilateral creditors to reschedule $12.5 billion of its
government-to-government debt in the next 38 years. The nation's concessional debt, about two-thirds of the
$12.5 billion, will be rescheduled over the full 38 years, including a 15-year grace period. The remaining
one-third of guaranteed commercial debt will be repaid over 23 years, including five years' grace.

NEW YORK -- Drug stocks moved lower on hesitation about a recovery in profits after Merck reduced its
earnings forecast, but the broad market kept its firm tone following a widely anticipated decision from the
Federal Reserve on interest rates.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average finished the session down 33.08 points, or 0.33%, at 9888.37. But the
whole of that decline came as a consequence of a steep falloff in shares of Merck, which dropped $6.29, or
9.4%, to $60.70, after the drug maker, hosting its annual business briefing, reduced its earnings outlook for
2001. In fact, absent the loss from Merck, the Dow industrial average would have finished the day 10.44
points in the black.
With worries about the profit forecast from a bellwether company, a number of other stocks in the
pharmaceuticals sector also trailed lower. American Home Products shed 1.44, or 2.4%, to 58.75. Eli Lilly fell
1.14, or 1.4%, to 80.40. Barr Laboratories gave up 1.08, or 1.4%, to 76.55.
However, the broad market held fast, continuing to anticipate a profit recovery from some important sectors,
such as technology, while gaining a measure of relief from the Fed's as-expected decision to reduce interest
rates again.
Shares of Nokia climbed 1.71, or 7.2%, to 25.50, after the Finnish mobile-phone-handset maker surprised
Wall Street by saying it may yet beat its fourth-quarter earnings projections.
Shares of Ericsson, a rival handset maker, posted a gain of 19 cents, or 3.3%, to 6.01, while some of the
component makers that feed parts into the handset business also moved higher. Shares of RF Micro Devices
(Nasdaq) improved 1.97, or 8.4%, to 25.52, while TriQuint Semiconductor (Nasdaq) added 1.26, or 7.9%, to
17.30. .
The Nasdaq Composite Index finished the session higher by 9.81, or 0.49%, back above the 2000-point mark
again at 2001.93.
"I don't think we got any big surprise by the Fed's decision to reduce interest rates" by a quarter of a
percentage point, said Matt Ruane, director of listed trading at Gerard Klauer Mattison. "I think this market still
needs to begin to show some progress on rebuilding profits, but, with everything the Fed has done this year,
there is a light at the end of the tunnel for an earnings recovery in 2002."
Shares of DoubleClick (Nasdaq) jumped 1.37, or 13%, to 12.15. The Internet advertising concern released a
study that it said showed almost half of European marketers are using online marketing as part of their
strategy.
A number of Internet companies with an exposure to the advertising market acted better on the session.
Yahoo (Nasdaq) advanced 60 cents, or 3.4%, to 18.42, while eBay (Nasdaq) rose 3.10, or 4.6%, to 70.10,
and AOL Time Warner increased 1, or 3.2%, to 32.

revenue drop in the fiscal third quarter from its second won't be as sharp as previously forecast. Rival Altera
(Nasdaq) posted a gain of 42 cents, or 1.7%, to 24.56.
Electronic-game maker Activision (Nasdaq) added 1.64, or 6.4%, to 27.20, after raising its estimates for
earnings and revenue in fiscal 2002 and 2003; the Santa Monica, Calif., concern cited strong computer and
video-game sales spurred by the recent launches of new game systems.

Health & TechnologyUnusual Disease-Care Plan to Be Unveiled
Health & Technology Unusual Disease-Care Plan to Be Unveiled
By Ron Winslow
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
788 words
10 December 2001
The Wall Street Journal
J
B8
English
(Copyright (c) 2001, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Blue Cross and Blue Shield of Minnesota and American Healthways Inc. expect to announce today a 10-year
agreement under which American Healthways will help manage the care of the plan's patients with heart
disease, diabetes, asthma and other chronic diseases.
The pact, unusual in both its length and the scope of illnesses handled by a single company, represents a
significant advance for the field known as disease management, in which patients with chronic ailments are
given special support to help them manage symptoms and avoid costly hospital visits.
The agreement also comes in the midst of a resurgence of health-care inflation that is causing many
employers to look for new strategies to contain medical costs. Officials at Blue Cross and American
Healthways said the program being launched offers a way to mitigate costs while improving outcomes for
patients, especially those who are heavy users of medical services.
"This is really the next evolution of where health plans should go to try to improve the health of our members
and, through that, lower costs," said Mark W. Banks, president and chief executive officer at the Blue Cross
plan.
Precise terms of the deal, which the parties describe as a strategic alliance, aren't being disclosed. Thomas
G. Cigarran, chairman and chief executive officer of American Healthways, Nashville, Tenn., said that fees
paid to his company over the entire 10-year length of the agreement could amount to between $200 million
and $300 million. The actual amount will depend not only on cost savings, but on how well the company
performs on a variety of measures, including evidence that patients' health improved and that they were
satisfied with the program.
In 4 p.m. trading on the Nasdaq Stock Market Friday, American Healthways shares were at $33.40, down
$1.15.
Initially, the program will focus on the "15% to 20% of the population that drives 75% to 80% of the medical
costs," Dr. Banks said. But over the duration of the contract, the parties plan to roll out services for all of Blue
Cross and Blue Shield of Minnesota's 2.1 million members, in the belief that disease-management techniques
also can be used effectively to aid in prevention of disease and maintaining a person's health.
American Healthways uses registered nurses who work with patients by telephone to encourage them to take
their medicines, get necessary checkups and take other steps to manage disease symptoms and follow
recommendations made by their physicians. The nurses have access to an electronic medical record that
tracks patient medical claims, drug prescriptions and laboratory tests. They also are specially trained to
develop trust with patients, a crucial factor in the approach's success, said Bill Gold, chief medical officer at
the Blue Cross plan.
The intent is that regular contact with nurses will, for instance, help patients meet blood-sugar,
blood-pressure and cholesterol-level targets that, according to a large body of medical research, help stave
off serious consequences of chronic diseases. "Compliance [with the targets] across the health system is very
low," Mr. Cigarran noted. "The gap is what causes suboptimal health and the unnecessarily high cost of
health care. People who are sick don't use the health-care system enough in ways that make them healthier."
The parties face challenges. Disease management has emerged in recent years in a variety of forms, with
mixed results. Typically, disease-management companies have focused on just one disease, yet many
patients suffer from several chronic conditions, rendering the single-disease approach ineffective or
cumbersome.
"Managed-care plans were having to contract with five or six different firms, and doctors would have to refer
patients to these different firms," said David Nash, professor of health policy at Jefferson Medical College,

--- Page 129 ---
Page 129 of 243 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Philadelphia, and editor of the journal Disease Management. "That was way too complicated for the market to
make it work." American Healthways will handle more than 15 chronic conditions under the new contract, one
reason Dr. Nash considers it "a breakthrough" for the field.
In addition, doctors and patients alike, wary after a decade of managed-care controversy that cost-saving
strategies could be good for care, may be skeptical. In part to address that issue, American Healthways and
Blue Cross said researchers at Johns Hopkins Medical Institutions will evaluate the program, measuring both
costs and outcomes, and will be free to publish such data in medical journals, whether the results are
favorable or not. "When done well, disease management has a significant impact on the health of members
and on the cost of care," said Dr. Gold.

Oat Reaches Five-Year Highs Amid Tight Supplies
Commodities
Oat Reaches Five-Year Highs Amid Tight Supplies
By Daniel Rosenberg
Dow Jones Newswires
597 words
6 December 2001
The Wall Street Journal
J
C13
English
(Copyright (c) 2001, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
CHICAGO -- Oat futures rode to new five-year highs at the Chicago Board of Trade and have now more than
doubled since the end of June.
Concerns about tight supplies and lack of quality in both the U.S. and Canadian oat crop have the market at
its highest level for a nearby contract since July 1996.
December oat futures rose 3.5 cents to $2.335 a bushel yesterday after earlier setting a new contract high of
$2.34. Back at the end of June, the contract was trading at $1.10 a bushel.
"We continue to find support from tight supplies in both the U.S. and Canada . . . and from prospective sellers
not wanting to step in front of this market," said Shawn McCambridge, an analyst with Prudential Securities in
Chicago. "We've had a steady uptrend since June, and shorts have been getting burned." A short position is
a bet that the market will fall.
Few are betting on falling prices now in the oat market. Indeed, some analysts think futures won't slow down
until they hit resistance near the 1996 highs of between $2.75 and $2.96 a bushel. The all-time high for oats
is nearly $4 a bushel, reached during the drought of 1988.
That mark doesn't seem to be in danger, at least not yet. But for the December contract, which expires a
week from tomorrow, the only path seems to be up. Open interest remains very high, with just over a week
left to trade, at 1,765 contracts, and deliveries against December futures are expected to be scant.
"There's deliverable supply, but it's in strong hands," said Dan Basse, an analyst with Chicago consulting firm
AgResource. "It's really a muscle play."
Yesterday's monthly crop survey from Statistics Canada showed end-of-November Canadian oat stocks at
2.8 million metric tons, down 18% from 3.4 million tons a year earlier.
The U.S. imports 40% of its oat supplies from Canada. But with Canada experiencing its worst growing
season since 1988, U.S. imports from that country are expected to be down 39% during the crop year that
began June 1.
To pick up the slack, the U.S. is relying more heavily on imports of oats from Scandinavia, but that comes
with increased transportation costs.
And U.S. oat production, estimated by the U.S. Department of Agriculture at 117 million bushels, is down
22% from last year. U.S. production peaked at 1.524 billion bushels in 1945. Even 20 years ago, U.S.
production was four times its current level. Over the past two decades, producers have found they can switch
to other, more lucrative crops such as oilseeds and corn.
In other commodities markets:
COPPER: Futures for copper fell on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange on technical,
or chart and options-related, selling. The March contract fell 0.6 cent to 70.3 cents a pound. Prices recovered
from their lows on news that the nonmanufacturing index from the National Association of Purchasing
Management surged in November.
GOLD: Futures for gold declined at the Comex because of a rally in U.S. equities and a firming U.S. dollar,
traders said. The February contract fell $1.70 to $274.60 a troy ounce. Meanwhile, although there was talk of
the Mideast tensions being monitored, the escalating-war tone in Israel didn't spur much safe-haven buying.

EUROPE
EU Regulators Grant
Microsoft's Critics
Right to Hearing
European Union antitrust regulators told Microsoft's critics that they have the right to request a hearing before
the European Commission, despite the U.S. software company's desire to move straight to settlement talks.
Microsoft last week withdrew a request for a hearing in the commission's antitrust investigation. But the
commission this week sent a letter to interested third parties, including rival companies, informing them that
they can ask for the hearing to be scheduled. It wasn't immediately clear whether any company would do so.
Microsoft argues that a proposed settlement between the company and the U.S. Justice Department in a
similar case would resolve all the key issues under investigation in Europe. But one EU official said the U.S.
settlement "only covers part of our concerns."
The commission alleges that Microsoft illegally used its dominant position in the European market for
operating systems for desktop computers to muscle its way into related markets for multimedia software and
programs for servers. Microsoft disputes the allegations but has offered to disclose more information about
the inner workings of its software than it does now.
Carlton Communications Hit by Ad Slump
British television broadcaster Carlton Communications blamed an advertising slump for a 39% decline in
fiscal-year underlying profit, and said it wasn't considering ditching its costly digital-TV joint venture with
Granada. Carlton, part owner of the ITV commercial-television network, said it was exploring all options for
ITV Digital and aims to cut the venture's costs by at least GBP 100 million ($142 million) in the current fiscal
year. Carlton said pretax profit for the year ended Sept. 30 from continuing businesses, before goodwill and
digital investments, was GBP 118 million, above market expectations of as much as GBP 103 million.
Revenue rose 5% to GBP 1.03 billion.
UPC Helped by Liberty Media Agreement
Shares and bonds in financially ailing Dutch cable operator United Pan-Europe Communications rallied
sharply on renewed hopes that the company will successfully restructure its 9.3 billion euros ($8.3 billion) in
long-term debt. The catalyst was news Monday that Liberty Media will take a controlling stake in fellow
Denver company UnitedGlobalCom, which holds a 53% stake in UPC, in exchange for, among other things,
rights to $2.57 billion of UPC's debt. Though plans for a similar deal between UnitedGlobalCom and Liberty
had already been announced, the companies said the deal was "definitive" and scheduled to close "in the first
quarter of 2002," pending shareholder approval. UPC shares rose 11 European cents, or 23%, to 59 cents in
Amsterdam trading, while UPC bonds rose in London about two points and traded at about 18 cents on the
dollar.
Britain's mmO2 to Delay 3G Services
Britain's mmO2, the mobile-phone operator recently spun off from British Telecommunications, said it will
delay the planned introduction of third-generation wireless services by at least six months to the beginning of
2003. Separately, BT Group, the new name for British Telecommunications, said its retail division projects
annual revenue growth of 3% and free-cash-flow growth of 4% a year over four years. In the prospectus for
mm02's spinoff last month, revenue and free cash flow for the division were projected as flat for the
foreseeable future.

--- Page 133 ---
Page 133 of 243 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Italy to Limit Foundations' Bank Role
The Italian government has unveiled legislation aimed at setting more stringent limits on the charitable
foundations that control most of the country's major banks. The limits, in an amendment to Italy's 2002
budget, include forbidding foundation heads from holding top management positions in banks and putting
local government officials in management positions at the foundations. The bill must be approved by
Parliament. Italian banks IntesaBCI, Sanpaolo IMI, UniCredito Italiano and Banca di Roma are all publicly
traded but are in the hands of unlisted foundations that don't have disclosure requirements.
BRIEFLY:
-- The Polish arm of General Motors, which makes Opel cars for the local market, plans to cut 350 of its 1,900
workers next year, an Opel Polska spokesman said.

ASIA/PACIFIC
Philippine Exports Decline at Slower Pace
Philippine exports, hit by declining electronics shipments, continued to fall in October. Export revenue fell
13.5% from a year earlier to $2.94 billion, the government said. For the year's first 10 months, exports fell
14% to $26.87 billion. The October decline in exports was much slower than August's 26% drop and a 22%
contraction in September. Analysts said it was too early to tell whether the country's export revenue will
continue to decline in coming months, but some believe the August plunge may have been the trough.
Barrick Is Silent on Normandy Mining
Speculation that Canada's Barrick Gold may be poised to join the battle for Normandy Mining, Australia's
biggest gold miner, pushed Normandy shares to their highest intraday level since a takeover war for
Normandy erupted last month between South Africa's AngloGold and Newmont Mining of the U.S. But
Normandy shares closed up only one Australian cent at A$1.64 (85 U.S. cents). Barrick's merger partner,
Homestake Mining, is an equal partner with Normandy in Australia's largest gold mine, the Super Pit.
Homestake shareholders are expected to approve the Barrick merger Dec. 14. A Barrick spokesman in
Toronto said it doesn't comment on market speculation.
China Mobile May Buy Parent's Networks
China Mobile, China's dominant wireless operator, said it is studying the possible purchase of eight provincial
cellular networks from its parent, China Mobile Communications, in a deal analysts estimate could be valued
at more than $12 billion. Part of the consideration could be raised through the issue of Chinese depositary
receipts, which would allow Chinese investors to legally buy Hong Kong-listed shares of China Mobile for the
first time.
BRIEFLY:
-- Australia's central bank cut its overnight cash rate a quarter point to 4.25% Wednesday, citing weakness
in the global economy and a subdued inflation outlook.
-- The chairman of Bharti Tele-Ventures, a telecommunications unit of India's privately held Bharti group,
confirmed it is in talks to buy out a 26% stake in its Bharti Mobile unit held by Sweden's Telia.
-- Singapore awarded licenses to two additional foreign banks, Britain's HSBC Holdings and Malayan Banking
of Malaysia. Six foreign banks now have "qualifying full bank" licenses that allow them as many as 15
locations, 10 of which may be branches. The licenses take effect Jan. 1.
-- Japan's cabinet approved guidelines for the main budget for the fiscal year beginning April 1 that will cap
new-bond issuance at 30 trillion yen ($242 billion), Economy Minister Heizo Takenaka said.

THE AMERICAS
Petrobras Re-Evaluates Deal With Enron
State-controlled Petroleo Brasileiro, or Petrobras, is re-evaluating its agreement to acquire stakes held by
U.S. energy company Enron in Brazilian natural-gas distributor Cia. Distribuidora de Gas do Rio de Janeiro,
known as CEG, and its CEG-Rio subsidiary. Petrobras said in April that it agreed to pay a total of $240 million
to Enron for a 25.38% stake in CEG and a 33.75% interest in CEG-Rio. Petrobras holds the remaining shares

--- Page 134 ---
Page 134 of 243 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
in the two companies. An Enron spokesman said the problem with concluding the purchases wasn't the price
but the possibility that "Enron could have used the companies as a guarantee for some other investment. We
can't go and pay for something if we don't know if it will actually be transferred to us."
Transbrasil Seeks Government Payment
Beleaguered Brazilian carrier Transbrasil, the country's fourth-largest airline, planned to ask the government
to take control of the company to help it avoid bankruptcy after suspending flights Monday, a person at the
airline said. "Transbrasil is only suspending its operations and not closing its activities definitively," the
company said in a statement to the Sao Paulo Stock Exchange. The person at the company said federal
intervention was the only way to force the government to pay 330 million reals ($135 million) that Transbrasil
says it is owed and needs to stay in business. The person said a formal request for the intervention by the
Civil Aviation Authority was likely to be made. Transbrasil has $350 million in debt and hasn't paid its 2,000
employees in full for the past two months.
BHP Billiton Reports Caribbean Oil Find
Anglo-Australian conglomerate BHP Billiton said it has made a significant oil find at its Canteen-1 exploration
well off the east coast of the Caribbean island of Trinidad. "With this being our fourth discovery in the area,
offshore Trinidad has the potential to become a major hydrocarbon-producing province," a BHP Billiton official
said. BHP Billiton owns a 45% stake in the block where the well was drilled; France's TotalFinaElf holds 30%
and Canada's Talisman Energy 25%.
BRIEFLY:

refinery conversion program, with projected capital costs totaling as much as 5.8 billion Canadian dollars
($3.7 billion) over 10 years.
-- The Confederation of Venezuelan Workers said it will join a one-day nationwide strike on Monday called by
business leaders to protest a package of economic laws passed by President Hugo Chavez.
