ASIA/PACIFIC
Taiwan's TSMC
Raises Forecasts
For Chip Profits
In another positive sign for the global chip industry, Taiwan Semiconductor Manufacturing Co. raised its 2001
profit forecast and gave a surprisingly bullish projection that revenue in the first quarter of 2002 will rise at
least 10% from this year's final quarter. Chairman Morris Chang said last week that business was expected to
continue improving in the first quarter amid rising orders. The world's largest contract chip maker is widely
viewed as a bellwether for the broader semiconductor industry because it manufactures chips for a wide
range of customers. TSMC forecast fourth-quarter profit will rise 1.5 times from the 1.24 billion New Taiwan
dollars ($36 million) it posted for the third quarter, saying it had seen a "substantial" increase in orders
beginning in October.
For the full year, TSMC projects profit of NT$13.2 billion, up from a September forecast of NT$11 billion.
Sales are forecast to reach NT$125.56 billion, up from NT$121.89 billion predicted earlier.
Normandy Board Recommends No Action
Australia's largest gold miner, Normandy Mining, hinted that it expects suitor Newmont Mining of the U.S. to
raise its bid and counter a revised takeover offer by South Africa's AngloGold. Normandy's board, after
weighing the revised AngloGold offer, advised shareholders to do nothing, saying "there is potential for further
developments." It set an effective Dec. 13 deadline for Newmont to come back with a better offer, saying it
anticipates providing more formal advice no later than two weeks before AngloGold's offer, valued at 3.68
billion Australian dollars (US$1.92 billion), closes on Dec. 27. AngloGold last week sweetened its original
stock-swap offer for Normandy with a cash payment of 20 Australian cents a share.
Tanabe Seiyaku, Taisho Call Off Merger
Japanese drug makers Tanabe Seiyaku and Taisho Pharmaceutical scrapped their planned integration of
operations, citing differences on how to reorganize their businesses. The companies agreed in September to
integrate their operations on April 1 under a holding company. The merger would have created Japan's
third-largest pharmaceutical company. Prior to the suspension of Tokyo Stock Exchange trading in the
companies' shares yesterday following the announcement, Taisho shares rose 8%, while Tanabe shares
declined 12%.
Pakistan Issues Islamic Banking Rules
Pakistan's central bank, which faces a deadline by the country's Supreme Court to transform the domestic
banking system into an interest-free Islamic one by July, issued a set of requirements for commercial banks
that hope to provide Islamic banking. The State Bank of Pakistan said the banks should be listed on the stock
exchange, with a minimum 50% of shares offered to retail investors. The banks, among other requirements,
should also have a minimum paid-up capital of one billion rupees ($161 million) and maintain a minimum 8%
capital-adequacy ratio. The central bank didn't indicate what will happen to existing commercial banks that
operate according to Western principles, giving rise to the possibility that it may allow two banking systems to
co-exist.

successful turnaround effort has strengthened the Japanese auto maker's finances. Moody's raised its ratings

EUROPE/MIDEAST
Euro-Zone Orders May Have Hit Bottom
The euro-zone's manufacturing sector shrank for the eighth straight month in November and remains weak.
But the pace of the slowdown eased for the second time in recent months, providing some hope that the
region's economy may have hit its worst levels and will pick up moderately next year. The Reuters purchasing
managers index rose slightly to 43.6 in November, from a record low of 42.9 in October. Any reading under
50 signals a contraction in the manufacturing sector. It was the second time the index has risen since it began
its decline a year and a half ago.
Cingular Orders Total Nearly $4 Billion
Cingular Wireless of the U.S. has placed orders with major European telecom-equipment makers valued at
almost $4 billion to build its U.S. high-speed wireless network. Cingular, the second-largest U.S. wireless
operator, said in October it would upgrade its entire network to the Global System for Mobile
Communications, or GSM, technology that delivers Internet data on handsets faster than standard mobile
networks. Cingular is a joint venture of BellSouth and SBC Communications. Announcements made
yesterday -- that the share of Cingular's orders going to Sweden's Telefon L.M. Ericsson was likely to total
more than $2 billion, to Finland's Nokia "well over" $1 billion, and to Germany's Siemens about $600 million --
indicated that the upgrade Cingular initially said totaled about $3 billion was closer to $4 billion.
Vattenfall to Buy Mirant's Bewag Stake
Swedish state-run utility Vattenfall boosted its position in Germany's power market by agreeing to buy the
44.8% stake in Bewag held by Mirant of the U.S. for $1.63 billion. Following the acquisition, Vattenfall will own
89.6% of Bewag shares outstanding. Mirant, based in Atlanta, and Vattenfall had unsuccessfully tried to run
Berlin-based Bewag jointly and integrate it into a new utility called Neue Kraft, which is to include Vattenfall's
other German assets. The enlarged company will be the third-largest energy producer in Germany, Vattenfall
said. Mirant's chief executive said the Bewag pullout was a "very difficult decision" but Vattenfall's offer was
too good to pass up.

-- Germany's Siemens won a $896.5 million contract to build a 1,500-megawatt power plant near Abu Dhabi,
Dubai. The Abu Dhabi electricity authority, CMS Energy of the U.S. and Britain's International Power will own
and operate the plant.

Bell Canada International announced a recapitalization plan to meet its short-term debt obligations and said

ASIA/PACIFIC
AngloGold Sweetens
Offer for Normandy,
Trumping Newmont
The battle for Australia's largest gold miner, Normandy Mining, heated up as South Africa's AngloGold
sweetened its bid to 3.7 billion Australian dollars ($1.93 billion), topping a rival offer by Newmont Mining of the
U.S. Normandy adviser Macquarie Bank said the miner's board would meet in the next few days to consider
AngloGold's revised offer. AngloGold added 20 Australian cents a share in cash to its previous stock-swap
offer, valuing Normandy at A$1.65 a share. The bid is aimed at trumping a rival offer from Newmont that was
recommended by Normandy's board this month. Newmont's offer is valued at as much as A$1.51 a share,
valuing Normandy at A$3.4 billion. The Newmont bid includes a five-cent-a-share cash component based on
at least 90% acceptance.
Newmont and Normandy both recommended that Normandy shareholders sit tight, while analysts began
debating the likelihood of a revised Newmont offer.
Australian Current-Account Gap Narrows
Australia's third-quarter current-account deficit narrowed to 2.87 billion Australian dollars (US$1.5 billion),
from A$5.02 billion a year earlier and A$3.33 billion in the prior quarter, the Bureau of Statistics said. The
seasonally adjusted deficit in the current account, the broadest measure of trade, was the lowest as a
percentage of gross domestic product in 21 years. But economists warned the figures may mark a trough in
the balance-of-payments cycle as the global slowdown dents demand for Australia's exports. The
third-quarter deficit, in annual terms, was 2.4% of GDP.
Japan's Jobless Rate Hits Record 5.4%
Japan's unemployment rate rose to a record 5.4% in October, from 5.3% in September, while consumer
prices continued to slide, yet more bad news for policy makers trying to keep the economy afloat. The
number of jobless reached 3.52 million, up 380,000 from a year earlier and increasing for the seventh straight
month, the government said. Separate data Friday showed that the core consumer price index fell 0.7% in
October from a year before, falling for the 25th straight month and underscoring the deflationary pressures on
the fragile economy. Meanwhile, the Bank of Japan's policy-making board, amid market speculation that it
might take more radical easing steps, yesterday left monetary policy unchanged, saying it would continue to
provide banks with copious liquidity.
Hyundai Securities Delays Share Issue
South Korea's Hyundai Securities will delay issuing new preferred shares to insurer American International
Group and investment firm W.L. Ross -- which had been scheduled today -- because the three companies
haven't reached an agreement on proposed investment terms, a Hyundai Securities spokesman said. The
share purchase is part of the U.S. companies' plan to invest 1.1 trillion won ($868 million) in three Hyundai
Group affiliates -- Hyundai Securities, Hyundai Investment Trust Securities and Hyundai Investment Trust
Management -- to buy controlling stakes in those companies. Wilbur Ross, chairman of W.L. Ross, said the
deal can't be completed until the U.S. firms finish their due diligence. AIG declined to comment.
China Huarong Sells Bad-Debt Portfolio

China Huarong Asset Management said it made China's first sale of nonperforming bank debt to a consortium
led by U.S. investment bank Morgan Stanley. Huarong said it would receive $277.8 million for the portfolio of
bad debt consisting mostly of loans secured by real-estate assets in 18 Chinese cities. Huarong will collect
about 21% of the face value of the bank loans, originally valued at $1.3 billion.
BRIEFLY:

After a nine-month investigation, the auditors said 4,976 bankers and executives were found to have
pocketed 6.5 trillion won intended to bail out their businesses.

EUROPE
Munich Re Posts Loss on Terror Claims
German reinsurer Muenchener Rueckversicherung, or Munich Re, posted a third-quarter loss of 1.21 billion
euros ($1.07 billion), as results were dented heavily by claims arising from the terrorist attacks in the U.S. But
analysts were relieved that the world's largest reinsurance company -- in terms of premium income -- said
that while the attacks would "leave deep scars" in full-year results, the company would post a profit for 2001
and was sticking to its estimate of 2.1 billion euros in damage claims. Gross premium income rose 20% to
8.77 billion euros, at the upper end of analysts' estimates. For the year's first nine months, gross premiums
increased 15% to 25.8 billion euros. Munich Re was reporting quarterly results for the first time.
Wellcome Trust Trims Holdings in Glaxo
Britain's Wellcome Trust, the world's largest medical charity, said it was selling down its stake in British
pharmaceuticals company GlaxoSmithKline to diversify its investment portfolio. The trust, which is selling its
shares through an accelerated bookbuilding exercise, is offering at least GPB 1 billion ($1.43 billion) in
GlaxoSmithKline stock, up to a maximum 125 million shares. Some of the shares will be taken up by Glaxo,
which plans to buy as much as GBP 1 billion of the shares held by Wellcome Trust as part of the company's
previously announced program to buy back as much as GBP 4 billion of its shares.
Kvaerner Rights Issue Fully Subscribed
Norwegian engineering company Kvaerner said a two billion Norwegian kroner ($223 million) rights issue has
been fully subscribed. Cash-strapped Kvaerner avoided bankruptcy Wednesday by agreeing to merge with
Norwegian oil-services company Aker Maritime, its largest shareholder. The rights offering, the first part of a
larger 3.5 billion kroner offering, is a key element in a long-term financial rescue plan unveiled by the two
companies.
BRIEFLY:
-- Credit Agricole's central operating body, Caisse Nationale du Credit Agricole, approved a planned 20 billion
euro ($17.7 billion) initial public offering for France's largest mutual bank. Details of the listing are expected to
be announced today.
-- In a landmark effort to squelch the trade in diamonds used to fund civil wars in Africa, representatives of the
diamond industry, human-rights groups and more than 30 governments agreed in Gabarone, Botswana, that
all shipments of rough stones must contain certificates of origin.
-- The Czech National Bank, in a surprise decision, cut key interest rates by a half percentage point. The
two-week repo rate was cut to 4.75% from 5.25%; the discount rate to 3.75% from 4.25%; and the so-called
Lombard rate to 5.75% from 6.25%.

South African Breweries Buys Brewer

South African Breweries agreed to buy a 97% stake in Central American brewing and bottling company
Cerveceria Hondurena for $537 million from Dole Food of the U.S. The deal continues the South African
company's aggressive expansion into emerging markets. SAB has already opened operations in China, India,
Uganda and Romania this year, and has a proven track record in creating links with strong local markets.

Renault to Shut Brazil Plant for 2 Months
Renault plans to halt work at its vehicle plants in Brazil in December and January because of slumping sales
amid the country's slowing economy, the French auto maker said. Renault will shut its auto plant in Parana
state from Dec. 3 to Jan. 26, and its engine plant will be closed from Dec 17. to Jan. 14. A new small-truck
plant to be shared with Nissan Motor, in which Renault holds a 37% stake, will be idled from Dec. 24 to Jan.
5. The plant is scheduled to be inaugurated on Dec. 20. The stoppages will affect about 4,000 workers.
BRIEFLY:
-- Air Canada and the Canadian Auto Workers union reached agreement on voluntary programs that will
avoid job cuts for certain employees and meet the carrier's cost-cutting goals. The pact includes leaves of
absence, programs to share jobs and work and early-retirement incentives.

U.K.'s BAE to Close
Regional-Jet Unit,
Cut 1,700 Positions
British defense and aerospace company BAE Systems said it will close its regional-jet business and cut about
1,700 jobs because of slumping demand for civil aircraft since the terrorist attacks in the U.S. BAE, which
also owns a 20% stake in European aircraft maker Airbus, said its military business was progressing well but
changes in expectations for Airbus would postpone BAE's return to growth, which it had predicted would be in
2002. The company said it will take a charge of GBP 250 million ($353 million) this year to cover the closure
of the regional-jet unit. It also said the restructuring of its aerostructure division, which makes fuselage parts
for Airbus and U.S. plane maker Boeing, will generate costs of GBP 150 million, including GBP 120 million
this year.
BAE said it will complete four Avro RJ regional jets it is building but will build no more. The history of the RJ
aircraft was marred last week by the crash of a Crossair RJ-100 in Zurich that killed 24 people.
Ahold Posts Profit, Maintains Outlook
Dutch food retailer Ahold reported a 17% increase in third-quarter profit and said it was on track to achieve a
15% rise in per share earnings for the full year despite a weaker global economy. Analysts said the reiteration
of the outlook confirmed the underlying strength of Ahold, the world's third-largest supermarket retailer and
the second-largest food-service supplier in the U.S. Profit rose to 304.2 million euros ($268 million), above
analysts' expectations of 302.7 million euros. Sales increased 12.3% to 15.5 billion euros, helped by
acquisitions. Ahold said that despite weaker economic conditions, like-for-like sales growth was 6.4% and
organic operating-profit growth was 16.4%. Its chairman said the Sept. 11 attacks in the U.S., where Ahold
generates more than half of its sales, had only a mild impact on the company's business.
Former South African Political Foes Ally
Two South African political parties, once sworn enemies over apartheid, announced an alliance that would
have been unthinkable under the country's former whites-only rule. The New National Party, heir to the party
that instituted apartheid, has joined forces with the ruling African National Congress, which came to power in
1994 after waging an armed struggle to overthrow the apartheid regime. The parties said their agreement
paves the way for reconciliation among all South Africans. The coalition lays the groundwork for the
predominantly white NNP to gain seats in national, provincial and local governments. The NNP has 28 seats
in the 400-member Parliament, and its influence has been waning.
Auto Makers May Form Formula One Rival
European auto makers involved in Formula One have invited the sport's racing teams to join them in forming
a breakaway circuit, the latest twist in a battle over television rights with German media and pay-TV company
Kirch Group. The five European auto makers, which said they are in the process of setting up a joint
company, met with representatives of six other Formula One teams, hoping to persuade them to help launch
a competing circuit by 2008. The auto makers include Fiat, sponsor of the Ferrari team; DaimlerChrysler's
Mercedes-Benz; Bayerische Motoren Werke; Renault; and Jaguar, a unit of Ford Motor. Kirch officials didn't
answer calls seeking comment.

Nintendo's president, Hiroshi Yamauchi, will use capital gains from the sale of shares in the Japanese

China's Gold Exchange to Test Systems
China's first gold exchange since the country came under communist rule in 1949 plans to open today for
simulated trading, bringing closer the end of the central bank's monopoly on gold. Only domestic firms will
be allowed to trade initially on the exchange, located in Shanghai's foreign-exchange trading center. The
firms will only be able to trade physical gold. Forward contracts and futures won't be offered, though they will
be introduced eventually, a market official said. The exchange will open with 108 members, including 23 gold
producers, the Xinmin Evening News said this month. State-owned Industrial & Commercial Bank of China
will be the exchange's settlement bank and trust company. The exchange will begin with simulated trading to
test the electronic systems. An official opening is tentatively scheduled in January.

Taiwan's index of leading economic indicators rose 2.5% in October, resuming a trend interrupted by the

index rebounded from a 1.4% decline in September, and has risen in four of the past five months, after
declining for 16 consecutive months. The index's upturn indicates the country's recession-hit economy has
turned a corner, analysts said.

Bank of Canada Cuts Rates by Half Point
The Bank of Canada reduced its benchmark interest rate by a half percentage point to 2.25%, its lowest level
in 41 years. The central bank since January has lowered its overnight rate, which is comparable to the U.S.
federal-funds rate, by 3.5 percentage points. Canada's commercial banks responded by lowering their prime
lending rates by a half point to a record-low 4%. The Bank of Canada, noting it "is still too early to
characterize the economic outlook with great assurance, given ongoing uncertainties," said it "continues to
anticipate that the economy will be operating with excess capacity throughout 2002." Canada's economy,
strong in the year's first half, has been hit recently by a drop in employment, weak retail sales and weaker
U.S. demand for exports.
Mexico to Investigate `Dirty War' Cases
Mexican President Vicente Fox said he would appoint a special prosecutor to investigate the "dirty war"

received a detailed report on torture and the forced disappearance of suspected leftists, rebels and activists
from the country's human-rights ombudsman, said that officials who committed human-rights violations
should face justice. "There are no needs of the state that are above the rule of law," he said at a news
conference announcing the report. The report was drawn up as part of efforts to clear up Mexico's history of
human-rights abuses, many of which occurred during a series of small leftist rebellions in the `70s.

Tax Planning (A Special Report)---Last Chance:A look at some valuable year-end tax-saving tips
Tax Planning (A Special Report) --- Last Chance: A look at some valuable year-end tax-saving tips
By Tom Herman
1,607 words
26 November 2001
The Wall Street Journal
J
R4
English
(Copyright (c) 2001, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
IT ISN'T TOO LATE.
There's still plenty of time left to make some smart year-end tax moves. Among those who stand to gain the
most are investors savaged by this year's stock market.
Look over your portfolio before the end of the year to see if it might make sense to get rid of some losers you
were thinking of selling anyway, says Mark A. Luscombe, a lawyer and certified public accountant at CCH
Inc., a Riverwoods, Ill., publisher of tax and other business information.
It can be difficult to bite the bullet, of course, because it means turning a paper loss into a real loss. But
periodic portfolio pruning makes good investment sense -- and it can cut your tax bill. So long as the stocks or
funds aren't part of a tax-advantaged account, such as a 401(k) plan or an individual retirement account,
investors can use the losses to offset realized gains on other investments, or even a few thousand dollars of
wages or other ordinary income. That will mean a smaller tax bill next April.
But taking advantage of the capital-loss rules isn't the only thing taxpayers can do to cut their taxes before the
end of the year. Accountants also are urging clients to consider such other traditional year-end strategies as
deferring income until next year and accelerating deductions into this year.
Here are some of the major year-end tax-saving suggestions:
TAX ALCHEMY: TURNING
LOSSES INTO GOLD
Suppose you made a $25,000 profit on a stock you bought several years ago and sold earlier this year. If that
was your sole transaction this year and you are subject to the 20% capital-gains tax rate, you typically would
owe $5,000 in capital-gains taxes.
But suppose you have owned another stock for a few years; it has gone down sharply in value and you have
been thinking of selling. If you dump that stock now for $25,000 less than you paid for it, you typically would
be able to use that loss to offset your gain on a dollar-for-dollar basis. The result: Your net profit would be
zero, and your capital-gains tax would be zero -- a tax saving of $5,000.
Now to another part of the law that may be less well-known but even more valuable in many cases: how to
handle net losses. You can deduct as much as $3,000 a year ($1,500 if you're married and filing a separate
tax return) of your net losses against your salary and other ordinary income. Thus, many experienced
investors often take their lumps around the end of each year, getting rid of losers they were thinking of selling
anyway. Many investors have net losses that far exceed the threshold. If so, keep in mind that the tax law
allows you to carry over unused losses to future years.
Some members of Congress have introduced bills to raise the $3,000-a-year limit. But Congress hasn't
approved the change, and tax analysts say prospects for enactment aren't good because there are so many
other competing tax proposals.
Beware of some important fine print: There is a jarring tax pothole known as the wash-sale rule. A wash sale
occurs when you sell a stock or some other security and, within 30 days before or after the transaction, you
buy substantially identical stock or securities. If you do that, you're not allowed to deduct your losses on that
sale, says Mr. Luscombe of CCH.
But if your loss was disallowed because of the wash-sale rules, it's not gone forever. Add the amount of the
disallowed loss to the cost of the new stock or securities when figuring your cost basis. This effectively
postpones the loss deduction until you sell the new stock or securities. Your holding period for the new stock

--- Page 145 ---
Page 145 of 243 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
or securities begins on the same day as the holding period of the stock or securities sold, the IRS says in
Publication 550 (available free on the IRS Web site, www.irs.gov).
It's usually fairly easy to avoid wash-sale trouble, though. Just don't buy exactly the same stock or bond you
sold.
Two additional thoughts: First, these capital-loss tax rules apply to your investments. The Internal Revenue
Service says you can't deduct any losses on the sale of your personal residence, even though you may
consider your home to be your biggest single investment.
Second, never buy or sell any stock solely for tax reasons. Be sure you base your investment decisions on
solid investment criteria, not just tax considerations. But there's also a corollary rule: Don't sell without at least
considering the tax implications.
TIMING INCOME
AND DEDUCTIONS
The old general rule still holds true in most cases: Whenever possible, accelerate deductions into the current
year and defer income into the following year, says Tom Ochsenschlager of accounting firm Grant Thornton
in Washington, D.C. The basic idea is to delay your income-tax payments as long as possible.
The income-deferral strategy works for millions of self-employed individuals and others who have some
degree of control over when they get paid. For example, a self-employed consultant seeking to defer income
until next year might delay sending a bill to a client until January.
Accelerating deductions into this year can be done by, say, making charitable contributions now, says Mr.
Ochsenschlager. Or pay state and local taxes now that aren't due until early next year.
But in some cases, such as taxpayers who expect to be in a much higher bracket next year, it may pay to do
the opposite: accelerating income into this year and delaying deductions until next year. The only way to
know for sure is to crunch the numbers both ways.
Also consider bunching certain expenses to take advantage of tax rules, says Mr. Ochsenschlager. For
instance, take a look at your medical expenses. Remember that they are deductible only to the extent they
exceed 7.5% of your adjusted gross income. Thus, in some cases where there's no chance you will exceed
the limit this year, it may pay to delay certain expenses until next year or, if you've already exceeded the
annual limitation, this might be an opportune time to incur elective medical expenses, such as new
eyeglasses, he says.
Before you act, make sure you don't shoot yourself in the foot by losing your eligibility for various credits, such
as the child credit, the Hope Scholarship credit, retirement-savings accounts and many other items. Also be
careful of taking some action that could result in your getting hammered by the alternative minimum tax, or
AMT. The AMT is a separate tax system set up several decades ago in an effort to make sure that very
wealthy people couldn't avoid paying any federal income taxes through the use of credits, deductions and
other items. But because it was never indexed for inflation, the AMT now is hitting many not-so-wealthy
people.
Unless you are intimately familiar with tax law and comfortable with higher math, it may pay to invest in
income-tax software or visit a tax professional to help you with various what-if scenarios.
MUTUAL FUNDS
Before making any major new investment in a mutual fund this year, be sure to ask whether the fund plans
any year-end distributions. It may not be an issue this year because stock prices have dropped so sharply.
But some types of funds, notably gold funds, have posted gains.
Thus, ask when the fund is planning to make any payouts and how much. If that payout is significant,
consider waiting to buy the fund until after the effective date for the distribution, says Mr. Luscombe of CCH.
Otherwise, you may be buying yourself a tax headache you could easily have avoided.
OTHER TAXING ISSUES
While income-tax considerations are the main issues at this time of year, here are a few other thoughts to
consider:
-- The luxury tax on expensive new automobiles will drop in January. This year, the rate is 4% on the amount
above $38,000. Next year, the rate will fall to 3%, and the threshold will rise to $40,000, according to

--- Page 146 ---
Page 146 of 243 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
projections by George G. Jones of CCH and Prof. James Young of Northern Illinois University in DeKalb, Ill.
This tax is scheduled to expire at the end of 2002.
-- Stay healthy. Estate taxes will drop sharply starting next year. The basic exclusion from gift and estate
taxes, now $675,000, will rise to $1 million next year and in 2003. The new law also reduces the highest rate
-- now typically 55% -- to 50% next year.
-- The gift-tax exclusion will rise next year. You will be able to give away as much as $11,000, up from
$10,000 this year, to anyone you want and to as many lucky recipients as you wish without having to worry
about tax considerations. When you make such a gift, you don't owe gift taxes, and recipients don't owe
income taxes.
---
Mr. Herman is a senior special writer for The Wall Street Journal in New York and author of the Journal's
weekly Tax Report column.

EUROPE
French Regulators
Order Inspections
Of Airbus Jetliners
French aviation regulators called for an inspection of all Airbus A300-600 and A310 aircraft as a precaution
following the crash of an American Airlines jet in New York last week. Airlines operating the 407 planes in
service world-wide were asked Friday to visually inspect the planes' tail fins and rudders over the next two
weeks, France's civil aviation authority said. The request coincided with an order by the Federal Aviation
Authority on Friday for airlines to immediately inspect the tail assemblies of Airbus A300-600 and A310 jets.
American Airlines, a unit of AMR, said it had completed the inspections ordered by the FAA and had found no
safety problems. Airbus is based in Toulouse, France.
French and U.S. investigators are focusing on the aircraft's tail fin and rudder, which fell off the American
Airlines A300-600 jet shortly after Flight 587 took off from John F. Kennedy Airport on Nov. 12. The plane
crashed seconds later, killing all 260 people on board and five on the ground. Investigators haven't yet
determined why the two parts fell off.
Schroder Warns Greens to Fall in Line
German Chancellor Gerhard Schroder, fresh from winning a confidence vote, urged his Social Democrats to
unite behind a German military role against terrorism and warned his coalition partners, the Greens, to fall in
line. Mr. Schroder told a party convention he aims to lead Germany into greater global responsibility despite
pacifist opposition that nearly brought down his center-left coalition last week. "If we waver, our country will
suffer," he said. "That is why I need your support for the policy that the German Parliament approved Friday."
Mr. Schroder, who was unopposed, was confirmed as party chairman for another two-year term. Germany
has pledged as many as 3,900 troops to the U.S.-led war on terrorism. His speech sent a dual message to
the Greens: The coalition will stand, but only if the Greens accept Mr. Schroder's authority.

Dutch chemicals company DSM sold its stake in natural-gas concern Energie Beheer Nederland, or EBN, to
the Dutch government for 1.24 billion euros ($1.1 billion) to sharpen its focus on higher-growth activities. The
government wanted to buy DSM's stake in EBN to simplify its planned restructuring of the Dutch natural-gas
market, government officials said. But it was an initiative that coincided with DSM's own restructuring plans to
concentrate on specialty chemicals and reduce the cyclical nature of its business.

German electronics and engineering company Siemens confirmed its losses in the U.S. for the fiscal year
ended Sept. 30 are in line with figures in a magazine report. Focus magazine, in an advance weekend
release, said Siemens's U.S. unit posted a fiscal-year pretax loss of 1.3 billion marks ($588 million), wider
than a pretax loss of 300 million in the previous fiscal year. A Siemens spokesman confirmed the U.S. unit's
loss was "in this range" but didn't give exact figures. Siemens last week reported fiscal-year profit fell 76% to
2.1 billion euros ($1.86 billion) but will give more earnings details Dec. 6.

Mexicans Rush Across the Border, This Time Headed South---Downturn, Terror in U.S.Drive Immigrants Out,Keep Aspirants Home
International
Mexicans Rush Across the Border, This Time Headed South --- Downturn, Terror in U.S. Drive
Immigrants Out, Keep Aspirants Home
By Wall Street Journal staff reporters Joel Millman in Tijuana, Mexico, and Eduardo Porter in Los Angeles
861 words
15 November 2001
The Wall Street Journal
J
A22
English
(Copyright (c) 2001, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
The ticket office of Transportes Golden State SA, here in Tijuana at the Mexican border's busiest crossing
point, used to hum with commerce. Every evening, buses bound for Las Vegas, Denver, Sacramento, Calif.,
or Yakima, Wash., pulled out, packed with Mexican migrants heading north for work.
These days, it is the return business that fills Golden State's coaches. "Our people are coming back," ticket
vendor Santos Galicia says. "They're afraid, or their families are afraid. Call your mother in Mexico and she
says, `I'm worried, my son. Come home.'"
For years, it seemed that the rush of immigrants from Mexico would never abate. But during the past few
months, terrorism and the economic downturn appear to have achieved what the border patrol never could:
slow and, to an extent, even reverse the flow of illegal immigration.
Last week, Mexico's National Migration Institute reported that more than 350,000 Mexican citizens had
returned from the U.S. during the two months since the Sept. 11 terrorist attacks, a 9% increase from the
year-earlier period. The jump comes in spite of fears that tighter immigration controls will make it much more
difficult to get back across the border in the new year, a concern that is prompting some immigrant farm
workers to wait out the winter in the U.S. Migration Commissioner Felipe Preciado says he expects another
two million Mexicans to come home by year end.
Many will be temporary visitors, part of an annual Christmas wave of returnees that courses through Mexico's
airports and border crossings. But if a trend that began well in advance of Sept. 11 continues, many of those
workers won't be planning a round trip. For the 12 months up to Sept. 30, apprehensions of illegal immigrants
at the Southern border dropped 25% from the previous year, to 1.2 million, their lowest level since 1995.
From Oct. 1 through Nov. 5, apprehensions dropped by 54%, to 43,000, an indication that thousands of
casual laborers have likely decided to postpone their commute until a recovery begins in the U.S.
Besides those no longer arriving, Mexicans facing shrunken job prospects in the U.S. appear to be folding
their tents. Mexico's Foreign Ministry reports that so far this year, its 46 consulates in the U.S. have issued
almost 50,000 more passports and presunciones, the travel documents Mexicans without official papers need
to come home, than last year -- a sign, consular officials say, that recession and fear of terrorism are taking
their toll.
Demand for documents is so strong in New York that the consulate there converted its art gallery into a
makeshift waiting room for applicants. "We never issued more than 1,000 of these documents in any month
before," says deputy consul Francisco Olavarria, who says the consulate issued more than 3,000 passes in
October.
"Many have left," says Arnulfo Chino, a Mexican immigrant from the state of Puebla who assists newly
unemployed immigrants at Organizacion Tepeyac in New York. The organization has helped the most
desperate by negotiating with airlines for a cheap one-way fare -- $201 -- to fly home on standby.
There is no accurate measure of unemployment among undocumented immigrants in the U.S., but the
numbers have certainly grown. During the past year, the official number of unemployed Hispanics jumped by
more than 350,000. In some sectors with a large illegal work force, such as hotels and restaurants, job losses
since Sept. 11 have run in the hundreds of thousands.
The homeward exodus could create problems for Mexican President Vicente Fox, who was swept into office
last year on a promise to create 1.3 million jobs annually. So far this year, Mexico has lost nearly half a million
jobs, and the prospect of an invasion of returning "guest workers" further swelling the unemployment ranks is
daunting. So too is any drop in the $8 billion to $10 billion in annual remittances immigrants send home.

--- Page 151 ---
Page 151 of 243 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
"Many will return with their savings, and those will be channeled into productive projects," Mr. Preciado says,
expressing a hope shared by other Mexican officials that a self-regulating flow of workers in a downturn offers
policy makers a silver lining. If anything, it may make it easier for both countries to negotiate immigration rules
to allow more workers to travel to the U.S.
A cooling off of Mexican migration could also have a long-term effect on the U.S. economy. According to
figures compiled by Washington's Urban Institute, the aggregate household income of Mexican immigrant
families soared in the 1990s, swelling from $139 billion a year in 1995 to more than $220 billion in 1999, with
most of that spending power fed by recent arrivals from Mexico.
That torrent of cash triggered a gold rush by U.S. and Mexican companies. Many of them may have to
postpone their expansion plans.

Fed's Rate Cut Is Unlikely to Ignite Asia Markets,As Investors Focus on Timing of U.S. Recovery
World Stock Markets
Fed's Rate Cut Is Unlikely to Ignite Asia Markets, As Investors Focus on Timing of U.S. Recovery
By Karen Richardson
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
1,260 words
7 November 2001
The Wall Street Journal
J
C11
English
(Copyright (c) 2001, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
HONG KONG -- The decision by the Federal Reserve Board to slash rates a half percentage point is unlikely
to ignite Asian markets.
Most markets here didn't rise sharply in anticipation of the U.S. rate cut, which occurred after Asian markets
had closed for the day. And analysts aren't looking for much of a bounce today either.
The real question in Asia is how quickly the U.S. economy will rebound. And now that U.S.
economic-recovery scenarios have been extended to the second quarter of next year at the earliest, global
stock markets are seen ticking up in anticipation by early next year, but probably not before. "The rate cuts
will eventually help in the market turnaround, but it's not going to be a one-day turn," said Jason Press, a
member of Goldman Sachs's Asian-Pacific strategy team.
Major Asian stock markets generally heralded the expected Fed rate cut with moderate gains earlier this
week. Yesterday, Australia rose 1.7%, while Japan's Nikkei 225 Stock Average climbed 1.8%. South Korea
and New Zealand each edged up while Taiwan and Malaysia ended flat. Singapore and Hong Kong fell
slightly. Hong Kong still ranks among the world's worst-performing stock markets with a year-to-date drop of
31%.
Safe-harbor markets, including gold and Asian government bonds, were quiet as sovereign-bond spreads to
U.S. Treasury paper narrowed only slightly.
In the interim, few are forecasting sustained rallies, and some are even predicting consolidation for Asian
stock markets that have bounced back recently from abysmally low valuations.
Export-oriented Taiwan, home to the world's biggest semiconductor foundry, has rallied 12% since the end of
September. By contrast, export-dependent Singapore and Malaysia have edged up 1.2% and fallen 3.7%,
respectively, in the same period. Meanwhile, South Korea has jumped 18% since Sept. 28.
Elizabeth Soon, a Schroder Investment Management fund manager, isn't counting on rate cuts alone to drive
stock markets. "A lot of people match low interest rates with good stock-market returns, but that doesn't
always happen," said Ms. Soon, who manages Schroder's $70 million Asian Fund. "It depends on where your
stock markets were before the cuts started, and in our case, they were very high."
Another reason analysts say rates may not work quickly is that with overcapacity in the U.S., cheaper money
isn't likely to provide as fast a stimulus to industrial investment as it would if the economy were running closer
to full-tilt.
Like Mr. Press, Ms. Soon says the more telling indicator will be the impact the cuts have on private
consumption both in the U.S. and in Asia. She said the rate cuts will eventually prompt Asian bank-deposit
holders, fixed-income investors and purchasers of capital-guaranteed funds to switch back into the equities
markets, especially once private consumption rebounds.
Betting on the strength and resilience of the U.S. consumer, Ms. Soon is marginally overweight the Asian
technology sector, which she expects will benefit substantially from a consumer-led U.S. recovery.
Regional market strategists such as Ajay Kapur at Morgan Stanley are recommending investors spread their
bets now in both the growth and consumer sectors, with the markets of Taiwan, South Korea and Singapore
as their top picks followed by India and Hong Kong/China.

--- Page 153 ---
Page 153 of 243 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Mr. Kapur expects a strong rebound in the low valuations, drawing a parallel between the current economic
environment and that of 1992, which was followed by a strong bull market the following year. "The markets
are looking beyond the valley and they see growth," he said.
While strategists aren't predicting a big stock-market rebound after the latest rate cut, they are in agreement
that the Fed's move should be welcomed by the world's beleaguered economies.
"These Fed cuts tell you the Fed is still on the job, that they realize there is a risk here and that they need to
get rates down quickly," said Shane Oliver, Sydney-based manager of Henderson Global Investors' global
strategy team and chief economist for the firm, which has $148 billion in total assets under management.
"That provides confidence the global economy will turn around within a reasonable space of time." He
predicts the U.S. economy will start to improve by the third quarter of next year.
---
Other Market Activity
European markets, most closing before the Fed's announcement on rates, were little changed. The region's
focus is now turning to tomorrow's meetings of the Bank of England and European Central Bank, which are
both expected to ease rates. Overall, the Dow Jones World Stock Index rose 1.56 points, or 0.92%, to
170.85. Excluding the U.S., the index increased 0.29 point, or 0.24%, to 121.44.
In TOKYO, the Nikkei Stock Average rose 1.8%, or 186.18 points, to 10633.72. Concerns over bad loans
continued to pummel bank stocks. Mitsubishi Tokyo Financial Group dropped 1.6% to 865,000 yen, extending
its losing streak to seven sessions. Mizuho Holdings gave up 1.2% to end at 343,000 yen, and Sumitomo
Mitsui Banking fell 0.7% to 732 yen. Chip makers extended recent gains. NEC rose 4.4% to 1,284 yen, and
Hitachi climbed 1.8% to 905 yen. Fast Retailing continued to drop after it reported Friday its October sales
dropped 25% from a year earlier. The retailer of casual clothes fell 10.7% to finish at 10,670 yen.
In LONDON, the FTSE 100 Index closed up 0.1%, or five points, at 5214.10. Cisco Systems' news on
Monday of better-than-expected first-quarter results helped boost technology stocks. Sage ended up five
pence, or 2.2%, at 234 pence, while TTP Communications was up 18 pence, or 13.6%, at 150.5 pence.
Spirent moved ahead by 44 pence, or 35.2%, to 169 pence after releasing a trading statement saying
year-to-date trading was in line with hopes and that it had signed a "significant" deal with Verizon.
In PARIS, the CAC 40 Index slipped 0.5%, or 23.65 points, to 4461.69. Pharmaceuticals group Aventis was
among the more heavily traded decliners, falling 2.3% to 84.40 euros, despite its reporting a 42% rise in
nine-month net profit in its core pharmaceuticals business. TF1 fell 3.6% to close at 27.50 euros after its
Monday evening revenue report confirmed a drop in the advertising market, a mainstay of its revenue stream.
Alcatel gained 1.5% to end at 18.92 euros.

Monthly Mutual Fund Review:Glittering Gold Funds Lure Investors---But Risks Can Outshine GainsFor Novices; Our PrimerFor Would-Be Prosp...
Monthly Mutual Fund Review: Glittering Gold Funds Lure Investors --- But Risks Can Outshine Gains
For Novices; Our Primer For Would-Be Prospectors
By Peter A. McKay
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
1,564 words
5 November 2001
The Wall Street Journal
J
R1
English
(Copyright (c) 2001, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
DON'T invest in anything you don't understand, goes the old investing advice. But who understands gold
anymore?
After its 20-year bear market in which many investors fled from the metal in droves and never looked back,
gold mutual funds are one of the few bright spots in an otherwise dismal investing picture this year. Despite a
2.43% drop in October, gold funds are up an average of 13.64% for the year, compared with a 19.38%
decline by the average diversified U.S.-stock fund, according to Lipper Inc.
Posting a gain in 2001 has been no easy task as only three of the 35 stock-only fund categories tracked by
Lipper have managed the feat. Even more impressive is that during the past 52 weeks, gold funds have
averaged a 30.28% gain, more than three times as much as small-cap value funds, the next
strongest-performing portfolio category.
Gold is traditionally an investor refuge during times of crisis, but precious-metals portfolios were gaining even
before the Sept. 11 terrorist attacks, with the average fund up 12.71% for the year through Sept. 10. But
gold's price spiked immediately afterward, peaking at $294.30 an ounce on Sept. 21, though both the
commodity price and share prices of companies that mine the metal have since given back some of the
gains.
"Ever since World War II, there hasn't been much need for people to have something they can flee a country
with, or physically hold onto in case their money becomes worthless," says George Gero, first vice president
of Prudential Securities. "That's meant people have ignored gold for some time. But they can't anymore"
because of its relative strength as other sectors have tumbled.
Gold funds are too risky for many portfolios, but their attractive returns lately are tempting more investors to
add precious-metals funds to their holdings. With that in mind, here are answers to a few basic questions
about gold and gold funds:
Hasn't gold's status as an investing haven during turmoil diminished over the years?
Yes. Gold's past role as a haven stemmed from its portability, universal acceptance as a store of value and
its lack of ties to any company or government, unlike paper assets.
But since 1979 -- when gold peaked above $800 an ounce -- it has undergone a steady price decline that
has brought it to about $280 an ounce today. That has led many former gold bugs to abandon the metal in
favor of bonds or, in recent years, the strong U.S. dollar.
In response, many mutual-fund companies have scrapped their precious-metals offerings over the years,
leaving only about 40 funds in the entire sector, says analyst Christopher M. Davis of fund-tracker
Morningstar Inc. Of those, he said, fewer than 10 invest only in gold companies; the others allocate between
10% and 20% of their portfolios in companies that mine other metals like platinum and silver.
Have investors flocked to gold funds this year, especially since Sept. 11?
Investor interest in gold has increased somewhat over the past year, given its strong performance. Gold's
robust performance at a time when many other stock groups have declined has underscored the metal's
usefulness in providing diversification to portfolios.
In the third quarter, $185 million in new money flowed into gold funds -- including $140 million in September
alone, according to Lipper. That was the largest monthly inflow for the sector in more than two years, says
Lipper analyst Donald Cassidy. But even with the uptick in gold's popularity lately, fund investors willing to

--- Page 155 ---
Page 155 of 243 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
put their money into gold are relatively few. As of Sept. 30, gold funds had total assets of $1.97 billion, a
"tiny" proportion of the $3 trillion invested in all U.S. stock funds, Mr. Cassidy says.
Given their shining performance, what's the outlook for gold funds?
Gold's rallies in recent years have usually been fleeting, so unless someone was already invested in the
sector before Sept. 11, there may be limited profit potential left in the current crisis, Mr. Davis says.
Still, strategists and fund managers say low interest rates will boost the gold price as long as rates remain
below the pace of inflation -- a situation that discourages people from holding on to dollars.
In the meantime, no one is expecting any gold-supply crunch. After years of lagging prices, every major
producer has mining projects on hold, waiting to reopen them should gold prices rally high enough.
What should I look for when selecting a gold fund?
Focus on established funds with experienced managers. All gold funds will be affected by the level of
interest rates, but also look for funds investing in companies that haven't "hedged" against their physical
metal production, says Frank Holmes, chairman and chief executive of U.S. Global Investors Inc., who
manages the Gold Shares Fund.
Gold-mining companies use hedges to lock in a current gold price in case the market falls while mines are
still producing. Mr. Holmes and other fund managers say the companies whose shares are best positioned to
continue rising are those that haven't placed such bearish bets.
Among them: Johannesburg-based Gold Fields, the world's second-largest producer of the metal. Investors
wanting to know which stocks a gold fund holds in its portfolio can check the fund prospectus available from
the fund sponsor or by visiting the fund company's Web site.
Why buy gold funds or stock in mining companies instead of the metal itself?
Joseph M. Foster, manager of Van Eck International Investors Gold Fund, tells clients they should consider
buying gold funds or mining-company shares the same as owning metal that is still in the ground. He says
that makes them a relatively convenient way to invest in the metal itself, considering that owning actual gold
coins or bullion carries the additional expense of storage and insurance.
"For a mining company, the Earth is a vault," he says. "Any gold-price increase is applied to all of the ounces
the company has in the ground that will be produced over many years."
What are the downsides to owning gold funds?
Volatility, for starters. Mining stocks, whether owned directly or through gold funds are perhaps three times
as volatile as gold's commodity price, Mr. Foster says.
Indeed, investors need iron-clad stomachs to tolerate gold-fund swings. Despite their gains this year, gold
funds lost 17% on average in 2000, and have posted average declines of 15% a year over the past five
years, according to Morningstar.
Morningstar's Mr. Davis notes that with relatively few mining companies for gold funds to invest in, the group
is highly susceptible to swings in an individual company's stock price. He also points out that the fees
charged investors in gold funds are high, averaging 2.2% a year compared with 1.42% for all U.S. equities
funds and 1.8% for international funds.
How can I tell if a gold fund has done well?
By far, the most watched gold index of mining-company stocks is the Philadelphia Stock Exchange
Gold/Silver Sector Index, which tracks 11 companies and is used by many precious-metals funds as a
benchmark for their performance. It is up 8.6%, or 4.42 points, so far this year, to 55.83 and has been
rebalanced since Sept. 11 to focus more intently on gold instead of other precious metals.
The Chicago Board Options Exchange Gold Index is another closely watched indicator, although even the
best gold funds are unlikely to duplicate its performance in 2001. It has soared 35%, or 10.87 points, to
40.51. The added volatility of the CBOE index is a result of its smaller makeup of only nine stocks, and the
fact that all are equally weighted, leaving the index more susceptible to fluctuations in a single stock. Notably,
the 58.9% rally of Toronto-based producer Agnico-Eagle Mines so far this year has had five times the effect
on the CBOE index as the Philadelphia one.

--- Page 156 ---
Page 156 of 243 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Of course, the major indexes and mining shares often move in sympathy with the metal's commodity price.
The benchmark U.S. gold trading takes place on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange,
where futures have risen 3.2%, or 8.80, to $280.80 this year.
If I'm going to buy a gold fund, how much of my portfolio should I allocate it?
Most managers say between 5% and 10% is a good target to provide some portfolio diversification. Of
course, investors who believe the worst is yet to come for the economy, the terrorism war and the future in
general may want to take a chance and put a bigger bet on gold funds.
"There's a good case for gold being a portfolio diversifier," says Morningstar's Mr. Davis, a gold bear who
recommends allocating less than 5%. "It will definitely outperform if everything else goes south. But you have
to decide what you think the odds of that are."

Brazilians Rue FadingOf the Jangadeiros,Storied Men of the Sea---A Traditional Fishery FloundersIn the Wake of Technology;At War With the Di...
Brazilians Rue Fading Of the Jangadeiros, Storied Men of the Sea --- A Traditional Fishery Flounders
In the Wake of Technology; At War With the Dive Boats
By Miriam Jordan
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
1,350 words
1 November 2001
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
(Copyright (c) 2001, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
PRAINHA DO CANTO VERDE, Brazil -- Beto de Lima's 20-foot wooden fishing boat looks something like an
overgrown windsurfer, yet it climbs deftly up set after set of towering Atlantic waves.
Until, that is, one wave rocks it violently and Mr. De Lima's crew members shout an alarm: an oar has slipped
out of the boat and been sucked into a whirlpool. Mr. De Lima plunges into the water to retrieve it,
disappearing for several minutes beneath the white foam.
It's just another day at the office.
Mr. De Lima is a jangadeiro, captain of a jangada (pronounced jung-AH-da), a compact, storied sailing vessel
that has been ferrying Brazilian fishermen to far away lobstering and fishing grounds since the 16th century.
Jangada fishing has always been grueling, dangerous work, pitting small craft and tough, ingenious sailors
against a fickle and sometimes treacherous sea. Of late, though, jangadeiros have faced obstacles far more
fierce than the ocean: competition from motorized dive boats, often manned by poorly trained human divers.
The boats use illegal fishing techniques that are stripping Brazil's fishing grounds of their stocks, and
threatening to put an end to Brazil's historic jangada fleet.
Consider this trip: Mr. De Lima and his crew will return with only three pounds of lobster after two nights and
three days at sea. Pile, Beto's father, says that in the 1960s he used to return from such voyages with more
than 200 pounds. Unlike his father, who fed his wife and 15 children on fishing earnings, Beto must make and
sell fruit popsicles in his spare time to earn enough money to support a family of three.
"Fishing has been in the family for 200 years," says Mr. De Lima. "Now, our ability to build a future as
fishermen is fading away."
The De Limas are hardly alone. In Cumbuco and Canoa Quebrada, once two of the largest fishing villages
along the lobster-rich coast of the northeast Brazilian state of Ceara, only about 10 jangadas remain,
compared with about 60 two decades ago. In economically desperate villages such as Fontes Beach, also in
Ceara, land speculators have bought up the beaches where traditional fishermen launch their boats, putting
up condos and hotels and crowding jangadeiros into an area resembling a slum.
Rather than following in the footsteps of their fathers, many of Prainha's youth are migrating to Brazil's cities
to take jobs stocking supermarket shelves and sweeping hotel lobbies. Others are risking their lives to earn
fast cash in the disease-ridden gold mines of the Amazon. Maimede Dantas, a Prainha fisherman who has
agitated for jangadeiro rights, says he has "lost" two of his sons to the allure of Brazil's biggest city, Sao
Paulo.
"The jangadeiro will become extinct before the lobster," predicts Adauto Fontele, a marine-life researcher at
the Federal University of Ceara.
What makes the jangadeiros' slow demise especially sad for many Brazilians is the central role the fishermen
have played in national lore and political protest movements. In the 1940s, four jangadeiros captured the
imagination of the country -- and of American film director Orson Welles -- by sailing 3,500 miles from a
northeastern village to Rio de Janeiro to demand that Brazil's military dictator grant basic labor rights to
fishermen. Mr. Welles came to Brazil to film a documentary about the jangadeiros' expedition, but shooting
stopped when the fishermen's leader, known as Jacare, was lost at sea while trying to recreate the voyage on
film.
"Jangadeiros are warriors of the sea," says Francisco Moreira, president of Terramar, a group that works with
traditional fishermen in Ceara, Brazil's largest lobster-producing region.

--- Page 158 ---
Page 158 of 243 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
In some cases these days, the jangadeiros are literally fighting back against what they see as the predations
of dive boats, whose divers use weighted nets or long rods of baited hooks to catch hundreds of pounds of
lobsters in a few hours, often breaking up reefs in the process. Moreover, Brazilian coast-guard officials say
they are fielding an increasing number of complaints by traditional fishermen who accuse dive boats of trying
to ram jangadas and sabotage harvests by yanking out their lobster traps. Mr. De Lima, in fact, in an incident
recorded by the coast guard earlier this year, said he narrowly thwarted an effort by a dive boat to run him
down, by tossing out a rope that became tangled in the attackers' propeller.
In the face of such incidents, clashes between jangadeiros and dive boats have escalated, and some
traditional fishermen have struck back by commandeering dive boats at sea and sinking or burning them.
Some see this as a critical juncture for a craft and fishing style dating back to native Indians when the
Portuguese arrived in northeast Brazil in the 16th century. The name comes from an association -- the boats
reminded the European settlers of sailing rafts they had seen in India, known as janga.
The fishermen have long been known for their social activism as well as their bravery. Back in the 19th
century, a jangadeiro known as "Sea Dragon" was a prominent activist against the slave trade here. The
jangadeiros' fatalistic courage often inspired Brazilian songwriters and poets. "It's sweet to die in the sea,"
says one song, "in the green waves of the sea."
Jangadas have changed only a little over the centuries. They are now constructed with planks of wood rather
than balsa wood logs. Every piece of the vessel is handmade, except the anchor, which is made of stone.
The raft is steered from a simple wooden bench positioned at the end of the vessel. It is launched from
beaches by crew members rolling the craft on logs across the sand.
To keep the sail taut -- and better able to endure the wind -- one crew member often splashes water onto it
using a coconut shell. Two of the four crew members stand on the edge of the boat, tugging on thick ropes
attached to the vessel and using their weight to keep it on an even keel. The jangadeiros know by the color of
the water where to lay their traps. The traps, made of twigs and wire mesh, look a little like rectangular
birdcages. "We memorize the reference points near the lobster territory," says Joao Fernandes, 40. "I don't
need a compass or a navigation system."
Still, jangadeiros' fishing prowess has proved to be no match for the highly mechanized dive boats, which first
began appearing in the mid-1980s and proliferated in the past decade in response to escalating prices on
international lobster markets. In fact, fisheries experts here say the U.S., as the biggest importer of Brazilian
lobsters, is unwittingly contributing to the demise of the jangadeiros because of its appetite for small lobster
tails.
Though Brazilian law bans the capture of lobsters with tails smaller than five inches -- catching immature
lobsters jeopardizes future harvests by taking the crustaceans out of the system before they can reproduce --
the law is widely flouted by the divers, and enforcement is lax, environmental groups say. Many, in fact,
blame such poaching for a 61% decline in Brazil's lobster population during the past decade.
"The U.S. has created a market for really small tails, so back in Brazil they keep catching them," says Paul
Raymond, a special agent specializing in lobster trade at the U.S. Department of Commerce.
This has all made for desperate times. In the village of Canoa Quebrada, jangadeiros not long ago
exchanged plots of beachfront land where they once docked their boats for a boom box or a few dollars
offered by foreigners. "We don't fish for real fish anymore," says jangadeiro Ze de Luca. "We fish for tourists."
(See related letter: "Letters to the Editor: Abandoned Welles Film Resurrected on Video" -- WSJ Nov. 14,
2001)

Bond and Other `Stodgy' Funds Come Back
Fund Track
Bond and Other `Stodgy' Funds Come Back
By Bridget O'Brian
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
989 words
19 October 2001
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
(Copyright (c) 2001, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
As recently as 18 months ago, they were the tortoises. Now they're the hares.
They are the slow-but-steady mutual funds that got lapped in the late 1990s as returns on technology and
growth funds zoomed sky high. Now, these decidedly unsexy investments have an all-too-rare attribute -- a
plus sign in front of their performance numbers.
That has given new luster to once-unloved groups such as bond, stable-value and gold funds, and other
investment vehicles such as fixed annuities.
"It's not all that surprising that in the past they were shunned, because the equity markets provided 10%,
20%, 30%-a-year returns," said Mark Marinella, co-chief investment officer for fixed income at State Street
Research & Management. "That certainly is not the norm."
Indeed, the average U.S. diversified stock fund is down 18.66% so far this year, according to Morningstar
Inc., and the average tech fund has lost 48.41%, making categories such as bond funds, with a 7.57% return
so far this year, shine in comparison.
"The word stodgy is not something we hear about anymore," said Peter Wall, senior vice president of product
development and research at J.P. Morgan Chase.
A half-generation of investors brought up on rising stock prices may be hard-pressed about how to navigate
this new, lower-octane investing scenario. Here's a look at some of the less glamorous but now-profitable
options.
BOND FUNDS
What a difference a year makes. In 2000, investors pulled a net $54 billion out of taxable bond funds,
according to AMG Data of Arcata, Calif.; this year, they have plowed in to the tune of $67 billion in new
money as of Oct. 10.
Bond-fund returns haven't changed much, but investor expectations have. As Chris Towle, portfolio manager
of Lord Abbett & Co.'s bond-debenture fund, put it, "How boring is that 8% return?"
But as bonds differ, so do bond funds, so look carefully at what the funds invest in. "In this environment, you
want to steer clear of lower-quality and corporate issues, and steer toward higher quality, such as Treasury
bonds," said Brent R. Harris, chairman of Pimco Funds. Pimco also likes GNMA securities, as well as the
Treasury Inflation Protected Bonds, or TIPs.
For investors who don't want to decide among bonds, there are funds such as Pimco Total Return, the $49
billion fund that selects among all bond categories. Its return this year has been 9.7%.
STABLE-VALUE FUNDS
These steady-Eddie funds have never had a negative performance year since they started in the 1930s,
according to the Stable Value Investment Association. For 2001 through September, they posted an
annualized return of 6.23%, according Hueler Cos., a data provider in Minneapolis; the 10-year return is
6.48%.
Available almost exclusively as an option in employer-sponsored retirement plans, rather than directly for the
public, stable-value funds invest in high-quality corporate bonds, Treasury bonds or mortgage-backed

--- Page 160 ---
Page 160 of 243 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
securities. Stable-value funds also have the same liquidity as money-market funds, meaning investors usually
can move in and out of them with whatever interest is accrued.
"A lot of investors now are looking at stable value as a way of stabilizing their portfolios and viewing it as a
safe harbor and anchor in their portfolios," said Mike Wyatt, president of T. Rowe Price Stable Value Asset
Management, a unit of T. Rowe Price Associates.
GOLD FUNDS
While hardly in the slow-but-steady category, these highly volatile funds are the traditional investment of
choice in times of turmoil. "War, inflation, a nasty domestic stock market, any of those tend to drive people to
gold," said Don Cassidy, senior analyst at Lipper Inc., the fund-tracking data firm. And with a year-to-date
return of 15.14%, it's easy to see why.
Some investment advisers suggest that gold funds are a good idea for a small portion of most portfolios as
an antidote to world tensions. But while shining in the short run, precious-metals funds lost an average of
17.01% in 2000, and their longer-term record has seriously lagged behind other sectors.
CLOSED-END MUNI BOND FUNDS
These have been the fund world's double-digit wonders this year.
Unlike their open-end siblings, which issue new shares and buy them back on demand, closed-end funds
issue a fixed number of shares that trade on an exchange. Municipal-bond funds save investors federal
income taxes, and depending on where the investor lives, and what the portfolio contains, can also be free of
state and local taxes.
"For investors looking for a hedge against declining interest rates, there's no better investment," says Bill
Fitzgerald, managing director of Nuveen Investments, a unit of the John Nuveen Co. "You get the
appreciation of the bonds plus the potential for increased tax-free income as short-term rates decline."
FIXED ANNUITIES
These tax-deferred investments offer investors a guaranteed rate of return, typically tied to corporate bonds
or some other underlying investment, as well as a return of principal.
Sales were moribund a few years ago, as investors flocked to variable annuities, which offer stock-investing
options. Now, some of the most popular annuities are fixed five-year instruments, such as the Gold 5 annuity
offered by Jefferson-Pilot Life Insurance Co., which guarantees 5.2% over five years.
But avoid longer-term annuities, some advisers say. "You want to be careful that there isn't a bottom in the
next few years and rates come up again," said Jeff Oster, president of Client Preservation, a San Francisco
annuity-marketing company. Also, read the prospectus, to make sure the rate is guaranteed for the length of
the annuity.

Wild Card:As Pakistan, India JoinU.S. in Fighting Terror,Kashmir Gets in Way---Islamabad Opposes al Qaeda,But What of MilitantsIt Support...
Wild Card: As Pakistan, India Join U.S. in Fighting Terror, Kashmir Gets in Way --- Islamabad Opposes
al Qaeda, But What of Militants It Supports Next Door? --- Mr. Dar's Unfinished Agenda
By Peter Fritsch
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
1,972 words
12 October 2001
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
(Copyright (c) 2001, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
RAWALPINDI, Pakistan -- The most important event in Mohammed Ahsan Dar's life happened six years
before he was born. One day in 1953, he says, police patrolling the Indian-controlled part of Kashmir
pistol-whipped his father, rendering him "half mad until he found peace in death."
An inbred hatred of India and a fierce Islamic nationalism made a killer of Mr. Dar. First he founded a militant
youth group to oppose mostly Hindu India's influence in mostly Muslim Kashmir. Landing in an Indian prison,
he says he was tortured for months before escaping in 1989.
Next, parlaying the fame of his jailbreak into a recruiting tool, he assembled a band of fierce anti-India
fighters. Again imprisoned by India, he says he languished in an eight-by-five-foot cell before winning release
two years ago.
This week, as U.S. cruise missiles struck nearby Afghanistan, Mr. Dar was once again preparing to do
guerrilla battle for his cause, Muslim control of all of Kashmir. "This fight is my unfinished agenda. And the
attack of Afghanistan just made me even more of a problem for you in the U.S.," Mr. Dar said, sitting on the
floor of his home near an Afghan refugee camp.
The existence of Muslim guerrillas inside Pakistan does indeed put the U.S. in a difficult position. The U.S.
badly needs Pakistani support for its global alliance against terrorism, and has it. But while the Western world
applauds Pakistan for its stance, the country remains a dangerous crucible for extremists prosecuting a jihad
of their own. The decades-long struggle to control Kashmir has taken an estimated 30,000 lives.
The latest episode, an explosion in Indian-controlled Kashmir Oct. 1 that killed about 40, sent tensions even
higher between Pakistan and another key U.S. ally in the anti-terror coalition, India. The Indians, increasingly
angry with Pakistan over the Kashmir issue, say that if the U.S. is serious about attacking the roots of terror, it
should expand its terrorism dragnet to Pakistan.
Secretary of State Colin Powell will have to walk this diplomatic high wire on a visit early next week to
Pakistan and India. His mission to the two nuclear neighbors suggests a new urgency for the West in trying to
resolve one of the world's most intractable territorial disputes.
Those seeking to oust India from Kashmir want to turn back the clock to before the 1947 partition of India,
which created the Muslim state of Pakistan and left much of Kashmir in Indian hands despite the region's
Muslim majority. Today, many of the anti-India fighters are young men who were trained in Afghanistan with
U.S. weapons left over from the anti-Soviet war -- in some cases in training camps run by Osama bin Laden.
A score of militant Muslim groups sponsor and carry out brutal acts of terror in Indian-controlled Kashmir,
which they say is payback for decades of abuse by Indian police and military forces.
Pakistan openly gives moral support to some of these groups. Western diplomats and former Pakistani
intelligence officials say Pakistan also gives financial and covert military support to a few of the groups,
though Pakistan denies this. A U.S. House of Representatives Task Force on Terrorism said in 1993 that
Pakistan was sponsoring and promoting separatism and terrorism, "primarily in Kashmir, as a strategic and
long-term program."
In Pakistan, many draw a distinction between terror and freedom fighting when it comes to Kashmir. "The
Pakistani government exerts an influence on these mujahedeen groups. And why shouldn't it if they are
legitimate freedom fighters?" asks Shireen Mazari, director of a Pakistan-government think tank called the
Institute for Strategic Studies.
Claiming responsibility for the Oct. 1 explosion in Indian-controlled Kashmir, in the city of Srinagar, was a
Pakistan-based group called Jaish-e-Mohammed. Some of its fighters have been trained in Afghan camps

--- Page 162 ---
Page 162 of 243 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
linked to al Qaeda, the Afghan-based network run by Osama bin Laden. A relative newcomer to the Kashmiri
war theater, Jaish-e-Mohammed is fed by Islamic schools in Pakistan, where its leader, Masood Azhar, is a
hero to many of the nation's idle disaffected.
Pakistan's Foreign Office condemned the Srinagar bombing, yet did so with an edge: "This act of terrorism is
especially reprehensible," it said, "as it appears to be aimed at maligning the legitimate struggle of the
Kashmiri people for their self-determination." Such varnish, in the view of India and others, suggests an
implicit support for brutal methods in a forgotten region at a time when all eyes are trained westward on
Afghanistan.
Pakistan's president, Gen. Pervez Musharraf, responded angrily Monday to Indian Prime Minister Atal Bihari
Vajpayee's allegations that Pakistan continues to sponsor terrorism in Kashmir. India responded coolly to a
subsequent phone call from Mr. Musharraf to Mr. Vajpayee inviting fresh talks on the Kashmir conflict.
Indeed, Indian officials suggested that another attack such as that in Srinagar could, in certain circumstances,
lead India to launch military strikes against camps in Pakistan where Kashmiri militants train.
Such a bellicose exchange between two heavily armed powers has the U.S. on edge and is quickly moving
Kashmir back onto the West's radar screen. British Prime Minister Tony Blair has pleaded for cool heads on
the Kashmir issue and said the conflict should be addressed by the West as part of the long-term campaign
against terrorism.
While Pakistan denies that Muslim Kashmiri militants thrive on its soil, that's not the story suggested by visits
to the poor neighborhoods of Rawalpindi. A rundown hotel restaurant, advertising Pepsi in giant block letters,
is one of many local rallying points for mujahedeen fighting in Kashmir.
Donation boxes, though recently declared illegal here, are stuffed with five-rupee notes worth about eight
cents each. The boxes belong to Jaish-e-Mohammed and to Lashkar-i-Tayyba. The second group, whose
name means "Army of the Pure," is a Kashmir group that claims to have sent thousands of fighters to help the
Taliban resist the U.S.
"Look around," says the restaurant's cashier. "These boxes are full all over town. Ask a Pakistani to
contribute five rupees for the struggle in Kashmir and he will give you 10."
Nearby, a safe house for the group Harkatul Mujahedeen functions openly, although visitors are discouraged.
"It is wise for you to go quickly," says a guard. The U.S. branded Harkatul a terrorist organization in 1995
after it kidnapped five Western tourists in Kashmir, beheading a Norwegian. In 1998, several Harkatul
militants died in U.S. missile attacks on Mr. bin Laden's Afghan hideouts.
A man named Pervez Ahmad freely discusses Pakistan government support for his own organization, the
Kashmir Liberation Cell, whose activities, he says, are limited to pamphleteering and organizing street
protests. "We are like an NGO [nongovernmental organization] like any other, supported by the government
of Pakistan-administered Kashmir," Mr. Ahmad says in clean English, flashing a diamond-encrusted gold
watch. Two former high-ranking Pakistan government officials say the Kashmir Liberation Cell is supported by
Pakistan's intelligence service, ISI.
While Mr. Ahmad says his group does no fighting, he adds that he counts among his friends men he
describes as "mujahedeen warlords," among them Mr. Dar. Mr. Ahmad reaches a few warlords on their cell
phones, trying to arrange a meeting with outsiders, but apologizes that "the jihad council has decided it does
not wish to speak at this time."
Government support for the Kashmir struggle is suggested, too, on the so-called Line of Control separating
the parts of Kashmir administered separately by Pakistan and India. A doctor who works with the Pakistan
army there says that he sometimes treats wounded mujahedeen and that the army provides covering fire for
incursions into India launched by militant groups. "It would be inhumane not to provide such cover when the
mujahedeen are attacked by India," he says.
The ties among Pakistani, Kashmiri and Afghan militants trace back to the early 1980s, when Pakistan
allowed entry to anyone arriving to fight the Soviet army in Afghanistan. Pakistan's ISI intelligence service and
a radical Islamic political party called Jamaat-e-Islami organized reception committees, receiving funds from
Saudi Arabia to funnel fighters to the Afghan resistance. An estimated 35,000 Muslim fighters from 43
countries arrived under that covert program between 1982 and 1992. Many of their weapons were made in
the U.S.
When the Soviets turned tail in 1989, some of these fighters trained their sights on Kashmir, where simmering
discontent with India's sometimes-harsh rule erupted into full-scale rebellion. As men like Mr. Dar took up
arms, many Kashmiri fighters crossed into Pakistan for more succor and more sophisticated military training.

--- Page 163 ---
Page 163 of 243 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
"We lacked experience and technical support," says Mr. Dar, who in 1989 founded a group called Hizbul
Mujahedeen. "But what we needed even more was an umbrella group to support us."
He found that in Jamaat-e-Islami, Pakistan's oldest and best-connected Islamic political party, known as JI.
But gradually, what for Mr. Dar and his 20,000 followers began as a purely Kashmiri movement for
independence was taken over by Pakistan and used as a political bludgeon to check India.
When the U.S. a decade ago considered branding Pakistan as a state sponsor of terrorism, say military
analysts, Pakistan's government moved Kashmir militant groups to eastern Afghanistan. It shifted
responsibility for their training to the JI. Then in 1996, when Mr. bin Laden arrived in Afghanistan, the
suspected terror leader established training camps for Kashmir fighters in the Afghan city of Khost. This
move, say analysts, cemented Pakistan's support for Mr. bin Laden's hosts, the fundamentalist Taliban rulers
of most of Afghanistan.
Pakistan's JI party has been particularly vocal since the Sept. 11 attacks in the U.S., hanging shrill
anti-Semitic banners around Pakistan's major cities. Other propaganda blasts the U.S. as "unrighteous,
seditious, arrogant." JI spiritual leader Qazi Hussain Ahmad recently made the chilling statement that if U.S.
ground forces enter Afghanistan, Pakistan's nuclear program "cannot remain safe."
Although Pakistani President Musharraf banned radical organizations months ago and recently placed a
virulent pro-Taliban religious leader under house arrest, Western diplomats say his government has been
reluctant to crack down on groups supporting the Kashmir cause or to attack their sources of funding.
To Mr. Dar's way of thinking, it is time for those fighting for the independence of Kashmir to emerge from the
shadow of Pakistan's influence and get back to their roots. "Our cause wasn't created in Pakistan," he says.
"It's created from our own soil."
Mr. Dar, now 42, says he wants to return to his family's fruit orchards in Kashmir's lush valleys, resuming the
schoolteacher career he abandoned long ago. But until Kashmir is free of Indian control, he plans to train his
four sons in the life and tactics of the mujahedeen. "I will do this for my father," he says, jabbing the air with
an angry finger. "You in America will call me a terrorist, but I am a freedom fighter. You will pay for forgetting
me."
