Reasons to Cheer Despite a Stock Selloff---Industrials Drop7.13%, as NYSEIs Able to Open
Monday's Markets
Reasons to Cheer Despite a Stock Selloff --- Industrials Drop 7.13%, as NYSE Is Able to Open
By E.S. Browning and Ken Brown
Staff Reporters of The Wall Street Journal
2,231 words
18 September 2001
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
(Copyright (c) 2001, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
In the most active day in New York Stock Exchange history, the Dow Jones Industrial Average and other
major indexes fell hard on the first day of trading since last week's terrorism attacks.
Though many investors and market officials were cheered that trading was able to resume at all, given the
logistical hurdles, the declines -- including a 7.13%, or 684.81-point, slide in the Dow Jones Industrial
Average -- pushed stocks clearly into a bear market by common definition.
Airline, travel and insurance stocks took the hardest hit in the wake of last week's World Trade Center
attacks. The vast majority of shares fell sharply on both the New York Stock Exchange and the Nasdaq Stock
Market.
Those declines largely overshadowed what were seen as glimmers of hope throughout the day. The fact that
the markets worked, considering the bare-bones services available in lower Manhattan, was an achievement.
Aside from scattered telephone problems at some Wall Street trading houses, market experts say the
exchanges operated smoothly.
"There are a lot of behind-the-scenes heroes," says Matthew deSalvo, head of North American stock trading
at Morgan Stanley. "The fact we were able to do a complete day of trading without any disruptions is a home
run for the marketplace."
The day was grueling. Traders at Morgan Stanley showed up for work by 6:30 a.m. Eastern time and were
still there at 5 p.m., wrapping up final details and getting ready for today's session. They didn't leave their
desks: The firm ordered in lunch, pizza for the traders handling NYSE stocks and Chinese food for those
making markets in Nasdaq stocks.
Mr. deSalvo hopes that today, the Jewish holiday of Rosh Hashanah, will be quieter and that the market will
trade in a narrow band. He looks forward to the day investors are finally able to once again focus their
attention on fundamentals: interest rates and corporate earnings. "We just really needed to make it through
today," he says.
Still, many analysts said that given the devastating impact the attacks could have on the economy, the fact
that the markets didn't fall further was seen as a relief. Despite their sharp decline, the Dow industrials
finished up from their afternoon lows, when they were down more than 700 points.
Rare advancers were tobacco shares, such as Philip Morris, defense stocks such as Northrop Grumman and
gold stocks such as Newmont Mining.
The good news was rare, though, on a day when traders had to navigate past a war zone and armed soldiers
to get to work. The industrials' fall put the index at 8920.70, down 23.9% from the closing record of 11722.98,
set on Jan. 14, 2000. It was the first time the industrials had been down more than 20% since 1990. By the
definition that a 20% decline confirms the onset of a bear market, this marked the first time since 1990 that all
major U.S. indexes were in a bear market.
The Nasdaq Composite fell 6.83%, or 115.83 points, to 1579.55, leaving it down 68.7% from its record of
5048.62, set on March 10 of last year. The Standard & Poor's 500-stock index dropped 4.92%, or 53.77
points, to 1038.77.
Severe though the declines were, they were far from the most severe on record in percentage terms.
Although the Dow industrials' decline was the biggest point drop ever, it was the 14th sharpest ever in
percentage terms. The Nasdaq drop was the 11th heaviest in percentage terms. The worst percentage drop
ever for the Dow industrials was 22.6% during the 508-point crash of Oct. 19, 1987 (due to the market's lower

--- Page 186 ---
Page 186 of 243 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
level back then, point moves had a bigger percentage impact than they do now). Despite yesterday's decline,
the industrials' value remains more than five times that of the 1987 low, which explains why the percentage
drop is so much less.
The Dow Jones Transportation Index, crushed by falling airline stocks, suffered its second-heaviest
percentage loss ever, exceeded only by the decline of 17% during the crash of 1987.
Professional investors said the day's trading was skewed by panic selling, by an extraordinary
government-inspired effort to prop up the market, and by the decision of many pros to step aside and delay
selling or buying until things settle down. It could be days or weeks, they say, before the volatility subsides
and a clear market direction emerges. The one thing that appeared clear, they said, was that the next few
months will be a difficult period of recession and stock-market volatility.
The markets tumbled almost immediately, with the Dow Jones Industrial Average down sharply in the first 15
minutes of trading. But after the initial fall, many stocks temporarily held firm, leading some investors to hope
-- wrongly -- that the market might have seen its worse. Some of the initial selling, traders said, came from
individual investors. Big institutions such as mutual funds and pension funds sold, but sold carefully, knowing
that a wave of selling could send the market into a tailspin.
Indeed, while Wall Street pundits and most retail brokers were urging investors to stick to their long-term
plans and not trade right away, most people ignored that advice, pummeling airlines such as American
Airlines parent AMR and travel-related stocks like Marriott International. Even Tiffany's shares plunged.
Mitch Rubin, manager of the Baron iOpportunity Fund, says he spent yesterday buying stocks he thinks may
benefit from the anticipated demise of travel, including cable-TV companies, such as Comcast and Charter
Communications, because he says demand for in-home entertainment is likely to be quite strong in the
coming months. He also bought some shares in SmartForce, a Redwood City, Calif., company that makes
software for online education seminars. He is betting the company will benefit from companies declining to
send their employees to trade shows and seminars as they have before.
Many money managers said their heart just wasn't in it today, though they knew they had an obligation to
their clients. For example, Michael Stead, chief investment officer at SIFE Trust Fund, a Walnut Creek, Calif.,
mutual fund that specializes in financial stocks, spent part of his time raising money for a charity to aid the
families of employees of Keefe, Bruyette & Woods, a broker that lost many of its executives and analysts.
Despite those kind of pauses, though, the selling yesterday was heavy and deep.
Traders said some mutual funds were selling to cover redemptions by investors, but many mutual-fund
portfolio managers said they were limiting their activity because the volatility and the artificial nature of the
trading made it difficult to make long-term investments.
Meanwhile, some mutual-fund companies, dealing with high volatility, had trouble sending pricing data for
their portfolios in time to make the tables in newspapers, according to Lipper Inc., a New York fund-tracking
firm. About 1,300 stock and bond portfolios were affected, according to Lipper.
Traders said once the market failed to rally in the late morning, some short sellers also moved in, driving
stock prices down further. Short sellers bet on stock drops by borrowing stock, selling it and hoping to buy it
back at a lower price later.
For the next week or so, institutional investors are going do their part to help preserve an orderly market,
managers say. But, over time, hedge funds and mutual funds "will do what is right for their clients," the
hedge-fund manager added, which means they will sell the stock of any company that appears to be
weakened.
James Paulsen, chief investment officer at Wells Capital Management in Minneapolis, added that a sense of
patriotism may have prevented some further selling, but "that fades away. This is capitalism. People are not
going to sit idly by and not get their portfolios where they are supposed to be." As a result, he says, the
market "could go lower in days to come."
Jeff Benton, chief operating officer of the Performance Specialist Group, said a lot of investors spent the day
"waiting for the freedom rally -- the patriotic rally -- that just didn't come today." He attributed the selloff at
least in part to the U.S. markets' "need to catch up to Europe and Asia." Still, he added, "this has been very
orderly."
There was some optimism amid the gloom. Some said the half-point cut in interest rates that the Federal
Reserve announced before the market opened, plus the boost in government spending, would give the
economy the juice it needed for a quicker rebound.

--- Page 187 ---
Page 187 of 243 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Others started to see value among select names in the most beaten-down industries. For example, Mike
Weinstein, an insurance analyst at Putnam Investments in Boston, said that while American International
Group, the big insurer, fell 4.4%, it already has said its losses won't exceed $500 million. He adds that
historically insurers have recouped more in premium price boosts than they have lost in paying out on claims
after catastrophic events.
Given that the market remains expensive, many fund managers are wondering where to invest. Shigeki
Makino, a senior portfolio manager with Putnam says that generally he and other fund managers are
discouraged from holding more than 5% of their funds in cash, but he hasn't found a lot of buying
opportunities in yesterday's drop. Indeed, many of the industries that were hit the hardest -- besides airlines
and the like -- were among the most expensive in the market. Semiconductors, for example, fell 8.8%.
In spite of the sinking Dow Jones Industrial Average, by noon the day's market activity was "the best that can
be expected," said Danny Trimble, a broker with Salomon Smith Barney on the floor of the NYSE. "It's tough.
But we're all a unit here," he said. In a show of patriotism, some brokers were wearing jackets or caps with
the stars and stripes on them, and miniature American flags flew from some specialists' posts.
Overall, the market value of U.S. stocks has fallen 35% on paper, or about $5.93 trillion, to about $11.03
trillion, since its high of about $16.96 trillion on March 24, 2000, based on the market value of the companies
in the Wilshire 5000 index. That includes the loss for investors yesterday of about $590.81 billion, or 5.08%.
The Wilshire 5000 includes about 7,200 companies, or nearly every publicly traded company with
headquarters in the U.S.
Outside the U.S., stocks declined in dollar terms. The Dow Jones World Stock Index, excluding U.S. stocks,
fell 3%, or 4.95 points, to 160.21. In major U.S. market action:
Stocks sagged. On the Big Board, where a record 2.37 billion shares traded, just 608 stocks rose and 2,675
fell.
Bonds declined. In a shortened session, the 10-year Treasury note fell 12/32, or $3.75 for each $1,000
invested. The yield, which moves inversely to the bond's price, rose to 4.60%. The 30-year bond was down
18/32 to yield 5.40%.
The dollar was mixed. Late in New York, it rose to 117.70 yen from 117.22, but the euro rose to 92.39 cents
from 92.11 cents.
---
Suzanne McGee, Henny Sender, Mitchell Pacelle, Jeff D. Opdyke and Aaron Lucchetti contributed to this
article.

From Overseas, Hopeful Signs for Stocks---Dollar Stabilizes;U.S. Bond TradeAlso Resumes
Thursday's Markets
From Overseas, Hopeful Signs for Stocks --- Dollar Stabilizes; U.S. Bond Trade Also Resumes
By E.S. Browning and Scott Kilman
Staff Reporters of The Wall Street Journal
1,023 words
14 September 2001
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
(Copyright (c) 2001, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
U.S. stock markets remained closed, but jittery American investors saw some glimmers of hope in the
performance of U.S. bonds and of foreign stocks.
Foreign stock markets largely rebounded from previous declines, the dollar was stable, and the resumption of
U.S. bond-market trading, after a two-day hiatus, was orderly and reassuring, traders said.
"I think it probably bodes well for an orderly opening on the U.S. stock market," said Graham Allen, chief bond
officer at Wells Capital Management, Wells Fargo's money-management arm.
There were plenty of signs that investor jitters remain, of course. Initial bond trading was volatile and heavy.
The gap between the prices of corporate bonds and Treasury bonds widened, a clear indication that investors
were switching out of riskier corporate securities in favor of Treasury bonds. And investors showed a clear
preference for the safest Treasury bonds, with two-year notes rising strongly, to their most extreme levels in
the 29 years that the Treasury has regularly been issuing that maturity, traders said. That led some investors
to conclude that there could be a similar flight to safer, more defensive stocks once U.S. stocks start trading,
as early as Monday.
But even that is hard to predict. The Toronto Stock Exchange, for example, resumed trading after its own
two-day halt, and rose 0.77%, an unexpectedly bullish sign. Furthermore, safety stocks such as gold-mining
and oil concerns fell, as riskier technology and banking stocks rose. European and Asian markets rebounded
after sharp declines earlier in the week, with German and British markets in particular staging strong
recoveries.
"I don't think it would be a shocker if the first day we opened stocks up," said Richard Nash, chief market
strategist at Victory Capital Management, the money management arm of Keycorp in Cleveland. He said he
could imagine "patriotic investors who didn't want to add to the panic. Instead of getting a knee-jerk negative
reaction, we might get a positive or neutral reaction on the first day."
But he added that the first day could be somewhat meaningless for the longer run. "What's important is what
is going to happen after the first buzz has worn off," he said.
On that score, there was more cause for worry. The University of Michigan released its latest consumer
sentiment survey, which indicated that through Monday, the day before the terrorist attacks, consumer
confidence had fallen sharply, raising more worries about the ability of the economy to escape recession.
One of the many unknown factors was the importance of government intervention. One reason for the orderly
markets, and, probably, for the foreign-stock gains, was that central banks pumped billions of dollars into
financial systems to make sure they operated smoothly. Although the European Central Bank decided
against cutting its benchmark interest rates, U.S. market rates now indicate that investors expect the Fed to
cut its target short-term rates by another half-percentage point at its next meeting or sooner. That could help
stocks and bonds in the short run, but in the longer run, the ability of the economy to recover is likely to be
more important.
The Chicago Mercantile Exchange and the Chicago Board of Trade resumed partial operations under heavy
security. It was the first time in decades the Chicago exchanges, which trade futures contracts on everything
from corn to foreign currency, were closed for so long.
At the Chicago Merc, brokers and clerks had to pass by a platoon of armed guards to get inside the building,
where they were given small American flags.

--- Page 190 ---
Page 190 of 243 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
A few blocks away at the CBOT, bond traders were momentarily baffled at how to restart their market.
Typically, an electronic overnight market has established a price that is used when the buzzer starts trading in
the open outcry pits. But floor trading and electronic trading resumed simultaneously yesterday.
"Up until about a minute before the opening, nobody knew where the market was," said Jerrold Duzenman,
an independent trader, adding: "Everyone was nervous."
But moments after the market opened, markets stabilized. "People are getting tapped on the shoulder and
told they shouldn't do anything unless it is absolutely necessary," said H. Jack Bouroudjian, president of
Commerz Futures.
The day's gradual move toward stability was reflected by the behavior of the 30-year Treasury bond. To the
shock of traders, its price plummeted at the opening, as investors migrated toward the relatively safer
two-year note. But as the day wore on, and nerves calmed, the 30-year bond recouped its losses and finished
strongly ahead. The more widely traded, benchmark 10-year note posted even stronger gains.
Bond traders added that repeated electronic glitches during the day made trading difficult. The devastation of
Cantor Fitzgerald, a key bond-trading firm that helped provide market data and whose headquarters was
destroyed in the World Trade Center attack, may have contributed to some of the glitches.
Investors got some mixed corporate news after the close. Software giant Oracle announced slightly
better-than-expected earnings for its just-ended fiscal quarter, but also announced an unexpectedly large
decline in its flagship database business.
Outside the U.S., stocks advanced in dollar terms. The Dow Jones World Stock Index, excluding U.S. stocks,
rose 0.66%, or 0.80 point, to 121.81.
In major U.S. market action:
The U.S. stock market remained closed.
Bonds gained. In a shortened session that ended at 2 p.m. Eastern time, the 10-year Treasury note rose 1
17/32, or $15.3125 for each $1,000 invested. The yield, which moves inversely to the bond's price, fell to
4.639%. The 30-year bond was up 20/32 to yield 5.396%.
The dollar declined. Late in New York, it fell to 118.81 yen from 119.53, while the euro rose to 91.22 cents
from 90.69 cents.

The World Braces for Economic Blows of Attack---A Survey of the Landscape,From Asia to Argentina;Japan's Special Problem
International
The World Braces for Economic Blows of Attack --- A Survey of the Landscape, From Asia to
Argentina; Japan's Special Problem
By Wall Street Journal staff reporters Christopher Rhoads in Berlin, G. Thomas Sims in Frankfurt, Phred
Dvorak in Tokyo, Jonathan Karp in Sao Paulo, Brazil, and Pamela Druckerman in Buenos Aires
1,531 words
13 September 2001
The Wall Street Journal
J
A14
English
(Copyright (c) 2001, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
As the aftershocks of the worst terrorist attack in U.S. history hit the world yesterday, international policy
makers scrambled to soften the impact on an already weak global economy.
Major central banks pumped more than $80 billion into financial markets to prevent gridlock. Finance
ministers from the U.S., Japan, Germany, Italy and other leading industrialized nations tried to calm mounting
fears that the attack could tip the world into outright recession.
"We are committed to ensuring that this tragedy will not be compounded by disruption to the global
economy," finance ministers and central bankers from the Group of Seven leading industrialized nations said
in a statement.
But with New York and Washington still reeling from the destruction of the World Trade Center and the
grievous damage to the Pentagon, and officials around the world just beginning to grapple with the economic
wreckage, questions outnumbered answers yesterday -- quite apart from the incalculable toll in human lives.
Many of the commodities and currencies that had whipped one way Tuesday -- after terrorists hijacked four
jetliners and crashed three of them into the Trade Center's two towers and the Pentagon -- seesawed back
the other way yesterday. Gold, oil and the euro fell. A number of stock markets in Europe and Asia bounced
back slightly after plunging in the hours after the terror unfolded. In Frankfurt, the Xetra DAX index of German
blue chips rose by 1.4% to 4335.20, and in London, the Financial Times-Stock Exchange 100-share index
rose 2.8% to 4882.10.
Japan, the 800-pound weakling of the world economy, got a shock of the sort it has been dreading ever since
its asset markets began eroding a decade ago. Its benchmark Nikkei 225 Stock Average plunged 6.6% to
9610.10, finishing not quite five points above the latest close of the Dow Jones Industrial Average. The mere
act of opening the stock and bond markets was risky for Japan, because even before the U.S. terror attacks,
fears had been mounting of a plummet in share values below the psychologically sensitive 10000 mark.
But the terror-inspired jitters raise the risks that Japan's banking system -- now perhaps the biggest single
vulnerability in the world financial network -- could tip back into the sort of crisis it fell into in 1998, during the
last global financial shock. The system has held firm so far, but Japanese share prices continued sliding in
early trading today, in contrast to the rebounds in European bourses. That slide is heightening anxiety over
the banks, which hold huge portfolios of stocks. At current share-price levels, analysts said, most of Japan's
big lenders are seeing large potential losses on their portfolios; in the reporting period that ends this month,
they will have to realize some of those losses, under new, "mark to market" accounting rules.
Thus, the stock sell-off makes it doubly important for Tokyo to take drastic action to fix its banking system,
said Koyo Ozeki, a credit analyst at Merrill Lynch in Tokyo. "During this time of global crisis, the banking
problem cannot just be a domestic one."
Another worry: Bankers in Tokyo were reporting some trouble raising dollars for international transactions,
because dollar foreign-exchange trading around the world has slowed to a trickle in the wake of the attacks.
This didn't appear to cause an immediate threat, but it did mean that Japanese banks yesterday paid a high
premium for dollars.
"It's not that credit [for Japanese lenders] has gotten worse -- it's a liquidity premium," said Akio Makabe,
chief economist at Dai-Ichi Kangyo Research Institute. Mr. Makabe said the focus today will be on how much
liquidity the U.S. Federal Reserve injects into the dollar markets, and whether there will be enough New York
bankers at their desks and operating to sell those dollars to Japanese and other banks.

--- Page 192 ---
Page 192 of 243 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Speculation mounted that central banks would accelerate interest-rate cuts in the coming weeks to buoy the
economy, but Wim Duisenberg, president of the European Central Bank, played down the likelihood that the
ECB would make such a move, saying it would suggest "panic." Bank of England Governor Eddie George
also said such a concerted action was unlikely.
With all eyes already focused on the U.S. consumer as holding the key to whether the U.S. economy will fall
into recession -- dragging the world's economy down with it -- many economists predicted that the
psychological damage from Tuesday's events could knock out any vestiges of consumer activity for the rest
of the year.
"We need not discuss anymore whether the U.S. is in recession," said Norbert Walter, chief economist of
Deutsche Bank in Frankfurt. "We are."
Credit Suisse First Boston predicted the direct effects of the attacks would reduce economic growth by three
percentage points, measured on an annual basis, for this quarter and next -- effectively fitting the technical
definition of a recession.
In Asia, banks, businesses and governments recoiled as well, trying their best to maintain market calm.
Pinning their hopes on a U.S. recovery later this year to pull them out of their own troubles, many countries in
the region could now drift into recession, economists said.
The region clawed its way out of its 1997-1998 financial crisis largely by exporting technology-related goods
to the U.S. That tech-driven recovery had already slowed considerably before the attack. Countries such as
Japan, Taiwan, Singapore and Hong Kong are probably giving up on hopes for an export-led recovery before
next year.
Economists said the travel, insurance and banking industries would be the most affected, though a cooling in
activity may be felt across the globe in the coming weeks as consumers, investors and businesses absorb the
magnitude of the attack.
For the airline industry, already suffering from the high oil prices of the past year, the effect should be even
greater than the fallout from the Gulf War, analysts said. "It's clear that the key factors are far worse this time
around," said Chris Avery, an airline industry analyst with J.P. Morgan in London. "The airline recession was
already much deeper than it was back then, and the terrorism concerns are also going to be much deeper."
While the banking system welcomed the injection of capital from the central banks as staving off any
short-term liquidity problems, the question now is what the developments mean for the bottom line. Analysts
predicted Tuesday's attack could deal a further blow to earnings, which have already suffered this year from
the economic slowdown and weak stock markets.
The banks with the biggest worries may be those with insurance operations, such as Credit Suisse Group
AG, ING Group NV and Fortis NV. So far, no one knows the extent of the liabilities international insurers will
face, nor which insurers -- including which banks -- will pick up the cost.
Most Latin American financial markets partly recovered from sharp declines after the attack, but Brazil and
Argentina face fresh economic difficulties despite stabilization aid from the International Monetary Fund.
Though the economic fallout of the attack will hit the region as a whole, the two nations are particularly
vulnerable because of their dependence on foreign capital. The attack is likely to aggravate the U.S.
economic slowdown and further sap foreign direct and portfolio investment, whose weakness this year has
already slashed Latin growth prospects.
Brazil, the region's biggest economy, has seen its hopes for a second straight year of buoyant growth dashed
amid a global slowdown, a local energy crisis and fallout from neighboring Argentina's battle with a stubborn
recession and the possibility of default on its $130 billion debt. The government has shaved its prediction of
GDP growth this year to 2.2% from 4.5%. Andre Loes, chief economist for Santander Central Hispano
Investment in Sao Paulo, lowered his 2001 growth forecast to 1.5% -- just before the attack. Now, he said,
the economic impact "will be more intense, but the degree of the intensity will be known only after the U.S.
retaliates."
Yesterday, Brazil's real closed at a new historic low of 2.69 to the dollar, bucking the trend of most regional
markets that reopened after the U.S. attacks.
For Argentina, the U.S. attacks came at a moment of relative calm in efforts to stay current on its government
debt. Some $6.3 billion in loans were formally approved by the IMF's board Friday and delivered to the
Argentine government Monday. The expectation of the arrival of those funds, aimed at fortifying Argentina's
financial system, has calmed international investors.
---

--- Page 193 ---
Page 193 of 243 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
With reports by Marcus Walker in Frankfurt, Paulo Prada and Dan Bilefsky in Brussels, Greg Ip and Jon
Hilsenrath in New York, and Jason Singer and Michael Williams in Tokyo.

Asian Stocks Slide as European Markets Seem to Aim for Normalcy
World Stock Markets
Asian Stocks Slide as European Markets Seem to Aim for Normalcy
By Wall Street Journal staff reporters Silvia Ascarelli in London, and Sarah McBride and Karen Richardson in
Hong Kong
1,348 words
13 September 2001
The Wall Street Journal
J
C3
English
(Copyright (c) 2001, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Asian stocks slid amid concern about the repercussions of terrorist attacks in the U.S. on global security and
economic growth, but European financial markets seemed to be trying for a semblance of normalcy.
Those Asian stock markets that opened suffered steep losses not seen since the region-wide financial crisis
of 1997. Among the hardest hit: Korea's Kospi fell 12%, Hong Kong's Hang Seng Index lost 8.9%, and
Singapore's Straits Times Index lost 8.9%. China was one of the more resilient markets, with Shanghai's
Class B shares losing 4.2% and Shenzhen's Class B shares losing 4.3%.
In Tokyo, the Nikkei Stock Average of 225 major issues lost 6.6%, or 682.85 points, to 9610.10, its lowest
close since December 1983. The stock exchange said it would continue to keep daily trading range limits
halved for individual stocks today. It will also delay by 30 minutes, to 9:30 a.m., the opening of trade for cash
stocks, stock futures and options, and convertible bonds. Stock markets in Taiwan, Malaysia, and Thailand
were closed.
European share prices recovered much of Tuesday's sell-off, as the wholesale flight to the relative safety of
cash and bonds abated. In a day characterized by wild price swings, the Dow Jones Stoxx index of 600
blue-chip stocks rose 1.8% while the narrower Dow Jones Stoxx 50 jumped 2.4%. The FTSE-100 in London
was up 2.9%, or 136.10 points, to 4882.10 and the Paris CAC-40 closed 1.3% higher. The Frankfurt Xetra
DAX index ended the day up 1.4%. "There's minimal trading being done today," said one London broker, who
compared the somber mood to that following the death of Princess Diana in 1997. "It's partly a mark of
respect and partly not wanting to create a false market -- but more the former than the latter. When you talk to
a client, they're just not interested" in trading shares.
Trading desks across Europe fell silent at 2:45 p.m. continental European time, 24 hours after a jet first hit the
World Trade Center in New York. Euronext halted trading during that minute, and the London Stock
Exchange suspended its automatic trade-execution service. Traders at the International Petroleum Exchange
and the London Metals Exchange went silent for a minute earlier in the day.
Overall, the Dow Jones World Stock Index fell 1.14 points, or 0.68%, to 165.66.
Australian property group Westfield Holdings, part of the group that has a multibillion dollar lease for New
York's World Trade Center, fell 5.1%. However, it said the company was fully insured against loss of income
and didn't expect the attack to have a material impact on its earnings.
Even if markets in Asia follow Europe's second-day lead in reviving or falling more slowly, things could
deteriorate in this region, and quickly, analysts and investors say. "The biggest concern for investors is what
will happen to the U.S. economy, which was already in danger of falling into a recession," said Takeshi
Nakamura, deputy general manager at Daiwa Securities SB Capital Markets Co. in Osaka, Japan.
While a few gold- and oil-related shares gained ground on expectations of higher commodities prices, almost
all other Asian blue-chip shares were down sharply.
Asian insurance companies lost ground because of fears they may have to pay for some of the lost property
and life. Oil and gold shares such as Sumitomo Metal Mining in Japan and BHP Billiton in Australia rose
early on, but ended the day lower.
But China is relatively insulated from the rest of the world's economy. "The Chinese stocks, especially the
domestic-consumption plays in China shouldn't have a direct relationship to what is happening in the U.S.,"
said Billy Chan, fund manager for Invesco Asset Management Asia in Hong Kong. "This could present a
buying opportunity for an unfortunate reason." But China stocks are simply too small to absorb all the money

--- Page 195 ---
Page 195 of 243 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
flooding out of Hong Kong market heavyweights HSBC Holdings and Li & Fung, said Lisa Stewart, director of
Bowen Capital Management in Hong Kong.
Despite the turmoil in Asia, European bourses opened for business as usual after consulting with each other,
regulators and market participants. But they all suspended trading in U.S. shares and derivatives products
linked to U.S. shares. Deutsche Boerse also said it would postpone the launch of electronic trading of U.S.
shares in euros, slated for Friday.
A broker in London said interest picked up as the day wore on and investors were able to turn away from the
nonstop television coverage of the search for survivors in the rubble of the World Trade Center. Investment
decisions were being based on judgments about which industries would be most insulated from an economic
downturn, traders said, which favored pharmaceuticals and utilities.
The Dow Jones Stoxx health care index led all sectors with a 6.7% gain, sparked by an 11% jump in the
share price of Glaxo SmithKline. The battered telecommunications and technology indexes rose 6.5% and
6.1%, respectively. On the losing side were the energy index, which was Tuesday's only gainer but fell 3.9%
yesterday, and economically cyclical stocks, down 3.0% on the whole. Among the big losers were Shell
Transport & Trading, down 5.7%, and Air France, which tumbled more than 10%.
For the most part, however, market analysts at many of the biggest investment banks declined to comment
on the day's activity. Merrill Lynch & Co. opted not to publish any analyst reports on companies or markets.
ABN Amro analysts would comment on individual corporate earnings but weren't discussing the broader
economic implications of Tuesday's terrorist attacks on the U.S. Goldman Sachs considered not publishing
any research at all, but decided such reports were "integral to maintaining orderly markets," said Lucas van
Praag, a spokesman for the firm in London. However, the firm decided not to make any changes to earnings
estimates or share recommendations in order to help keep markets calm.
But it also told investors trying to judge the economic implications of the attacks that it expects a likely
"substantial adverse effect on consumer and business confidence world-wide in the near term." The recovery
that the firm's equity strategists had expected in European shares now is "unlikely" and corporate earnings in
2001 are now likely to fall by an average of 10% to 12%, rather than the 6% that had been forecast.
European government officials and regulators tried to reinforce the sense of calm. The U.K.'s Financial
Services Authority and the Bank of England, in a joint statement, said they expect London financial markets
to remain open and described trading as "orderly." European Central Bank President Wim Duisenberg said
the central bank was ready to help markets operate normally, should the need arise. The ECB will discuss
the impact of the attack on the U.S. at its meeting today.
---
Eric Bellman of Dow Jones Newswires contributed to this article.

Weak U.S. Dollar, Equities Fail to Strengthen Gold
Commodities
Weak U.S. Dollar, Equities Fail to Strengthen Gold
By David Bogoslaw
Dow Jones Newswires
580 words
11 September 2001
The Wall Street Journal
J
C15
English
(Copyright (c) 2001, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
NEW YORK -- Consider it a backhanded compliment that some traders are disappointed by gold's failure to
capitalize on U.S. dollar and equities weakness.
The December gold contract on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange fell another $1.40
to close at $273.70 a troy ounce. The contract is down more than $10 from its mid-August high.
"It was commission houses, funds, and Europeans selling gold lower," said a trader at an international bank
in New York. "People are pretty disappointed with the lack of follow-through [buying], with all the help we got
from the euro and stocks last week, so they're getting out."
The Bank of England's scheduled auction of 20 metric tons of gold tomorrow is expected to restrict gold to a
trading range of between $270 and $275 a troy ounce.
U.S. equity markets have been under near-constant pressure since the Dow Jones Industrial Average broke
below the 10,000 mark the week before the Labor Day weekend. A deluge of bearish U.S. economic data
during the past week accelerated the sell-off. When the Labor Department released data showing the August
unemployment rate had climbed to 4.9% from 4.5% for July, the Dow Jones Industrial Average dumped
another 250 points.
Gold, which has spent much of the summer tracking the euro against the dollar in the absence of any
fundamental influence, made a feeble attempt during the past week to get through resistance just above $276
an ounce but succumbed to profit-taking and sank back to $275.
Dollar-denominated gold becomes more affordable to foreign consumers when their local currencies rise in
value relative to the greenback.
"I would have expected gold to react strongly to the lower interest rates, which are bullish for gold, the euro
[coming] back up . . . and the stock market [going] down," said George Gero, senior vice president at
Prudential Securities in New York. He said he believed all those factors had been countered by the auction
on Wednesday, which traditionally attracts selling prior to the event.
But an analyst at a New York investment house dismissed the notion that gold is regarded as a safe haven
for investors seeking refuge from unpredictable equity markets. He cited the argument that gold does better
from a fundamental standpoint when the economy is doing well and discretionary income is more available
for items such as jewelry.
In other commodity markets:
CRUDE: Crude oil futures posted moderate losses at Nymex as traders booked profits on the past week's
gains. Front-month October crude oil futures, which rose more than $1 a barrel Thursday and Friday, fell 40
cents to $27.63 a barrel. The weakness came amid moderate trading volumes, leading some analysts to
predict a resumption of the past week's uptrend.
PORK BELLIES: Sparked by continued strong bacon demand and thin stocks, nearby February belly futures
climbed 1.52 cents at the Chicago Mercantile Exchange to 84.17 cents a pound. Though the prime summer
bacon season is over, demand is robust even as stocks of bellies, the part of the pig used for bacon
production, fall to historically low levels. The most recent monthly Cold Storage report from the U.S.
Agriculture Departmentshowed frozen stored supplies at just half the previous year's level.

Threaten to JumpFrom the Golden Gate;You'll Get Tough Love---Ironworker Volunteers Will GoOver the Rail to Save You,But Don't Expect Empath...
Threaten to Jump From the Golden Gate; You'll Get Tough Love --- Ironworker Volunteers Will Go
Over the Rail to Save You, But Don't Expect Empathy
By Molly Williams
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
1,259 words
10 September 2001
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
(Copyright (c) 2001, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
SAN FRANCISCO -- Before dawn on a cold, foggy March morning in 1993, a 26-year-old man wearing a
trench coat over his business suit and brandishing a kitchen knife climbed over the railing of the Golden Gate
Bridge and onto a narrow catwalk.
From there he peered into near-certain death in the foaming Pacific Ocean 220 feet below. As he considered
his next step, police and rescue vehicles blocked several lanes on the bridge, backing up the morning
commute for miles into Marin County.
Ironworker Glen Sievert was snug in his bed, sound asleep, when he got the call. Soon, police were clearing
a path for him through the throng of rescue workers clustered on the bridge.
The face-off lasted more than five hours, until Mr. Sievert issued the man an ultimatum: Give up in 30
seconds or the burly Mr. Sievert would come after him. The man snapped off a quick salute -- and jumped.
"He called my bluff," Mr. Sievert says. But Mr. Sievert wasn't quite finished with him.
This is as tough as tough love gets. Between San Francisco and Marin County, the Golden Gate Bridge may
be crossed by more psychotherapists every day than any other bridge in America. Yet try committing suicide
from the reddish-orange span and the guy who rushes out to talk you down -- in coveralls and steel-toed
boots -- is sure to know more about metal fatigue than depression. The suicide squad at one of the nation's
most popular spots to kill oneself is manned entirely by ironworkers.
"They may be looked at as unconventional," says bridge manager Kary Witt, "but they're pretty darn
successful."
Golden Gate Bridge officials don't like to talk publicly about suicide and don't keep public records of how
many people have died. Officials stopped counting deaths in 1995, shortly before the 1,000th person jumped.
But the bridge, with its towering vistas and wide-open sidewalks, remains a powerful magnet to despondent
and deranged people.
The bridge is managed by the Golden Gate Bridge Highway and Transportation District, which is made up of
a board appointed by county governments; the bridge was built by counties, not by the state. The bridge
district is free to handle the suicide issue as it wishes and has stuck with its volunteer team of about a dozen
ironworkers to respond to calls.
It long has resisted erecting a physical barrier to suicides, most recently in 1998, on the grounds that it would
mar the appearance of the bridge, might cost too much money and might not even work. Mr. Witt, the bridge
manager, says, "We've been quite effective with methods other than a barrier. There would be a broad public
dissent to the aesthetics of a barrier."
The ironworkers claim they have saved more lives than have been lost. The San Francisco Suicide
Prevention hotline and crisis center estimates that about 30 people die jumping off the bridge each year and
that the ironworkers save about 60 others.
They're the only ones close to the scene, bridge officials say. They wear safety harnesses and know the
bridge's underbelly cold. And they're willing to go places that no one else will.
"Nobody other than us wants to go over the rail," says ironworker Terry Roberson, a 16-year bridge veteran.
The California Highway Patrol and the San Francisco Police Department respond to calls but won't climb over
the railing. Once, when a professional counselor did come to the bridge to coach the ironworkers on suicide

--- Page 199 ---
Page 199 of 243 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
prevention, Mr. Roberson says, a man went over the railing, to his death, that very same day. The counselor,
summoned to the scene, "got so freaked out he never came back," Mr. Roberson says.
The ironworkers have been assisting with rescues almost since the bridge was opened in 1937. They sign up
for the duty and either respond to radio requests for help while they are working, or respond to calls they get
from the bridge sergeant in the middle of the night.
Negotiating tactics have been passed down from one generation of apprentice ironworkers to the next.
Empathy is not high on the list. Some of the bridge men regard suicidal people with contempt -- as workplace
hazards who tie up traffic, distract them from their jobs and give the bridge a bad name.
"I don't like these people. I have my own problems," says Mr. Sievert, 53, who has worked on the bridge for
26 years and sports a gold ring with the insignia of the bridge on it. "My job is to get them off the bridge.
Beyond that, it's up to someone else."
The ironworkers pitch themselves as regular folks who know how tough life can be. If the person threatening
to jump doesn't respond to casual conversation, their goal is to buy time, establish some rapport, then inch
close enough to nab them. To accomplish that, they have devised some unconventional techniques.
One approach is complete nonchalance -- asking, say, for the person's watch or wallet, "since you're about to
die anyway." Another is to instill fear -- describing, in gruesome detail, how painful it will be to hit the water at
more than 100 miles an hour: "It's like hitting a brick wall. What if you survive?"
The San Francisco Police Department wants to replace the ironworkers in talking down suicides with its own
trained hostage-negotiators, who sometimes help the ironworkers. Eve Meyer, director of Suicide Prevention
in San Francisco, which operates a counseling center and hotline, calls the ironworkers' methods "irregular."
Still, she says, "you can't argue with success."
People routinely kick, scratch and scream as the bridge workers haul them back to safety, says Ken Hopper,
45, a weather-beaten ironworker from Arkansas. Once, a woman left a circle of teeth marks in Mr. Sievert's
shoulder as he and Mr. Hopper reeled her in. Bridge workers have had to contend with knives and guns, too.
Mr. Roberson saw a father throw his toddler off the bridge, then jump himself. Mr. Hopper talked down a
young college student only to have her return the next morning and leap to her death. Not long after that, he
took a break from rescue work for a few years.
"I used to stuff all those feelings in a bag, and eventually the bag got so big it burst," says Mr. Hopper, who
has worked on the bridge for 17 years. "It's pretty devastating."
When the suicidal young man in the trench coat climbed over the railing in 1993, he tied a nylon rope around
his neck and fastened the other end to the bridge. Waving a serrated knife, he kept Mr. Sievert and others at
bay for hours, never saying why he wanted to die. The ironworker, fed up and exhausted, finally made a
move to untie the rope from the bridge. The man put down his knife to try to stop him, and Mr. Sievert
grabbed the knife. Then came the 30-second ultimatum -- and the leap.
Mr. Sievert vaulted over the railing after him, landing on a beam. He and several other workers hoisted the
man up by the rope he had tied around his neck and laid him out, unconscious but alive.

ASIA/PACIFIC
AngloGold Bids
For Australian Firm
Normandy Mining
South Africa's AngloGold, the world's largest gold producer, launched a surprise stock offer for Normandy
Mining, valuing Australia's biggest gold miner at 3.2 billion Australian dollars (US$1.66 billion). Normandy
described the proposal as "logical" and said it recognized the need for further consolidation in the sector. But
its chief executive, Robert Champion de Crespigny, who briefed reporters in Melbourne with AngloGold
Executive Director Kelvin Williams, said the offer wasn't an agreed bid. AngloGold plans to offer 2.15 of its
shares for every 100 Normandy shares, valuing the Australian miner at A$1.42 a share, a 29% premium to its
closing price Tuesday; Normandy shares rose 22% yesterday to A$1.34. AngloGold shares fell 6.4% in
Johannesburg trading.
"The combination of AngloGold and Normandy will create the world's first truly global gold company,"
AngloGold Chairman Bobby Godsell said. Normandy didn't comment on the offer terms.
Key Australian Rate Cut by Quarter Point
Australia's central bank took out insurance against a slowing global economy, cutting the overnight cash rate
by a quarter point to 4.75% despite buoyant domestic economic activity. In a controversial move, the Reserve
Bank of Australia followed a global trend toward lower rates but also warned of a house-price boom that could
sow the seeds for a possible rise in rates next year, economists said. The global economy's course in the
next month, along with the U.S. Federal Reserve's policy deliberations, will determine whether the Reserve
Bank will cut rates again, they said. But most economists expect the U.S. and world economy to bottom out.
Since February, the central bank has cut the overnight cash rate 1.5 percentage points to its lowest level
since December 1998, when it was also 4.75%.
Windows XP Complaint Filed in Korea
South Korea's Daum Communications, the country's largest Internet portal, said it filed a complaint against
Microsoft over the U.S. company's planned release of its new Windows XP operating system. In the
complaint, filed with Korea's Fair Trade Commission, Daum said Microsoft's bundling of a variety of
application softwarewith Windows XP constitutes an unfair business practice. Windows XP is scheduled for
widespread retail release on Oct. 25. A Microsoft Korea spokesman said it needed to study "the complaint in
detail before issuing any official comment." But he said that if the commission "wants to make enquiries to us
about it, we are willing to respond to it." The commission said it would soon launch an investigation but wasn't
"sure how long" it would take.
Ericsson Plans to Double China Outlays
Swedish telecommunications-equipment maker Telefon L.M. Ericsson said it plans to increase its direct and
indirect investment in China to $5.1 billion by 2005 from $2.4 billion currently. It also said it will bring more
local Chinese companies into its global supply chain. Ericsson has nine joint ventures in China and is one of
the country's largest suppliers of telecom-network equipment and mobile phones.
BRIEFLY:

--- Page 201 ---
Page 201 of 243 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
-- South Korea's KorAm Bank said it will sell bad loans with a face value of 347.3 billion won ($271 million) to
a consortium, led by U.S. investment fund Cerberus Capital Management, that was selected following an
auction. The loans will be sold at a 33% recovery rate. The sale is a part of the bank's effort to reduce its bad
loans by 700 billion won in the year's second half.
-- China will permit more operators to enter the mobile-phone market when it migrates to third-generation
technology, the Ministry of Information Industry's deputy director, Han Xia, told a Beijing technology summit.
China would definitely bring in "new operators" with the introduction of third-generation services to allow the
industry to develop, he said.
-- Chinese Premier Zhu Rongji said he "definitely" won't seek a second five-year term because he is "too old."
The 72-year-old Mr. Zhu, on a Brussels visit with European Union leaders, told reporters that he will step
down when his term ends in 2003.
---
EUROPE
Britain's Logica Bucks Technology Slump
Bucking the recent downturn in the technology sector, Britain's Logica posted better-than-expected fiscal-year
results and predicted a "strong financial performance" in the current fiscal year. The British
information-technology services company said operating profit for the year ended June 30 rose 47% to pound
sterling 120.7 million ($174.2 million), above a Credit Suisse First Boston estimate of pound sterling 113.1
million, as sales jumped 33% to pound sterling 1.13 billion. Logica said it is making money off the economic
downturn, as customers ask it to install computer systems, such as inventory-management software, that
help to cut costs.
France Telecom to Increase TPSA Stake
France Telecom agreed to buy an additional 12.5% interest in Telekomunikacja Polska SA, or TPSA, for 3.5
billion zlotys ($821 million) as part of a last-minute privatization effort by Poland's government. France
Telecom and its local partner, Kulczyk Holding, agreed to pay 20 zlotys a share for the stake in state
telephone company TPSA. The investors also hold an option to buy an additional 2.5% stake, plus one share,
in TPSA by the end of 2002, which would give them control. The per-share price of the 12.5% stake is about
half what the firms pledged to pay when they bought 35% of TPSA last year.
Russia's Lukoil Posts First-Period Profit
Lukoil Holdings, Russia's largest oil producer, said it posted first-quarter profit of $690 million, its first
quarterly results compiled under U.S. generally accepted accounting principles. The company didn't provide
year-earlier figures under GAAP, but analysts said the results were in line with estimates. Lukoil posted profit
of $596 million in the first quarter of 2000 under Russian accounting standards. Revenue according to GAAP
totaled $3.56 billion, compared with $2.77 billion under Russian accounting standards a year earlier.
BRIEFLY:
-- KarstadtQuelle, Germany's largest department-store operator, has agreed "in principle" to form a joint
venture to open coffee houses in Germany with Starbucks of the U.S., its chairman said. He expects the deal
to be completed in a few weeks.
-- The number of unemployed Germans unexpectedly fell in August, the first decline in seven months.
German unemployment figures showed the number of jobless in the eastern states fell by 7,000, while rising
by 5,000 in the western states.
-- Dutch food retailer Ahold, a day after announcing the acquisitions of U.S. companies Alliant Exchange and
Bruno's Supermarkets, was asking investors yesterday for the cash to finance them. Demand for the share
offering, which sought to raise about $2 billion, easily exceeded supply, people familiar with the sale said.
-- BNP-Paribas, France's largest bank, said second-quarter profit fell 7.2% from a year earlier to 1.16 billion
euros ($1.03 billion), with the ill effects of a slowing world economy biting less than expected. Operating profit
rose 4.5% to 1.38 billion euros.
-- Belgium's Interbrew said first-half profit rose 64% from a year earlier to 200 million euros ($177.7 million),
as revenue jumped 69% to 3.48 billion euros, boosted by acquisitions. It expects earnings before interest,
taxes, depreciation and amortization, or Ebitda, to increase 8% this year.

THE AMERICAS
Pastrana to Extend Colombia Safe Haven
Colombian President Andres Pastrana plans to continue offering a controversial safe haven to rebel forces
for at least six more months in order to pursue peace negotiations in the zone, Colombia's interior minister
said. "The alternative is all-out war," Interior Minister Armando Estrada told the country's Congress late
Tuesday. The safe haven, which has been extended several times since its creation in 1998 to jump-start
peace talks, was slated to expire in October. But public sentiment in Colombia has been turning against the
Switzerland-size sanctuary for the leftist Revolutionary Armed Forces of Colombia.
Cairns Group Upbeat on Trade Talks
The Cairns Group wrapped up its three-day ministerial meeting in Punta del Este, Uruguay, more confident
than before that new global trade talks will feature agricultural reform on the agenda. Mark Vaile, Australia's
minister for trade and president of the Cairns Group, said he perceived "a much more significant level of
commitment from the major players. I sense a much stronger commitment to achieve a positive outcome." Mr.
Vaile, while noting the U.S.'s commitment to placing agriculture reform on the global agenda, pointed out that
the elimination of nearly $1 billion a day in world-wide agriculture subsidies will be very challenging. A U.S.
delegation led by Agriculture Secretary Ann Veneman and U.S. Trade Representative Robert Zoellick
participated in talks with the Cairns Group of 17 agricultural exporting nations.
BRIEFLY:
-- TransCanada PipeLines and National Fuel Gas of the U.S. agreed to pursue commercial development of a
215-mile natural-gas pipeline from Ontario to Pennsylvania. Preliminary cost of the pipeline is estimated at
$350 million to $400 million, with service slated to begin in late 2004.
-- The Venezuelan bolivar weakened after President Hugo Chavez said late Tuesday he has the option of
declaring a "state of emergency" to implement measures to stem demand for dollars. The bolivar ended
Venezuelan trading at a midrate of 741.25 to the dollar, weaker than Tuesday's 738.50 close, the Central
Bank said.

Gold Falls on Rising Confidence in U.S. Economy
Commodities
Gold Falls on Rising Confidence in U.S. Economy
By David Bogoslaw
Dow Jones Newswires
387 words
4 September 2001
The Wall Street Journal
J
C11
English
(Copyright (c) 2001, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
NEW YORK -- Gold prices fell Friday at the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange and gave
back their rally from the previous session as the dollar surged after the release of encouraging
business-activity figures from Chicago.
December-contract gold on Comex dropped $1 to $276.50 a troy ounce.
Commodity markets in the U.S. were closed yesterday for Labor Day, and reopen today.
A bounce in the Purchasing Management Association of Chicago's August index to 43.5 from 38 in July,
better than the 39.8 reading which had been forecast, bolstered confidence in the U.S. economy and the
dollar with it, causing most foreign currencies to lose ground.
The data inspired gains in U.S. stocks and spurred the Dow Jones Industrial Average to crest briefly above
10000; it was below that level on Thursday.
"It was the inverse of yesterday [Thursday] to a lesser degree. You had the firming stock market and gold
came off," explained Jim Pogoda, a trader with Mitsubishi International Corp. in New York.
Gold historically has been favored by investors when financial markets and the dollar are weakening. In
recent years, such buying hasn't been as reliable an occurrence as before. Still, a weaker dollar both makes
gold cheaper to buyers and raises the risk of domestic inflation, enhancing the appeal of gold as an
inflation-proof investment.
In other commodity markets Friday:
HOGS: Prices made sharp gains at the Chicago Mercantile Exchange, on pre-weekend short covering -- the
buying of futures to cancel previous sales -- and on strength in the pork-belly market. The October contract
rose 1.42 cents to 58.25 cents a pound. The rise came despite a drop in cash prices for hogs. "Nothing has
changed fundamentally," a CME hog trader said. "Cash is still unraveling. But people had already sold so
much that they couldn't afford to sell any more, and went running for cover. It was freaky Friday here."
SOYBEANS: Prices rose at the Chicago Board of Trade and were fueled by a rally in soybean meal futures
and on strong commodity-fund short covering, according to traders. The November soybean contract added
five cents to $4.86 a bushel.

States Brace for Increases In Medicaid Spending
Rising pharmaceutical prices and other costs will continue to frustrate state efforts to rein in Medicaid
spending.
Expenditures for the federal-state program for the poor, disabled and children are expected to increase by
11.1% in fiscal 2002, compared with 7.4% for overall state spending, according to preliminary numbers from a
survey of Medicaid directors by the Kaiser Commission on Medicaid and the Uninsured, a Washington, D.C.,
research center. The survey also predicts Medicaid spending will grow faster than in fiscal 2001, which ended
June 30, despite aggressive cost-cutting efforts such as asking drug manufacturers for rebates. Last fiscal
year saw a 9.9% increase in Medicaid costs, according to the survey. Though other surveys suggest
Medicaid-spending growth may moderate a bit, they all agree it will outstrip increases in total state spending.
Nearly every state -- 48 out of 50 -- cited rising pharmaceutical costs as one of the top two or three factors
driving Medicaid cost increases. "There is no question that pharmacy growth is in the middle of everybody's
radar screen," says Vernon Smith, a principal with Health Management Associates, a Lansing, Mich.,
consulting firm that implemented the survey for the Kaiser Commission.
The second-most-cited cause, mentioned by 31 states, was increased reimbursement rates for nursing
homes, doctors, dentists and other providers. The next two reasons cited by state officials were increased
enrollment (27 states) and the increased cost of long-term care (24 states).
Already, 20 states said they expected to need to appropriate supplemental budget funds or to dip into a
Medicaid trust fund they use for emergencies by the end of the current fiscal year to meet the rising costs. In
fiscal 2001, 36 states needed supplemental funding, according to Mr. Smith.
-- Russell Gold
Pain in the Plains
With the notable exception of agriculture-related products, most manufacturers in the Great Plains still
anticipate further job cutbacks despite slashed production and lowered inventories.
The Federal Reserve Bank of Kansas City, Mo., says plant managers' expectations of future production and
orders both hit new lows in the survey's seven-year history. Participants in the survey range from Kansas
grain processors to semiconductor-chip plants in Colorado.
"The firms have finally come to the realization that things might be slow for a while," says Chad Wilkerson,
associate economist with the Kansas City Fed, whose seven-state region stretches from western Missouri to
the Rocky Mountains. Expectations of future production dropped significantly since the last survey three
months ago, although more manufacturers still anticipate an increase than a decrease by year end.
A ray of optimism comes from makers of farm equipment such as Harsh International Inc. in Easton, Colo. Its
manufacturing line for cattle-feeding machinery, which accounts for 60% of its production, is humming along.
Company President Robert E. Brown expects overall production to stay flat because of sluggish orders for
truck hydraulics, which make up the rest of the company's business. But "in 12 months," Mr. Brown adds, "we
would like to think we'll see a bit of an upswing."
-- Russell Gold
Rising Lone Star
Texas, which added 3.9 million people in the '90s to vault ahead of New York as the nation's
second-most-populous state, doesn't have to worry about being surpassed anytime soon. The Dallas Federal

--- Page 205 ---
Page 205 of 243 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Reserve Bank reports there "is virtually no chance" any state will pass Texas' population in this century. But
No. 1 California had better watch its back. The Dallas Fed said Texas could pass California in population by
2065.
But that population growth is spread unevenly, so Texas is in no danger of shedding its cowboy image. Of
Texas' 254 counties, 61 have six or fewer people per square mile, the 19th-century federal definition of the
frontier, reports the bank.
-- Russell Gold
Odds and Ends
Oregon Gov. John Kitzhaber last week vetoed legislation that would have cut the state's capital-gains tax to
4% from 9% by 2005, and cost the state $255 million a year in revenue when fully implemented. An override
is unlikely. It was the second time in as many sessions that the Democratic governor vetoed a big
capital-gains tax cut passed by the Republican Legislature. . . . The University of Washington is the latest to
blame the energy crunch for making the cost of higher education higher. Regents last week approved an
energy surcharge that will cost a full-time student about $135 a year. At least one student is challenging the
legality of the surcharge in court, with a hearing on a motion to block the fee scheduled for this week. State
universities in Oregon, Louisiana and Maine also have added energy charges to tuition bills over the past
several weeks.

World Watch

World Watch
Compiled by Farran Nehme
1,596 words
21 August 2001
The Wall Street Journal
J
A12
English
(Copyright (c) 2001, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
EUROPE
EU Eases Stance
On Trade Sanctions
After WTO Victory
The European Union sounded a conciliatory note in a dispute over U.S. tax breaks for exporters that the
World Trade Organization has ruled amount to a subsidy program. The previously reported WTO ruling,
published Monday, sided with the EU in a dispute over a law that allowed U.S. companies to set up foreign
sales corporations to shield some overseas profits from taxes. Although the U.S. amended the law last year
to try to meet the EU's objections, the WTO said the system still breaches rules outlawing export subsidies.
EU officials welcomed the ruling, calling for the U.S. to make "wholesale changes" to its tax laws. But they
also sounded a conciliatory note on sanctions. Although the EU filed for a total of $4 billion in trade sanctions
at an earlier stage of the case, an EU trade official said Brussels might choose to impose a smaller amount or
to delay all sanctions, provided it saw evidence the U.S. is trying to comply with WTO rules. EU officials also
said they will wait until all appeals are exhausted before imposing any sanctions. The U.S. has until Oct. 19 to
appeal the ruling.
U.S. Trade Representative Robert Zoellick said the Bush administration is talking to U.S. businesses, the EU
and Congress about how to resolve the dispute. "Our goal is to promote U.S. economic interests while at the
same time fulfilling our WTO obligations, and we believe we will be able to accomplish both," he said.
Euro-Zone Industrial Output Rose in June
Euro-zone industrial production showed unexpected strength in June, data showed, surprising analysts who
expected the 12-nation region to mirror the weakness in U.S. and Japanese industry. But while the 0.6%
monthly rise in euro-zone output reinforced positive signals from France last week, it didn't damp
expectations that the European Central Bank would cut interest rates, possibly before the end of August.
The data from the European Union's statistics agency showed euro-zone output in June posted a monthly
increase for only the second time this year, and rose 1.6% from a year earlier. Output of durable consumer
goods led the monthly gain, and production of capital goods helped the annual increase.
U.K.'s June Trade Deficit Hits Record
The United Kingdom's global trade deficit in June reached a record GBP 2.3 billion ($3.33 billion), according
to official figures. The main reason for the widening gap was higher imports of "erratic items," especially
aircraft, mainly from the U.S., and precious stones, a spokesman for the National Statistics agency said.
Excluding the items, export levels were little changed in June, while imports fell 1%.
Swatch Group Profit Falls 23%
Swiss watch producer Swatch Group said profit for the first half of 2001 fell 23% to 231 million Swiss francs
($139.5 million) from 300 million francs a year earlier. Last year's first-half profit included extraordinary
income of 44 million francs from a convertible bond, Swatch said. Excluding the one-time item, profit for the
first half of 2001 fell 9.8%. Sales rose 2.4% to 1.96 billion francs. The company lowered its full-year revenue
growth estimate to 5% to 8%, down from 10% to 12%, citing the slowing economy. Swatch said sales growth
will depend on Christmas-season sales.

-- British and French aviation regulators probably will simultaneously restore airworthiness certificates for the

ASIA/PACIFIC
Zhu Says Chinese Economy Needs Boost
Premier Zhu Rongji said China's economy, though still expanding rapidly, is in need of a boost to make up for
faltering export growth as the world economy slows, state media reported. "We should expand demand
through investment and consumption and try by every means to boost exports," Mr. Zhu said during an
inspection tour of a province in southwestern China. For the first half, China's economy grew 7.9% from a
year earlier, but analysts expect growth to slow during the second half as weaker exports take a toll. During
an annual summer retreat, Chinese leaders set the course for policies to expand domestic demand to help
offset slowing export growth, participants said. The policies include a salary boost for civil servants to help lift
consumption as well as easier credit for home and car buyers, analysts said; the government also probably
will speed up payment of tax rebates to domestic exporters.
IMF Opens Talks With Indonesia
Top officials from the International Monetary Fund opened talks with Indonesia's new government, which is
optimistic that a vital $5 billion loan program, suspended in December due to slow progress on reforms, can
be revived. President Megawati Sukarnoputri has given priority to fixing ties with international creditors,
especially the IMF, as part of an effort to stabilize the country. Indonesian State Enterprises Minister
Laksamana Sukardi said he expected agreement on a pact for reforms, outlined in a letter of intent, within
days.
BHP Billiton Profit Increases 1.5%
BHP Billiton, one of the world's largest mining conglomerates, reported a solid profit for the year ended June
30 of $1.53 billion, up 1.5% from year-earlier pro forma profit. The result was at the low end of market
expectations for the newly merged Australian-British company, after accounting for a $148 million write-down
on BHP's 52%-owned Ok Tedi copper and gold mine in Papua New Guinea. Excluding that loss and other
one-time charges stemming from merger costs and asset write-downs, profit rose 26% to $2.19 billion. BHP
Billiton said the global economic slowdown will hurt earnings. Total sales rose 3.7% to $19.08 billion. Shares
of BHP fell 0.4% to GBP 3.17 ($4.58) on the London stock exchange.
Cathay's Labor Dispute Delays Decision
Cathay Pacific Airways' standoff with its pilots' union is delaying a decision on possibly selling and then
leasing back some or all of the 20 Airbus A330-300 jets the Hong Kong airline will have by year end.
Sale-leaseback deals bring cash to an airline but can add to long-term operating costs. Cathay said it must
delay a decision until the dispute is resolved, according to a notice it sent to jet-leasing companies. The
union's work slowdown, which began July 3, cost the airline 350 million Hong Kong dollars (US$44.9 million)
in July.

laundering profits from drug trafficking. But Chile's Supreme Court said evidence was lacking that the four
detained members of Aero Continente's senior management were involved in money laundering. A probe
continues.

Thinking Things Over:Money Puzzle: Easy or Tight?
Thinking Things Over: Money Puzzle: Easy or Tight?
By Robert L. Bartley
1,124 words
20 August 2001
The Wall Street Journal
J
A15
English
(Copyright (c) 2001, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
I've known Alan Greenspan so long I met Ayn Rand at a going-away party the first time he joined government
back in the Ford administration. From long lunches in an aerie above Wall Street, I can testify he has one of
the most complex minds I've ever encountered.
Even before he joined the central bankers' club, it was hard but fascinating to follow the twists and turns of his
analysis. But somehow he always managed to tie the strands together into a sensible whole, coming out in
what seemed to me the right place. So with Alan as Federal Reserve chairman, I've tended to swallow my
doubts about a discretionary monetary policy, guided by the intuition of one man rather than by explicit and
consistent rules and guideposts.
And indeed, Mr. Greenspan's guidance has served the republic well, with a long run of prosperity and
increasingly stable prices. Still, the Federal Open Market Committee gathers tomorrow in an atmosphere of
unease. Perhaps it's only the lag between monetary policy changes and their economic effect, but then
again, perhaps something has gone awry.
The Fed has been energetically cutting its target interest rate since January, but with no evident effect.
Long-term interest rates have scarcely budged. The economy has continued to stall, with hopes for a
second-half recovery vanishing. Through most of the period gold and other commodity prices remained
moribund and the dollar went from strength to strength in the foreign-exchange markets. The dollar slippage
in the last few weeks may be the first sign that the easing is having some effect, but even this remains
tenuous.
All of which raises again the ancient issue of goals and targets for monetary policy. In particular, are
changes in the traditional Fed funds rate really "easing" or "tightening"? Have the recent cuts in the rate really
been a pro-active policy of stimulus? Or have they merely been reactions to what the market has
independently been doing to interest rates, as Arthur Laffer argued here March 22?
In recent years, the strongest challenge to interest-rate targeting has come from former Fed Governor Wayne
Angell, a "price-rule" proponent. At the Fed, he devised an index of 10 sensitive prices, now also available
through Bear Stearns and Co., where he is chief economist. The index stood at 129 with the last boost in the
Fed funds rate in May 2000, and at 105 with the first cut in the rate in January 2001. The steep decline
suggests tight money was overdone.
Mr. Angell also argues that the Fed is too tight even today, indeed that there is a risk of deflation. The M-2
money supply has grown, but he argues this is merely hoarding of money in interest-bearing accounts during
a weak stock market. If the Fed were ahead of the markets in cutting interest rates, sensitive prices would
be rising. Mr. Angell's core index is up only modestly, to 113, while the price of gold has been remarkably
steady over the year. He argues that a quarter-point cut in the target tomorrow is already discounted, and that
to get ahead of the market the Fed needs to cut a half point.
The warning of deflation is a serious matter, as the example of Japan testifies. Falling prices create incentives
to hoard rather than consume, and it's not easy for a central bank to stop a self-feeding deflationary spiral.
I've resisted the deflation warning because for most of the year price indexes have been rising, albeit partly
because of energy prices artificially boosted by the OPEC cartel. But now both producer and consumer prices
have posted outright declines, supporting the case that the Fed has not been easy at all.
A deflationary impulse has been particularly evident in the price of imports; nonpetroleum import prices fell
1% in July and stand 2.2% below a year earlier. Including petroleum, prices declined 1.6% in July, the
sharpest drop on record, and stand down 4.2% year-over-year. This of course reflects the strong dollar,
another indicator of monetary tightness.

--- Page 212 ---
Page 212 of 243 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Nobel laureate Robert Mundell, dean of international economists and my own long-time guru, also believes
the Fed has been tight rather than easy, as reflected in falling commodity prices, a low gold price, a high
dollar and mild deflation in countries fixed to the dollar (troubled Argentina, for example). "The current
interest-rate declines have not done more than keep pace with the market. Following the market down does
not make money easy, it just prevents it from getting tighter."
Mr. Mundell also warns of volatile exchange rates just ahead as euro banknotes replace national currencies
early next year. He anticipates a substantial conversion of German marks into dollars to avoid recording
foreign DM holdings in the official exchange into euros. "The dollar could shoot up toward the end of the year
and the euro plummet."
The Mundell solution is that the Fed and the European Central Bank should agree to a floor for the euro at
85 cents and a ceiling at $1.15. The banks would buy euros if they fell to 85 cents, would also support this
floor in the futures market, and most importantly, would not "sterilize," or offset currency purchases by sale of
domestic bonds. No speculator could challenge this kind of coordinated intervention, dictating monetary
policy itself.
The U.S. and the Europeans did set a floor and ceiling in the Plaza and Louvre accords during the 1980s. But
in 1987 the agreements fell apart, precipitating the stock market crash that was Chairman Greenspan's first
challenge; the break-up issue was whether the strong-currency country or the weak-currency country bore
the responsibility to intervene. Mr. Mundell proposes that this should depend on whether the world needs
more liquidity or less. In the current environment, the Fed should buy euros to protect the floor, combat
deflation and build a reserve of euros.
Whether any such formal agreement is within reach, the recent strengthening of the euro and easing of the
dollar should be welcomed -- not as a boost to U.S. exports but as a force for price stability. Almost by
definition, import prices are marginal prices in many sectors of the economy, and if they're volatile general
price stability will be elusive.
Perhaps, too, the lower dollar is the first sign that Chairman Greenspan is one step ahead of the deflationary
demon. I have the feeling of being in the midst of another of those tortuous conversations, waiting for Alan to
pull everything together one more time.
